MEDICAL CERTIFICATE.
“ Ararat (Victoria), 25th Aug., 1902.
“ During the past few years Eczema has become of very
frequent occurrence, especially in children. In the treat-
ment of this troublesome affection the usual medicinal
remedies failed in many cases to give relief, and I was very
pleased to have a new preparation, named ‘ Oleac,’ put into
my hands for trial. I can recall to my mind three cases of
children in which the usual remedies had no beneficial
effect, and the application of ‘Oleac’ according to the
printed directions removed all the irritation of the skin.
I have also heard of many adults using the ‘ Oleac ’ with
great benefit.— JAMES SMEAL, Surgeon, &c., Ararat ”

THE BED PAGE.
Trial by Newspaper.
THAT the flagrant interference on the part
of newspapers in the administration of jus-
tice is allowed in Australia can only be ex-
plained when it is understood with what
curious persistence the tradition of the “patriotic
motives of the press” survives the passing away of
all the justification it ever had. In the early
days of the struggle of human Liberty “the
press ” was the refuge of the reformer, and
on printed sheets were circulated the protests of
the people’s champions against tyranny. From
these pamphlets of reform sprang newspapers,
which have since gradually developed to
their present state of absolutely com-
mercial enterprises, selling news or opinions,
facts or lies, comments more or less just,
reports, generally of the evil, occasionally of
the good in life, but always holding steadily
in view the aim of other merchants —to sell at a
profit.
* * * *
The present-day organisation of a newspaper
allows no more room for patriotism than the
business of selling drapery ; for principle than the
occupation of selling groceries. A newspaper
proprietor is often patriotic, often of high
principle, and his newspaper will then reflect the
opinions he holds ; just as when any other sort of
business man is conspicuously high-minded, he
proves it in the conduct of his affairs. But as a
business, and it is purely a business, newspaper-
running has no more to do with “elevating the
people,” “inculcating a high moral tone,” “edu-
cating and enlightening the ignorant ” than has
selling tea or land, or sensational novels. As
often as not, a newspaper is owned by a joiiit-stock
company, made up of persons of vastly different
social, political and religious views, and necessarily
aliuciys run in direct conflict with some of the most
cherished principles of some of its proprietors;
but so long as good dividends are paid all are
satisfied. Nor would there be reason for anyone
to question this state of things but for the fact
that most of these newspapers, on behalf of their
proprietors, claim to be in the business, not for the
sake of the pennies it will bring, but from some
lofty motives of patriotism.
* • * •
Such cant is self-evidently false if looked
into, and is to the last degree nauseating.
Nevertheless it pays because it is believed ; and is
persisted in because it pays. Why it is believed
is an inscrutable problem of human folly. It
would seem to reasonable minds impossible
to believe in the lofty motives, etc., of a
strenuous freetrade newspaper which is owned
by 10,0 gentlemen,- .00 of whom believe that
Freetrade is a curse to the country, and 40 of
whom believe that it is the only means of salva-
tion, and all of whom are amicably united in draw-
ing dividends from the concern. A very moderate
amount of intelligent thought applied to the
subject would surely convince every reasoning
man that the average newspaper proprietary is in
business for what it can make, and for no other
motive whatsoever, and has often precious little
scruple as to the means of making. Yet the com-
munity is pretty generally gulled, from the ribbon-
seller at one end of the intellectual scale to the
Supreme Court Judge at the other. Legislatures
are ready to give special privileges for the free
carriage of the newspaper-merchant’s wares, to
exempt his raw material from duty and his em-
ployees from anti-sweating regulations, to give him
a license to abuse and slander which the ordinary
citizen has not, and finally to allow him, in the
pursuit of his gains, to interfere with the course
of justice in a way which, if attempted by a grocer,
would earn him 10 years’ gaol.
• * * *
The public, with a taste which is possibly per-
verted, possibly not, regards with keen favor news
about crimes, criminals, or alleged criminals. Pan-
dering to this taste, the press—well, most of the
press—devotes its chief energies to a sensational
and attractive display of criminal items. They
form its bargain counter, so to speak. To
tickle the palate of the public, lies, exagge-
rations, gross, injustices, are often indulged
in by the newspapers; and generally with com-
plete immunity from punishment. A suspected
criminal is frequently tried, found guilty, and
condemned by the newspapers long before he has
faced a jury of his equals. His appearance before
a jury is a farce ; he has been already tried by the
newspapers, and the jury can only register their
verdict. And the newspaper, in coming to its
decisions, is restrained by no laws of evidence. In
the case of the wretched Mrs. Sangal, for in-
stance, the daily newspaper sentence of hanging
was come to after a long, bloodshot gloat over the
details of the long-ago Boot-trunk Tragedy in
which Mrs. Sangal’s sister happened to be in-
terested. Possibly an impartial jury, with the
facts of Mrs. Sangal’s crime, and with medical
evidence as to the frequency of sudden mania in
women during pregnancy, before it, would‘have
sent the unhappy wretch to some secure
asylum for the rest of her days. But the
newspapers had decided otherwise on the strength
of the absolutely immaterial fact that Selina
Sangal had a notorious sister. To give another
case within the same month, Worthington
was tried and condemned by certain news-
papers after his arrest and before his legal
trial. If the facts in regard to Madame
Juvent had not been strong enough to fairly con-
vict him, he would nevertheless have gone to
gaol; the newspapers had tried and sentenced
him on his alleged American record.
* * * *
A prisoner under arrest cannot cross-examine the
newspaper which is trying and condemning him ;
he cannot call evidence in rebuttal; he cannot
even make a speech from the dock; he is tried
and sentenced unheard, and the sentence of
the newspaper is almost certainly endorsed by
the jury. This infamous injustice is tolerated
because jurymen, legislators, judges, are, even in
this 20th century, under the delusion that the
newspaper is a patriotic, educative, public-weal-
seeking institution. The cant still succeeds, and
even the newspaper which, with indecent and
savage cruelty, hurries to the gallows a hunted
criminal, howling and pelting at his steps, for the
sake of increasing its profits, can pose, with the
blood-money still defiling its hands, as a “guardian
of the public good,” a “ moral influence ” and “an
educative power.” Disgusting is the cant ; more
disgusting the folly which allows it to deceive.
The Philosophy of History.
BUCKLE’S great work, “The History of
Civilisation,” has at length appeared in a
cheap edition. The price of the old im-
pression (245.) put it out of the reach of
many readers; it is now published at less than
half that price. The book —which is not a his-
tory so much as a series of philosophical conten-
tions, illustrated with facts from history—has
excited much discussion and evoked much
hostility. Criticism and hostility are challenged
by no means timidly in its introductory chapter,
for therein Mr. Buckle claims that hitherto there
has been no history in the true sense of the word,
and that it has been left to him to do for historical
research what Darwin did for biology—bring it to
the basis of exact science. This somewhat flam-
boyant promise was not fulfilled. At the conclu-
sion of Buckle’s history there is left the impression
of profound research, of ingenious and subtle
reasoning, but certainly not. of scientific scru-
pulousness as to fact and argument. In fact,
though apparently an inductive philosopher, seek-
ing the causes of things, as did Bacon, by tracing
back from actual phenomena, Buckle often falls un-
consciously into themethodsofthedeductiveschool.
He creates a “first principle” in his own mind,
and, seeking industriously for phenomena to justify
its conclusions, triumphantly produces some small
shreds of fact from which he claims to argue in-
ductively to the premise on which he has already
decided. For instance, having a theory that the
Aspect of Nature has a vast influence on the pro-
gress of civilisation and of intellectual thought,
Buckle commits himself to the statement that the
difference between the civilisations of India and of
ancient Greece can be traced to that. But it is
hard to follow him in such a daring leap. If it
were obvious that in other countries (such
as Spain), possessing almost the same natural
features as Greece, there had been a similar growth
of civilisation before the Christian era, as
in the land of Plato, the argument would have some
better basis. But that is not so. It is evident in
this, asin manyotherthings, thatßuckle’scontempt
for people with “a perverse hatred of generalisa-
tion ” leads him to extremes of generalisation
which cannot be regarded as scientific.
Nevertheless, Buckle has done splendid work,
though it is incomplete work, and though it is
marred by some errors and some rashness of
speculation. The idea of making the study of the
philosophy of history the main task, and not
merely the subsidiary amusement, of the historian
may not have exactly originated with the author
of “ The History of Civilisation ”; but he cer-
tainly was the first to give that idea practical
life. His effort is not to record facts, but to get at
the reasons for the facts. In so vast a field of re-
search, it would be hardly possible that the first
explorer should come back with a perfect map.
The credit belongs to Buckle that he has shown,
with a distinctness which challenges contradic-
tion, some of the great “ natural features ” of his-
tory : has set down, as it were, landmarks which
have made the task of all future explorers infin-
itely more easy.
Civilisation progresses, Buckle claims, with the
progress of intellect, not of morals. In moral
thought, the world has been stationary for very
many centuries. Nothing that Christ taught in
ethics was unknown to philosophers and to re-
ligions before Him. So clearly, since there
has been no advance in moral thought, and
there has been an advance in civilisation,
morals have nothing to do with civilisation.
A_ daring argument, which seems to neglect
this fact: that if there has been no advance
in moral thought there has been great advance in
moral practice. The philosopher of to-day may
not know more of ethics than the philosopher of
the year 1, but certainly there is a much greater
proportion of people who know better of the dis-
tinction between right and wrong. And is not
that moral progress ? In his very first conclusion
of importance, Buckle, to my mind, must be held
to have rashly and incorrectly generalised. There
are other instances of the same too-great
daring of conclusion on his part. He, for
example, stated that “the war spirit in Eng-
land was becoming utterly extinct ” owing to the
growth of the intellectual, not of the moral force in
the nation. But there has just been concluded a
bitter war of aggression—a practical proof of the
incorrectness of this conclusion. Even without
that, it would seem reasonable to argue that, pos-
tulating a very high intellectual development; a
nation might very well be a most martial one,
undertaking wars of aggression and conquest for
the sake of the material benefit to be gained
thereby; and that the growth of a high moral
spirit, a better and more general recognition of
ethics, would be a greater safeguard against war
than intellectual progress.
But in some of his conclusions—those affecting
matters which he made the subject of patient and
minute inquiry rather than of rash generalisation—
Buckle is a safe guide. His demonstration of the
blighting influence of superstition on civilisation
is complete: his contention that wherever or
whenever a religious organisation has acquired
temporal power the result has been persecution
and retrogression, is established by overwhelming
proof; and his examination into the effects of
militarism on progress is full and enlightening.
It is clear that he attempted too much, that
having clearly traced to their sources some of the
springs of human conduct, he was tempted to
guess at other sources, which he had not made the
subject of such careful inquiry, and to state his
rash generalisations with the same positiveness as
his scientific inductions. In spite of that, his
History stands as one of the notable monuments
of human thought. What he has done far out-
balances what he has failed to do. (From George
Robertson and Co., Sydney.)
F.R.
A Tobacchanalian.
WALLACE IRWIN shows as a humorist
of some grace in his new “ New
Rubaiyat.” Parodies, or any ap-
proaches to parodies, are usually ana-
thema ; but this is hardly a parody, rather an
ingenious imitation of the Fitzgerald translation
of “Omar,” with tobacco, not wine, glorified as
the surnmum bonum of existence. Professing to
be a “translation from Bornese verse,” the
quatrains are seldom merely silly, are very often
humorous, and occasionally are strongly flavored
with epigrammatic salt. When, in addition, the
printing and illustrations are excellent, and there
is a laughable mock-heroic introduction; the
merits are summed up of a book which should be
welcomed in the smoking-room.
Some of the quatrains as samples:—
IV.
Mark how Havana’s sensuous-philtred mead
Dispels the cackling Hag of Night at Need,
And foggy-aureoled the Smoke reveals
The Poppy Flowers that blossom from the Weed,
xx.
Svelte Zamperina’s Dips incarnadine,
And languored lifting, fasten unto mine
Their rubric Message giving Hint and Clew
How frequently a Kiss in Time Saves Nine.
XXXIII.
The Thunderbolts of Heaven’s potent sway
Gather and break, but never can dismay.
When Indestructible Resistless meet,
The “ Please Remit” confronts “ the Oannot Pay.”
xcvi.
Indeed, indeed, Repentance oft before
I swore, but was I smoking when I swore ?
And ever and anon I made Resolve
And sealed the holy Pledge—with one Puff More.
XCVIII.
Then let the balmed Tobacco be my Sheath,
The ardent weed above me and beneath ;
And let me like a Living Incense rise,
A Fifty-Gent Cigar between my Teeth.
Between Ourselves.
MAX O’RELL’S latest, “Between Our-
selves,” is terse and epigrammatic.
Some extracts:
When I take my ladies to a theatre in Paris, there is
nothing- we 1 ke so much as to walk along the boulevard
at midnight, and sit for half-an-hour in one of the cafes.
In New York, after the play, we walked along Broadway
or Fifth Avenue back to our hotel. In neither case did I
once regret it. In London (Che English must forgive me
for saying so, but they know it is the truth), I have to
see the carriage brought right opposite the door, so that
I may push my ladies inside and take them home like
criminals, to spare them a minuce’s sight of the London
West End sights.
He states that he is tired of hearing of “ the gay
Paris ” in English and American halls.
I once went to one of these gay places, and found it
fearfully dreary. It was low, but not even wicked in an
artistic sense. The deepest impression I received was
the enormous amount of English I heard, and I inter-
viewed the manager. “ Why, my dear sir,” he said, “if
it was not for the English who come here, we should have
to put up our shutters.
He accounts for the happiness of the French, as
a nation, to the absence of the money-grubbing
instinct, and states that when a Frenchman (in
• the country) amasses a small fortune, of a couple
of hundred a year, he retires, and gives someone
else a chance. Also, that of a population of
39,000,000, there are 7,000,000 landowners, and,
outside the cities, 72 per cent, of the people own
the houses they live in. In England, he states,
“ practically all the land belongs to about thirty
aristocrats, who have never done anything, except
take the trouble of being born, and who, for the
privileges they enjoy, return nothing to the com-
munity.”
Chap, xxiv., on the Americanisation of the
world:
I say that, before twenty years, Europe will be at the
mercy of America in every respect, its stock exchanges,
its commerce, its industries, just as its customs, will be
American. When shall we see an American woman on a
European throne ? I rather like the idea of it; the
sovereigns of Europe practically belong to one family, and
royalty is sadly deteriorating. The healthy daughter of
some rich Kansas farmer might produce princes and
princesses, robust in body and mind, and in these demo-
cratic times I fail to see why kings and emperors should
not follow the example of their aristocracies. Maybe
they would obtain such dowries from America that our
taxes could be reduced.
Chap, xxv., on Millionaires.
No man can possibly become a millionaire who does
not pay thousands of workers less than they are entitled
to receive for the work and wealth which the}' produce.
The two greatest, truest, and best governed demo-
cracies in the world are New Zealand and the little, island
of Jersey. Apparently the ideal of the democratic
leaders of New South Wales and Victoria is that the
working-man should,' at the expense of the country’s
interests, do a minimum of work for a maximum of pay
within easy reach of city dissipations. But in New
Zealand the Govt, opposes centralisation, and its aim is
to encourage a class of peasant proprietors.
In chap, xviii Max O’Rell tells the familiar
truth that —
Novelists, poets and authors should not marry “ for
the sake of their wives.” There is a worse wife for an
author than a woman who does not appreciate him.
There is the terrible one who thinks she can write as well
as he, and who constantly irritates him by her criticisms.
Some Oupidiana:—
It requires an ounce of courage to get married, and a
ton of it to remain married. Love is a romance, matri-
mony is a dry essay. Novels are more entertaining than
essays.
If a man truly loves a woman, he avoids temptations;
if he loves one a little, he succumbs to them ; if he does
not love one at all, he looks for them.
We blame women when we are the victims of their
inconstancy ; we find them charming when we profit by
it.
A secret, they say, torments a woman like a pam
inside, but she is not ill very long.
Matrimony is sometimes such a heavy burden that it
takes more than two people to carry it.
The book (56 chapters) is well worth reading.
In compiling the Commonwealth Annual this
year the publishers have followed the safe plan of
palling on practically all the best-known Austra-
ban writers and artists to contribute. Henry
Lawson, Victor Daley, “Banjo” Paterson, Ed-
ward Dyson, Ethel Turner, Walter Jeffery, Albert
Dorrington, “ Steele Rudd ” and Roderic Quinn
have all given, if not of their best, at any rate of
good matter with their signatures attached. The
illustrations by Fred Leist, Arthur Collingridge,
H. Garlick, D. H. Souter, A. J. Fischer and
others are generally very well done. The make-
up is closely modelled on English mag. style ; it
is a very attractive shilling’s-worth.
Maude Wheeler on the Literary Woman :
Of course writing women make copy out of their affairs
—of the heart and otherwise. Examples are infinite in
number. What’s the good of having vivid and startling
times if they are never to be turned into copy ? Women
who write are abnormal, and generally quite useless in
other directions. Men prefer a woman who can cook;
cooking is an artistic necessity, literature is a luxury.
Some literary women attract writing men—witness the
Brownings, and George Eliot and Lewes. Certainly a
man who writes understands literary women better than
the ordinary crowd, but that doesn’t always make him
hanker after a lettered chum. There are worse things in
the world than making copy out of one’s husband ; it’s
better than making it out of another woman’s husband.
je
On the marriage of literary folk, Ada
Kidgell writes ;
Those who declare the marriages of literary people to
be failures lose sight of the fact that most marriages are
failures, because most human beings are failures. But
the affairs of literary incompatibles are, as a rule, public
property, either from their own or other people’s adver-
tising, whereas average folk keep their troubles, matri-
monial or otherwise, to themselves. Past and present
records prove a higher rate of successes in writers’ mating
ventures than in those of other classes, especially where
both partners have the scribbling habit (always excepting
the Carlyles). Authors who consider a wife handy as a
cook and sock-mender, and choose for auditors of their
verse ladies in laburnum gowns, would probably simi-
larly carry interesting points of the tea trade or the stock
exchange to leisured listeners. They don’t seek ex-
traneous consolation because they are poets, but because
they are men. Dickens would have broken his wife’s
heart, and Thackeray sent his mad, and Heine demanded
the adoration of “ Die mouche,” if they had been in, the
ink-making trade instead of the ink-spilling and married
to the wrong woman. Few women writers make the mis-
take of wedding a man of dissimilar tastes. When they
do, as in the cases of Caroline Norton and Felicia
Hemans, there is tragedy. George Eliot, Elizabeth
Browning, and Charlotte Bronte took as partuers men of
like interests and were entirely happy. Unfortunately,
these instances seem to show that it is only the woman
of very mature years who is equal to this wise choice.
The moral apparently is : Look out for fnental equality ;
if you are intelligent, take an intelligent mate ; if you
are a fool, wed with folly.
J*
Writes “ G. 8.”: The discussion in your columns
over errors in the pronunciation of classical names
reminds me that I read somewhere, in my childish
days, a rhymed protest addressed by a professor
of Greek to his pastor. It was somewhat after
this fashion :
You said last Sunday—it did pain us—
“ You know the household of Stephanus,”
“ Sfephanus ” is the man we know ;
And let us hope you’ll call him so.
RECEIVED.
“Fred. Walker,” by Clementina Black, a con-
tinuation of Duckworth and Co.’s “Popular
Library of Art.” . This series maintains its high
standard of interest; the illustrations are excel-
lent and the letterpress bright. Mr. Walker is
represented in Australia by a picture in the Mel-
bourne Art Gallery, “ The Right of Way.”
“Public Service Retrenchment in N.5.W.,” by
G. Lynn Little. This is an account of the Reid
massacre of the Civil Service in 1896, told by one
of the victims. Mr. Little does not tell his story
well, but prints a confused jumble of accounts
and papers.
J*
TO CORRESPONDENTS;
E.W.D. : The personification of “ Dives” as the typical
rich man is only an instance of one of the commonest ten-
dencies of language; no need to argue that “ dives ” is
a Latin adjective ; that is admitted.
Ethel Turner continues on the pleasant, rose-
bordered path she has chosen, in her latest book,
“Little Mother Meg” (Ward,- Lock and Co.),
which shows no lessening of charm and interest
compared with previous works. The Australian
child is happy in having so skilful and so sympa-
thetic a painter, and Ethel Turner is wise in
keeping to her metier; for she has established by
doing so a first place among Australian writers in
the affections of the reading public. The reward
is deserved ; she has the art of telling simple
things is an interesting way ; a graceful style ; and
a sweet wholesomeness.
Louis Becke’s latest book has the unwieldly
title of “The Strange Adventures of James
Sherwinton and other Stories.” The scene is again
the South Seas, and suggests that Mr. Becke is
gathering up with some effort the crumbs of his
experiences. Never a writer of imagination, but
rather one of vivid sight and brilliant sketching
ability, Becke’s first work was so charming because
it gave with an air of bright reality scenes and
incidents of a savage life. Now he goes back on
old tracks of memory, and is not able to conjure
up such vivid pictures as of old. He should go
forth and get fresh experiences. Our copy is
from Greo. Robertson and Co , Sydney.

Morbid Months of Misery.
“A DESPAIRING WRECK OF HUMANITY.* 1
A Great Friend in Time of Need.
% Case of tyr. H. BARNETT.
(fiT A SPECIAL REPORTER.)
It is worthy of note that the interviews
which have from time to time appeared in
these columns are singularly free from mere
theorising about the subjects dealt with,
and are entirely confined to practical, though
none the less interesting, details, an addition
to which is made by Henry Barnett, of No.
212 James-street, Leichhardt.
“ To show what important events some-
times arise from trivial causes,’* said the
gentleman mentioned, “Imust inform you
that one day I scratched my elbow with a
piece of zinc, which was hanging loosely in-
side a waggon. It was nothing to grow
alarmed about, I thought, and I took no
notice of it until the next day, when my
arm appeared to be slightly inflamed,, but I
did not take any precaution to stop it get-
ting anv worse. Not till a week afterwards
did I bother myself, and by that time my
arm was so much swollen, and the pain was
so intense, that I could not go any longer
without seeking some advice.”
“Where did you go for itJ” the repor-
ter enquired.
“ To the hospital, and on three successive
visits my arm was operated upon. It was
then that I realised the great disaster which
had overtaken me, as the doctor said I had
just come in time to save losing my life from
blood poisoning, so by that you will know
that I was in a dreadful state.”
“Were you able to attend to your busi-
ness ? ”
“Work of any description was tieyond
my powers for nearly four months, for my
constitution was fairly broken up and I was
almost helpless. This being the first illness
I ever had, you may depend that I felt my
misfortune very keenly, and the thought
that I would never be able to use my arm
again nearly drove me frantic. I sat and
brooded over my troubles till my mind
lapsed into a chronic state of despondency,
and although the doctor said I had come
‘ just in time,’ I did not feel so sure about
it, as I felt so generally weak and wretched
, that I doubted if I could survive the terrible
calamity which had occurred. For five
days and nights I did not eat a morsel of
food, although I tried hard enough to take
a little nourishment, but I could not swal-
low what I put to my mouth, and this, to-
gether with the agonising pains I was
suffering, made a thorough wreck of me.
My cruel tortures would not allow me to
sleep, neither did I know which way to lie
for ease, so that when daylight came I was
glad to get up in spite of having scarcely
sufficient strength to walk about, for if I
tii.il an attempt my head turned so
giddy that I st*t>n had to sit down again.
My wife was kept going tdght and day bath-
ing and bandaging my arm, but even the
great care I received and the doctor’s medi-
cines combined could not succeed in elimi-
nating the poison which prevented the
wound from healing I was completely at
the mercy of my original complaint, which in
time made my recovery more improbable,
because of the ruin it wrought on my in-
ternal organs, and as ailment after ailment
was piled up I saw that my prospects were
more gloomy than ever. My nerves were
always on the quiver, which showed that
the strain on my system was too great for
them, and I felt particularly nervous on
hearing any noises, which also grated on my
ears and made me extremely irritable. I
oould see that it was no good taking any
more doctor’s medicines, as I was getting no
benefit from them and they made me feel
sick; besides, they did not help me in re-
gard to my appetite, so I decided on pur-
chasing some Clements Tonic.”
a 11 How did that idea originate ? ”
r H Well, I had taken Clements Tonic to
give me,an appetite once before when I was
feeling a bit jaded, and it made me eat like
a horse ; and now that I was so feeble, and
run down as low as I could be, I thought
Clements Tonic might do the same again.
And, upon my word, it did ; but for a while
I was sorry that Clements Tonic made me
eat so heartily, as my digestive organs were
in such a shockingly bad state that all food
disagreed with me. My stomach always
got filled with wind, and my chest pains
were so weighty that I could scarcely get
my breath, while the awful pains I had
hitherto been maddened with were added
to by something going wrong between my
shoulders. The taste in my mouth and the
furred condition of my tongue testified to
the disordered state of my liver, but the
aches in my limbs and across my loins told
the direful tale that my vitality was per-
fectly exhausted. When I come to look
back upon those weeks of abject torture and
misery, when I could not even read for the
swollen feeling in my eyes, with no anima-
tion in my body, and with no hope left, I
consider it a merciful of
Providence which influenced me to perse-
vere with Clements Tonic, by the aid of
which came a delightful experience that
broke the morbid monotony of all those
months. When the first bottle of Clements
Tonic was finished I was sleeping more
soundly than I had done for some time past,
the reason being that there was a decrease
in the pains which had kept me awake so
consistently, and how thankful I was for
the rest, too ! To find that X could obtain
relief from Clements Tonic gave me a fresh
interest in life, although I did not antici-
pate throwing off my afflictions altogether,
as they had held out so obstinately against
other medicines ; but I was grateful for all
mercies, and when the discomforts following
my meals were lessened you can imagine
my great surprise. Little by little my
strength returned, and the vitalizing pro-
perties of Clements Tonic came in evidence
by the gradual healing of the wound in my
arm, and now there is nothing but a scar to
show where it was opened. I had lost a
considerable amount of flesh during my
illness, but in a few weeks it was all re-
stored, so it is needless to say that Clements
Tonic put my digestive organs in thorough
order, besides infusing so much power to my
nervous system that you could not have got
a tremble out of my nerves no matter how
vou might have tried. Before long I had
shaken off every pain that had tormented me,
and with the full complement of strength
which Clements Tonic restored to my sys-
tem, I went 1 ack to work none the worse
for the terrible trials I had undergone.” „
“ Are you sure, you were quite cured ?”
“I ha\e not had the least thing wrong
with me since, and I feel as hearty as though
my constitution were made of iron, so
Clements Tonic did its work well. I was
a despairing wreck of humanity when it
was called into requisition, and for saving
me as it did I owe my life-long thanks to
Clements Tonic—an admission which you
can publish in any way that may do good.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Greasin’.
I can hear the stokers singin’
As they give the boilers coal,
And the big swells set her swingin’
With a lazy, heavy roll.
There’s a crazy steam-pump moanin’,
There’s a tail-rod just a-squeal,
And a shaftin’-bearin’ groanin’
’Way down aft beside her heel.
Every damned eccentric’s winkin’,
And the cranks are makin’ eyes ;
All a-shiver when they’re sinkin’
And a-rattlin’ when they rise.
There is shoutin’ down the tunnel,
Where the mad propellers toil,
And from packin’ to her funnel
Every darn’ thing’s callin’ “ Oil!”
And I’m oilin’, oilin’, oilin’
In a temp’rature like hell,
With the heated oil a-smokin’
Till you feel’s if you’re a-chokin’,
And the knockin’ guide-bars tell
That I’ve got to be a-toilin’
Till God rings His knock-off bell.
There’s a cool wind on the water
(Or there was an hour ago),
And the moonlight made me sorter
Want to take Life pretty slow ;
And the funnel was a-swayin’
’Thwart the twinklin’ stars aloft,
And I hear the water playin’
Round her transom, white and soft.
It has made me think, and wonder
Why I’ve got to graft below
With this ceaseless, rollin’ thunder
Thumpin’, thumpin’ blow on blow.
If ’twere only for a season—
On and off—l wouldn’t mind,
’Stead of everlastin’ greasin’
To this everlastin' grind.
But I’m oilin', oilin’, oilin’,
And I’ll do so till the last,
For a man can’t change his labor—
P’raps his job won’t fit his neighbor —
And he cannot change his past,
So I'll go on toilin’, moilin’,
Till the Final Bugle-Blast.
Oh ! the skipper looks a daisy
In his tropic uniform,
And the officers are lazy
When the atmosphere is warm;
And the trimmer’s work is tryin’,
And the stokers curse the glare,
But they ain’t exactly fryin’,
They do get a bit of air—
The salooners they seem happy
With their music and their girls
That have eyes so bright and snappy
And a smile like deep-sea pearls ;
But I guess no one’s no bloomin’
Better off than me below,
With the great cranks boomin’, boomin’
Thunderous and very slow.
Tho' Fm greasin’, greasin’, greasin’,
There’s some hearts above I know
That’s as bitter and as burnin’
And as full of hopeless yearnin’
As the hearts that beat below ;
And they know not rest nor easin’,
Like them rods a-swinging so.
Oh ! I hear the firemen damnin’,
Glarin’ fires and singin’ steam,
And the heavy doors are slammin’,
And I have no time to dream,
When the cranks laugh loud in sinkin’
And look solemn when they rise.
And each queer eccentric’s winkin’
At the light, and makin’ eyes.
Oli ! the toll the owners levy
Is a heavy one indeed,
And the hand of God is heavy
When you’ve drifted from your creed.
Yet, through all the engine’s moanin’,
Seems to me I hear a cry:
“Life would know less grief and groanin’
If the oil-cups wasn’t dry.”
And I ponder as I’m greasin’
On the friction that there is —
Glad to-days and sad to-morrows.
Oh l the grief of one man’s sorrows,
And the joy of one man’s bliss —
Life’s machin’ry wants some easin’
Such as laughter and a kiss.
QUILP N.
An absurd ukase of Sydney Water Board
is that one cutting off the plunge-bath. To many,
the plunge is more than a luxury ; it is an abso-
lute necessity. And, anyhow, the saving is not
more than a million gallons a day. Meters should
be attached to every house-supply, and a maximum
allowance at usual rates allowed, everyone using
more to “ pay through the nose.” The educa-
tional value of meters is enormous. Broken Hill
people waste no water. They all have meters.
Melbourne Argus :
The all-important points on which definite information
is wanted at the earliest moment are—What does the
deficit threaten to be, and what are the Government’s
complete retrenchment and taxation proposals ? The
answers to these questions will he the main substance of
the Budget. When Parliament has them it will know
both what it must do and why. The Federal Treasurer
has announced that the probable return of customs
revenue to Victoria will be less by a substantial sum than
had been hoped for.
This is unblushingly printed by the paper whose
sheet-anchor argument in its barracking for Free-
trade during the tariff debate was that the
customs revenue was going up by such leaps and
bounds that there was no necessity to further tax
for revenue.

SATURDAY, NOVEMBER 15, 1902.
The Syndicate Railway Crase.
Australia has constructed public works and spent upon
them about 230 millions sterling ; but it has adopted a
policy which, in its general effect, limits their usefulness
and renders them a source of other than profit and pros-
perity. Settlement is obstructed, immigration is dis-
couraged, and the increase of population grows
more and more unsatisfactory. Since the year 1880 the
railway mileage of Victoria has increased by 176 per
Cent., but the population has improved by only 40 per
cent. Even allowing that the extension of the iron road
has been excessive, it is hard to believe that the slow
advance of population has justified reasonable expecta-
tions. In America private'management of railways has
led to the encouragement of settlement in a manner quite
impracticable in Australia under State direction, and
the associated systems of restriction of commerce and
industry. “ (Jockspur ” lines, of which the Pinnaroo
Railway, if constructed, would be a glaring example,
cross-country connections, and other non-paying lines in
Victoria, are responsible for a yearly interest shortage of
£300,000 ; while more than half-a-million sterling has
been added to the capital account for useless surveys and
dismantled and closed railways. Such facts constitute an
eloquent testimony to a rampant rural parochialism, the
obverse side of the medal of Little Australianism. —
Adelaide Observer.
Look at what private railways have done for land settle-
ment in Canada and the United States. — Jenkins
Iscariot, of S.A. (abbreviated from his speech in favor of
the proposed Northern Territory 90,000,000-acre steal).
I feel convinced, after seeing the method of manage-
ment of railways in America, which are all privately
owned, that there would be little difficulty in getting a
syndicate of capitalists to lease our railways and pay
three per cent, of interest on their cost. — David Elder,
in Melbourne Age.
The above are quoted at random as a few
samples of a kind of literature which is just
now flooding the Australian press. When-
ever things are a little worse than usual in
Australia—when the great dead weight of
unprofitable debt, incurred because the
politicians have persistently declined for 40
years to levy enough taxes to meet the cost
of government, weighs more heavily than
usual; and the land sales fall off because the
best part of the public estate has been used
up to pay current expenses ; and mining is
depressed; and drought is bad ; and it is
difficult to keep on buying more imported
luxuries in proportion to our population
than any other country on earth—literature
of this kind always becomes common. Then
the comforting Tory press and the
Jenkinses and the Elders and the other
mental remnants assure themselves that
the 40 years of waste, of unproductive
borrowing, and refusal to pay sufficient taxes,
have nothing at all to do with the case. And
the Jenkinses and the rest discover that
privately-owned railways, because of what
they have done in promoting land-settlement
in Canada and the United States, are the
remedy for all our troubles.
* * * *
In this matter, Jenkins and co. assume
off-hand that land - grant railways would
enable people to settle down and prosper in
regions where even the crow is giving up the
struggle as a bad job. They assume that
land-grant railways will make it rain
where it doesn’t rain now; will make a
range of Rocky Mountains down the
centre of Australia to condense the clouds ;
will supply us with a Mississippi and a
Missouri, and a hundred other big rivers,
and will make irrigation possible where there
is no water to irrigate with. Also, that
because private railways have done much
or little for settlement in Texas and
Colorado and Wyoming and Dakota,
which are striped like a zebra 'with
ever-flowing Streams, or in Manitoba with
its thirty or forty splendid lakes and its
two or three hundred rivers, that they can
easily do the same on the countless leagues
of dust, with a mudhole about every 100
miles, which make up most of Australia.
Their theories that syndicates will make the
grass grow, and companies will make the
dead sheep live again, and private enterprise
will lay the dust, are interesting examples
of how man is always finding out that there
is some way of getting rid of his debts with-
out paying them, and that it wasn’t the
whisky which made him drunk, but the fish.
• * • •
In such a matter it is always better to
leave off promiscuous whirling and get down
to facts. The only part of the United
States which even dimly resembles Australia
is the part which lies east of the Rocky
Mountains, and west of Nebraska. Even
that portion is vastly better than most of
Australia—more fertile and better watered.
It is a region with from 10 to 25 inches of
rain, and with occasional excursions up to
50 inches. The average is probably about
22 inches—the same as Russia; whereas
nearly half of Australia receives about five
inches, and two-thirds of it doesn’t average
10 inches. Texas is in a comparatively
fertile belt of the U.S. It is mentioned with
grief that in one year in its history the
driest part of Texas only had a 10-inch
rainfall. Texas has been settled for over
200 years. It was so far advanced 70 years
ago as to set up as an independent republic,
and conduct a long war on its own re-
sources, and utterly defeat the Napoleon of
Mexico at San Jacinto. It dates back to
times before Australia was thought of. It
commenced early the gorgeous system of
private land-grant railways which is to “pro-
mote settlement ” in Australia, and 20 years
ago it had 5800 miles of private railway—
more than was possessed then by all Aus-
tralia. It has greatly increased its mileage since
then. And it has persons to the square
mile—a great deal less than Victoria. Yet
Texas is fortunate beyond the average.
* * * •
Nevada has South Australia’s rainfall, and
mineral resources such as S.A. never pos-
sessed,and it is private-railwayed to a great
extent, and it has two people to five square
miles—less than half as many as S.A., with
the further difference that S.A.’s population
is increasing ; Nevada’s is dying away. Put
into tabular form these are the results :
Popula-
tion
per 100
square
miles.
N.W. Territories, Canada (private railways) .. 11
Westralia (private railways, now partly taken
over by State, and State lines) 19
Nevada, U.S. (private railways) 40
S. Australia and N. Territory (State railways). 40
British Columbia, Canada (private railways) .. 60
Queensland (State railways) 75
Wyoming-, U.S. (private railways) 90
S. Australia, apart from N. Territory (State rail-
ways) 97
Arizona, U.S. (private railways) .. .. 110
New Mexico, U.S. ( „ „ ) .. .. 160
Montana, U.S. ( „ „ ) ... .. 170
Idaho, U.S. ( „ „ ) .. ..190
Utah, U.S. ( „ „ ) .. .. 340
Manitoba, Canada ( ~ ~ ) .. .. 385
New South Wales (State railways) 438
Oregon, U.S. (private railways) .. .. 440
Quebec, Canada ( „ „ ) .. .. 485
Colorado, U.S. ( ~ ~ ) .. .. 520
Tasmania (mostly State railways) 657
Washington, U.S. (private railways) .. .. 770
California, U.S. ( ~ ~ ) .. .. 950
Texas, U.S. ( „ „ ) .. .. 1160
Victoria (State railways) 1367
As a matter of strict accuracy, California,
Washington and Oregon should not be in-
cluded in this list. They are well-watered
coastal States, which don’t in the least
resemble any but the most extremely
favored parts of Australia. But when they
are included we strike the fact that Califor-
nia, which has been building up for over 200
years, with good soil and good rainfall over
most of its area, with its immense gold
deposits, with more railway than any Aus-
tralian State possesses, and with all the
blessed benefits of private railway owner-
ship to “ promote settlement,” isn’t so
well settled as Victoria. Colorado, with
richer gold mines than Victoria, and a great
area of fertile country, and streaked all over
with rivers and private enterprise railways,
can’t even compare with Tasmania for close
settlement. And Canada has had over 350
years in which to produce its results, Quebec
being one of its longest-settled provinces.
* • • *
There is nothing in the actual facts to
show that land-grant railway syndicates
know any better how to settle a new
country than a State railway department
does—or even that they know so well. The
United States is thickly peopled in places—
the places where are the great manufactur-
ing industries that were created by its Pro-
fcectionisfc policy, and the great mines. When
the land-grant railway builders struck out
into the wilderness to “ promote settle-
ment ” they succeeded just as badly as the
State did—or worse ; and what success they
realised cost an appalling price. Along the
great Southern Pacific line (which received
a decent-sized principalityin land subsidy), or
the Atlantic and Pacific line (which received
a 42,000,000-acre grant), or the Central
Pacific (which received a grant of 12,000,000
acres) or the Northern Pacific (which received
47,000,000 acres), or the Union Pacific
(which received 12,000,000 acres), things are
mostly very like the dreary road to Bourke,
except that the weather and the grass are
quite different. Over huge stretches there is
a Kne-repairer’s house every 20 miles, and
every 100 miles a “ town ” which is made up
of a few railway employees, and the woman
who washes their clothes, and some sundries.
The United States lives on its mines
and manufactures—manufactures alone re-
present about twice as much income
as the agricultural and pastoral industries
put together. The opening up of the agri-
cultural country by land-grant railways is a
very small item in the wealth of the Union.
As to trying to make wealth by similarly
opening up the waterless Australian interior
where the rabbit and the crow are alike
giving up the fight, there is just about as
good a prospect for a land-grant railway to
Sheol.
• • • . •
Certainly the United States has got over
some difficulties which Australia has not yet
surmounted, but Australia can surmount
them in exactly the same way if it is so in-
clined. Almost every railway in the States
has been bankrupt—some of them two or
three times over. In the 12 years 1880-1891
some £390,000,000 of alleged railway capital
disappeared by insolvency, through over
36,000 miles of railway being sold by
the bailiff, and these are only 12 years
taken at random out of 50 or 60. If
Australia likes to repudiate a correspond-
ing proportion of its public debt, which
is its railway capital, it can also reduce the
burdens on its lines, reduce its charges, and
get the money to buy the new and much
more economical rolling-stock it so much
requires. And if, in addition, it likes to
endow its State railways with land on the
same scale in point of area and value, as the
United States, and if it had the land to do it
with, it could, in N.S.W. at all events, re-
duce its charges to about a third of the pre-
sent figure. The success of the United
States’ private railways isn’t anything like
what is commonly supposed. The dead-
head trouble is even worse than on the
Australian State lines—it is reckoned that
one-tenth of the passengers travel on the
nod. The closed and abandoned railway is
just as conspicuous a feature there as here.
The dividends earned are just about on the
Australian level. The land-grant system
has, in many cases, blocked settlement
in a most shameful fashion in 1882
five companies had 22,000,000 acres
locked up, and refused to either sell or
lease an acre, because when they took
out their formal title, which would
enable them to deal with the property,
the land would become liable to taxes, and
they considered it cheaper, pending an in-
crease in values, to pay no taxes and block
all progress. The rolling stock on the show
lines is magnificent, while on many lines
that lie away from the principal centres and
are little seen by the average visitor, it is old,
worn, neglected and damnable. The great
secret of the low rates charged on Ameri-
can lines lies in the fact that the
country pays a large proportion of the
charges in the shape of grants and subsidies,
that an immense amount of the capital has
been repudiated, thus reducing the interest-
burden, and that the American manager has
a rich and fertile country to trade upon.
Also the American isn’t an Australian, and
isn’t wedded to English ideas ; consequently,
he uses larger and lighter trucks and heavier
engines, and other economies. A good part
of the money to buy these was obtained by
repudiating liabilities. The money for such
episodes as the war between the Santa F 5
Co. and the Denver and Rio Grande Co.,
when they maintained small armies, tore up
each other’s lines, and fought small battles
with artillery, offered rewards for the cap-
ture of each other’s managers, and burned
Colorado generally, was often obtained in the
same way.
* * * *
Private railways won’t make Australia
fertile. All the good land Australia possesses
wouldn’t supply land grants on the American
scale, and it is bad policy to give away all
your victuals merely to pay for having them
cooked. Australia can’t afford to pay
£60,000,000 in land and cash to a trans-
continental syndicate, as Canada did —it
hasn’t £60,000,000. And, after all,
what land-grant railways have, or have
not, done in America, under totally
different conditions, doesn’t concern us.
Anyone who wants to know what they can
do here under our own conditions has only
to look at the Westralian Midland concern,
which received nearly 3,000,000 acres, and
undertook to settle 5000 immigrants on the
land, and borrowed £500,000 from the State,
and then went insolvent, and never settled
anybody on the land at all. The Tasmanian
Main Line and the Deloraine Railway, and
the Emu Bay concern, and the abortive
Great Western are other cases in point—in
fact Australia has had some dozens of private
railways, and has been sorry for them all.
No amount of lying about what private rail-
way management has done at the other side
of the world can counterbalance the fact that
we have experimented with it here for fifty
years, and have regularly fallen in.
Australia’s trouble is that it won’t pay
enough taxes to cover its outgoings, but
borrows, and puts off the evil day, and
hopes that something will turn up. When
the evil day becomes uncomfortably pressing
someone suggests private railways as a
remedy, and someone suggests prayer, and
someone suggests Freetrade, and someone
suggests more loans to “ develop the great
natural resources of the country,” and some-
one else proposes faith in Pkovidence. And
all these things are vanity. The one plain,
brutal remedy is to stop borrowing, to pay
more taxes, to buy less and manufacture
more, to cut up the big estates in the rare
patches of good country and settle the
people on them, to build our public works
out of our own money—and possibly to export
Owe’Sullivan.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Defunct Kyabram Programme.
The great Kyabramapootra ‘ ‘ Reform ” fraud i 3
dying so very young that it was hardly worth
while for the cheerless fowl to be born at all. If
there was anything on which the Kyabram pro-
gramme was solid it was the putting of the Vic-
torian State finances on an absolutely sound
basis. It wasn’t specially democratic ;it wasn’t
brilliant; it wasn’t even wildly intelligible ; but
in the matter of its financial honesty it was teak-
built with a concrete flooring. Its avowed object
was to extinguish the deficit, to make ends meet,
to stop the financial drift, to put an end to the
wretched Peacock policy of charging unproductive
works to loans, to check borrowing—in fact, it
was a financial reform party first, last, and all
the time. Irvine and Shiels and Bent held
office on the Kyabram policy exclusively. And
now the Irvine Government loan proposals for its
first financial year are out, and it proposes to
spend £578,500 on repairs to railways (though
repairs are an item that, above all others, should
be paid out of revenue), and about £200,000 on
other equally unproductive works. Among other
things it proposes to charge £40,000 of municipal
subsidy to loans, also £15,000 for repairs to roads,
and there are many other equally awful items.
So the first step of the Government which was to
stop the spending of loan money on repairs and
unproductive jobs, is to propose the spending of
more loan money on unproductive works than any
Vic. Govt, has done for very many years ! As for
stopping the deficit, Premier Peacock, last year,
had a shortage of about £450,000, and that lugu-
brious and miserable “reformer,” Shiels, says he
foresees a shortage of over £1,000,000 this year,
and the economies so far proposed don’t really
cover one-third of this. When the great Financial
Reform Party sees its way already to steal far
more loan money for revenue purposes than
Peacock ever did, and at the same time to have
a far larger deficit than Peacock had, and when
its chief expedients for meeting the shortage con-
sist in misappropriating the sinking fund and in
postponing the payment of certain debts, the
Kyabram programme, regarded as one of financial
reform, has fallen through in a pitiable fashion.
The Age, in a recent issue, solemnly warned the
Victorian Labor members that in opposing the
Irvine Government they are running counter to
the will of the people as expressed at last general
election. It would be much more truthful to say
that the Labor members are the only ones who
are attending to the will of the people. If the
people declared solidly for anything it was for
economy. A Government that proposes to steal
nearly £BOO,OOO of loan money to cover its ordinary
expenditure, and expects a huge deficit in addi-
tion, wasn’t what the people bargained for.

Resurrecting the Flyspeck's Gridiron.
Van Dobson’s Land seems in a somewhat alarmed
state at the prospect of losing its beloved Great
Western Railway Syndicate. Although conces-
sions and time-extensions innumerable have been
granted to the promoters at various dates, which
concessions have for years been hawked around
among the money markets of the world without
the hawkers having been able to find a buyer at
the price asked, there area very few weeks to run
before the latest G. W. Railway Act becomes
void. So, unless the promoters can accomplish in
six weeks that which they have failed to bring off
in six years, the chances are that the famous grid-
iron won’t grid after all. The Act gives the Co.
to Dec. 15 next to prove its bona Jides by
commencing the construction of the line, and to
satisfy the Government that at least £250,000
is available to continue such construction. And
as present indications hardly justify a hope for the
successful flotation, Guesdon, M.H.R. (hitherto a
fervid barracker for the syndicate), has ap-
proached the Lewis Government with a proposi-
tion to this effect:
If the present promoters fail to carry out the provi-
sions of the Act, it should not be allowed to lapse, but
should be vested in the Government of the day, as a
negotiable instrument, whereby arrangements can be
entered into with any contractors, or financiers, who
will undertake the construction of the railway, the
concessions under the Act to be transferred to them as
consideration for such construction, &c.
This proposition has been seriously discussed at a
conference of Tasmanian Ministers and railway
officials. Guesdon, M.H.R., accentuates the
painful fact that Tas. legislators are as slow to
learn as ever, and indicates that the distressful
island of Dodery is still as hopelessly and unac-
countably enamored of the useless syndicate which
never does anything, as of old. No State in these
latitudes has suffered more ignominy and disaster
at the hands of syndicates than has Tasmania.
Its early railways were mostly built by syndicates,
and very badly built at that. Then they were so
badly managed by syndicates, that popular clamor
drove the Government to buy them up at a high
price, in order to give the people a decent service
that didn’t require passengers to get out and
shove behind. The Emu Bay line still remains
to show Tasmania how private management can
charge the public 4d. a mile for the privilege of
riding in the worst carriages in Tasmania, and
yet fail to make dividends out of it. And still
Tasmania has a small, bedraggled sort of idea
that syndicate railways are the one sure remedy
for all its troubles.
The New Queensland Land Bill.
The new Land Bill just now before Q. Assembly
might be tersely described as a measure to capi-
talise the drought in the interests of financial
institutions. It provides for the extension of
pastoral tenures to an extent hitherto undreamt of
—inasmuch as first-class Western country is given


an extension of 21 years after the end of the pre-
sent unexpired term of lease—which will make,
in many cases, a total term of between 30 and 40
years from present date. Lands in the Darling
Downs and Burnett districts, hitherto held
exempt from extension, because, as the
pastoralists themselves last year confessed,
“the rainfall was comparatively regular and
much of the land suitable for agricultural
purposes” are also to receive an extension (in the
case of first-class lands) of ten years in addition to
present unexpired term and much larger extensions
(making in all a maximum of 42 years) in the case
of lands other than first-class. The general
provisions for resumption in West Q. are phenom-
enally small, while re-assessment of rent can only
take place twice in the whole term of the extended
lease —so that, in many cases of first-class Western
country, re-assessment will only be possible once in
twenty years. . . . This measure is, avowedly, an
“imitation of the Western Lands Act of N.5.W.,”
but whereas the Western Lands of N.S.W. are,
roughly speaking, bounded by a line about 230
miles from the coast, the lands dealt with by the
Q. Bill come, in some cases, within eight miles of
the seaboard, and comprise areas of first class pas-
toral land—superior in normal seasons, to any-
thing else in Australia. The idea of applying the
principle underlying the N.S.W. Western Lands
extensions to country like the Flinders, the
Mitchell, the Burnett, and the Darling Downs
is a wildly humorous one, and could only
be seriously entertained in a country sick to
death with drought, deficit and mis-government.
Resumptions under the new Bill can only take
place to the extent of seven-sixteenths of the hold-
ing during the whole term of the extended lease.
Inasmuch as the aggregation of grazing areas has
been a crying evil in times past, the Philp Govt,
has set to work to remedy this by abolishing the
provisions against dummying, thereby allow-
ing the big lessee to aggregate as much
as he likes himself and thereby counteract
his rival the aggregating selector. This
is reform with a vengeance ! In order, probably,
to counterbalance the huge concessions granted to
the big pastoral lessees, Minister O’Connell has
handled the grazing farmer very gingerly. Grazing
selectors all over Queensland are in an uproar
over the Bill, which apparently makes mock of
their misfortunes by allowing them to buy their
selections outright at 10s. an acre—2s. fid. cash
down, and the rest to be paid over a term of
20 years. Fancy allowing a selector, in the
claws of some financial institution—a man who'
cannot even pay up his ordinary rental —the
privilege of buying out his land at 10s. an acre !
He can only do it one way—by borrowing the
money from some bank, and accepting the bank
in place of the State as a landlord. Why not
classify his land (as the big holdings are classified)
and extend his tenure accordingly ? The most
scandalous feature, however, of the Bill is the
removal of all restrictions on the alienation of
Crown lands. The Philp Govt, is desperate
for want of cash, and is determined to break
or burst something. The recent experiments
in railway syndicate legislation were nothing
in comparison with this Bill, which will simply
paralyse West Queensland in the course of the
next few years. A measure of relief for the
pastoral industry was undoubtedly necessary; but
a measure for the practical locking-up and sacrifice
of the best part of a province can scarcely be called
a necessity—even after a five years’ drought. But
the average Queenslander is so broken-hearted
with deficit and disaster that he’ll stand almost
anything.

Bent’s Luck.
Minister for Railways Tommy Bent has practi-
cally brushed aside the nominal head of Vic.
Railway Department, and re-established political
control, and under his management the Vic. rail-
ways have become the joke of the continent.
Thomas is a man of tremendous energy, and he
casts his of substance headlong into every-
thing he undertakes, but he casts it recklessly and
without discrimination, and everything goes
wrong. Two days’ record of the Vic. lines under
Bent’s management lately ran somewhat as
follows:
Theatrical company couldn’t perform because its
scenery and clothes were wandering up and down Tommy
Bent’s railways somewhere. Co. angry and the loser by
a night’s takings.
Melbourne-Beechworth train two hours late.
Breakdown of the “ tablet ” system at Benalla, signal-
man being unable to get tablet out of box. Train two
hours late, and piloted by a man with a bike. Great
language. Signalman at Benalla still struggling to get
tablet out of box.
Number of special trains put on for Tatura show. All
about two hours late. Great execration among people
who had come to see the bloated heifer.
Great row at Wycheproof on the ground that the Mel-
bourne train has given up all attempt to keep time, and
is always an hour behind.
Engine left Drouin quite forgetting to take its passen-
gers with it. Passengers filled Drouin with language for
some hours. Engine gradually became aware something
was wrong, and came back. Train reached its destina-
tion some time.
Engine on Gippsland mail collapsed. Goods engine
hauled the train along gradually for a while and then
collapsed also. Train stood and admired the scenery till
another engine could be sent for.
Complaint from eastern districts that the crippled
engine takes 18 hours to haul the train 107) miles, and
that the district is going to walk in future.
Complaint that goods train broke down and stood at
Morwell for 1) days, and that Morwell didn’t like the
smell of the perishable part of the cargo.
Pour more engines break down. Great language in
various parts of State.
Complaint that passengers to Birchip didn’t think it
fair that their train should stand for 5) hours or some
such period at Donald. Said they got tired of looking at
Donald.
Minister Bent meanwhile issues orders at the
top of his voice in Melbourne, and sits up most of
the night telegraphing, directing that everything
shall be done instantly, even if there is nothing to
do it with, delivering ukases, threatening to sack
everybody, and leaping 4ft. in the air with
exeitement and superfluous energy and a burst
collar. But things grow worse instead of better,
and out in the country districts they have to tie
the Victorian train together with string and grass
nowadays. The same sort of thing happened
when Bent shouted and perspired in the same
billet twenty years ago. Thomas has enormous
energy but no luck.
The Liverpool Tubercle Farm.
As a consequence of an exposure by Dr. J. B.
Nash, M.L.C., of the revolting conditions under
which the consumptive poor were killed off in
Liverpool Asylum, a resolution was carried in
N.S.W. Leg. Council last year:
That, in the opinion of this House, the housing of con-
sumptives by the State is not in a satisfactory condition,
and that it is desirable, in the interests of the public
health, and for the benefit of the sufferers, that some
more adequate provision be made for their accommo-
dation and treatment.
Later the whole question of the hospital treatment
of consumptives was discussed at a meeting con-
vened by the medical profession in Sydney, and a
deputation to Premier See secured a promise that
a hospital for phthisis patients should be at once
built. Then the matter dropped. N.S.W. in the
financial year ended July, 1902, spent out of re-
venue, out of authorised loans, out of various
more or less regular appropriations from trust
funds, something like £18,000,000. But, when
last week, Dr. Nash ventured to remind the
Government of its promise in regard to the treat-
ment of destitute phthisis patients, Mr. Suttor
replied:—
There had been no change in the system of dealing
with consumptives, consequent on the resolution, and
they were still sent to the Liverpool Asylum. Nothing
more could be done until funds were available for the
erection of a new building.
So there being no funds, out of all the millions, to
wipe out this hideous brutality, the tubercle farm
remains. Sending consumptives to Liverpool
Asylum is simply murdering them. The
place is not an asylum; it is a hot-house
for the cultivation in human soil of the
tubercle bacillus, which kills its hosts in
from two to five years under the exceptionally
favorable circumstances provided for its ravages.
And with time the bacillus is becoming more
expert. The Liverpool tubercle-farm had an
average, to the end of 1900, of 67 deaths a year
out of an average of 165 inmates ! But in 1901
the bacillus scored 76 victims, 60 of whom were
under 50 years of age. For 1902, so far (to the
end of September), the deaths have been 53, and,
with the usual high rate in the summer months of
November and December, will probably reach
last year’s level. This callously brutal “ hospital”
kills its patients with absolute certainty; it
never has a cure. Though there is practically no
such thing as an incurable phthisis case, to the
modern medical mind, Liverpool Asylum regards
all its inmates as incurable, and kills them off at
the rate of forty per cent, per annum with mathe-
matical. precision. With this institution on its
hands, it is a surprising thing that the N.S.W.
Government should trouble to keep a hangman.
Capital offenders might, with as much certainty
of death, be condemned to a bed in “ The Liver-
pool Hospital for Indigent Consumptives.”
Within a year they would certainly be infected,
within five, or less, dead.

The Judge Parker Scandal. 4
The collapse of the Parker scandal, in Westralia,
is an interesting item in the history of yellow and
shrieky journalism. Mr. Parker had just been
appointed to the bench when a large, indefinable
scandal and reproach gathered round him. The
Spectator, a print of the loud and blithering
order, put the reproach into print—the substance
of it being that an article containing an exposure
of Judge Parker’s past career had been set up
for insertion in a Westralian Sunday paper, but
had been suppressed because the conscience-
stricken judge paid £lOO or £2OO hush-money to
keep his evil deeds out of print. After that, the
Parker scandal broke loose in Parliament, in the
press, in the streets, in public-houses, and raged
like a wild beast up and down Groperland. The
Spectator man was twice prosecuted, but, as is
generally the case, the jury disagreed; if a paper
is only sufficiently violent it almost invariably has
one friend at least on the jury, who, out of admir-
ation for its adjectives, won’t convict it under any
circumstances, or on any evidence. Then Judge
Parker secured a commission of inquiry, with
curious results. George, M.L.A., who first
brought the matter before Parliament, said he
knew nothing of the case himself, but Dr.
Taaffe had shown him a copy of the suppressed
article, and that one Charles had assured him,
in the doctor’s presence, that evidence in support
of Judge Parker’s misdeeds, and the evils and
dissipations of his past life, would be abundantly
forthcoming when wanted. George said he had
made no inquiry himself, and knew nothing
agamst Judge Parker. Dr. Taaffe said he
knew nothing, but that he had got a copy of the
suppressed article from Charles and had passed
it on. Charles denied that he had promised to
supply evidence against Judge Parker; he
had no evidence beyond common rumor.
Neither he nor Taaffe nor George, M.L.A.,
knew anything personally about Mr. Parker
having paid for the suppression of the
damaging article, or about the truth of any
damaging remarks in the article. The first
definite person that the commission got hold of
was Thomas Walker, of the Sunday paper, who
said the suppressed article had been written on
the strength of documents supplied by Charles,
and it was suppressed because of a dark suspicion
that the contents were one vast lie, and that the
whole business was being worked up in the
interests of a legal gentleman who had expected
to receive the judicial appointment which fell to
Mr. Parker. Also, Walker accused Charles of
having started the false rumor to the effect that
Judge Parker paid for the suppression of the
article in order to save his own small, brief
dignity. Charles, he said, had an interest in the
paper, and had become unduly inflated over it,
and had told people about the article “he”
was going to publish, and the way “he”
was going to blight Parker ; and rather
than admit that, after all, he had no
control over the paper, and that his article had
been thrown out by a higher authority, he pre-
ferred to represent that the paper in which he was
interested had taken a bribe from the Judge. Then
the man who took up the case in the Spectator
couldn’t remember where he got his information,
except that it was “ common report.” They were
a very indifferent set of witnesses, all except
Thomas Walker, who was definite enough fora
dozen, and who obviously regarded the whole affair
as a lie and a conspiracy, and an attempt to foist
imaginary information on his paper. The Com-
mission decided that Judge Parker was acquitted
without a stain on his character—in fact, it
couldn’t possibly decide any other way, consider-
ing the anxiety of everybody concerned to state
that he knew nothing personally, but had only
repeated something that was said by somebody,
who denied ever having said it at all.

Mr. Balaam Alison.
And Balak said unto Balaam, I called thee to curse
mine enemies, and, behold, thou hast altogether blessed
them these three times.—-Numbers xxiv.
The N.S.W. congress of employers'unions, etc.,
convened to curse Socialism, the Labor party, and
Labor legislation, was unpleasantly shocked last
week when Mr. W. Alison, secretary of the Tax-
payers’ Union, of N.S.W., rose to speak. No
Balak could ever have had more faith in a
Balaam than the assembled Tories had, that from
Mr. Alison’s lips would come curses rich and rare
with regard to the Arbitration Act, which was the
chosen subject of his speech. And Mr. Alison
seems to have tried to do his best and to damn
the Arbitration Acts in N.S.W. and Maoriland •
but finally a spirit of fair-play moved him and
Balaam spake:
Employers in the future would have rules laid down for
the conduct of their business and must abide by them
One alternative left to them was to shut up their business’
Of course, this was not the only view to take of the
position. Employers had the satisfaction of knowing that
once the conditions were fixed they would remain ner-
manent for a time long enough to make their business
arrangements for an extension of work or in taking con-
tracts. The Court had shown every desire to be perfectly
fair between the two parties. No complaints had arisen
that unjust rates of wages or absolutely ridiculous hours
of work had been awarded. It might be that an employer
would find in the more settled conditions and the absence
of the constant and harassing fear of strikes and changes,
some compensation for the great interference made by
the legal restrictions and regulations affecting his enter-
prise and industry. On the other hand, an employee
freed from the constant study of the best means of
compelling an employer to give him higher wages and
better conditions, might concentrate his energies in
giving the best care and attention to his employer’s
business.
Therein are stated the two great arguments in
favor of compulsory arbitration, the two chief
reasons Why The Bulletin urges the Federal
Parliament, if it can persuade the jealous
State Governments to hand over Labor questions
to its care, to undertake the responsibility of
establishing it as a system over all the Common-
wealth : (1) That it tends to fair and permanent
industrial relations ; (2) that it frees the employee
from the harassing necessity of constantly safe-
guarding his wages and hours from aggression,
and thus gives him the chance to do better work.
Mr. Alison seems to be much too honest a prophet
for a Kyabramapootric concern like the Taxpayers’
Union.

The Pampered Parmer.
The Freetraders’ fetish, as it bobs up again in
Launceston (Tas.) Examiner :
But the fanner in Victoria has been steadily sacrificed
for the city worker.’ The policy of the country has been
dictated by the Trades Hall, and those who sit at its
council board know little, and care less, about the require-
ments of the man upon the land. Yet when his harvest
fails the city man is the first to feel the pinch.
It would be interesting to know how much more
the Victorian farmer wants. He has had Protec-
tion, just as the town worker has. He got cheap
land, and in many cases he never paid for it. He
got irrigation works made for him, and refused to
pay for them, so the burden was thrown on the
general revenue, of which the town worker has to
provide half. He had unprofitable railways made
for him in all directions, and freights were cut
below cost, and the town worker helps to pay for
them. The town worker is taxed to help to make the
farmer’s roads and bridges. Then the farmer gets
cheap money through the State Land Bank. Hegets
the cheapest labor in the market, being exempted
from the Factories Act and the anti-sweating laws.
His starving stock is carried almost free, and fod-
der for his starving stock is carried at a great re-
duction. He gets rabbit-proof fencing on specially
low terms. He pays local rates of from 6d. to Is.
in the £, and the town worker, whose own rates
run up to 3s. 6d. in the £, is also taxed to make up
the municipal subsidy paid to the pampered
farmer because the latter hasn’t the courage
and the honesty to pay his way. He gets
an absurdly large share of political power;
the Vic. country districts have many more
members than they are entitled to on a
population basis, and the cow-and-butter-and-
wheat party runs politics. The Ministry consists
almost wholly of country members. And with all
this Victoria’s manufactures are worth nearly
£4,000,000 a year more to the country than the
pampered agricultural industry, and are worth a
great deal more than the agricultural and dairying
industry put together, and are worth almost as
much as the agricultural and pastoral business
combined. This enormous subsidising of the
farmer has loaded Victoria with debt (and other
States as well) and it will have to end soon
through the public credit giving out.

The Sacredness of the Jury-Verdict.
A few weeks ago the Sydney daily papers were
almost unanimous in howling for the fall of the
See Ministry because, in a criminal trial, the jury
having said “guilty” and the Judge “not guilty,”
the Attorney-General granted a petition for the
release of the man convicted. The extreme sacred-
ness of a jury’s verdict was strongly impressed by
the Daily Telegraph, the Sydney Morning
Herald and the Evening News, and it was
almost argued that, even if the verdict were
wrong and inflicting punishment on an inno-
cent man, it should be respected. Those who
were not so blinded by party prejudice as to
fail to see that it is in defiance of all British law
traditions to keep a man in gaol against the con-
science of the Judge who tried him, did not join
with the daily papers in this extreme view; but
they have since been waiting patiently, though so
far without avail, for these journals to say some-
thing decisive in regard to the case of Mrs.
Sangal. That woman was found guilty by the
jury trying her, “with a recommendation to
mercy”—that is to say, the jury concluded that
she was a party to a murder, that she should be
punished, but that she should not be hanged.
The Victorian Executive proposes to set aside
part of the jury’s verdict —an essential part—and
hang the (now-pregnant) woman under circum-
stances of unspeakable brutality. Yet there has
been no whisper of protest from the papers which
became most excited over the “ insult to the jury ”
in the Friedman case. There are no party politics
to be served in this case ; and if Mrs. Sangal is
hanged, there will be a rich harvest of pence from
retailing again the story of her crime.

Lewis’ Legirons.
Some Tasmanian legislators, evidently smarting
under newspaper criticism, have expressed dis-
satisfaction with the Newspaper Act of the Island
as it relates to damages for libel, and Premier Lewis
promises to consider the advisability of placing an
altogether new Newspaper Act on the Statute
Book of Van Dobson Land—though he may have
forgotten all about it by now. The old Statute,
in true Van Dobson style, starts out by treating
the proprietor of a newspaper as a criminal who
must be closely watched and put down in the
interests of law and order, immediately he
offends the Sacred Cow of Authority. Any
other person may print and circulate libellous
matter without rendering himself amenable to
anything but the Common Law, but the
man who arouses the just suspicion of the Van
Dobson party by owning a newspaper is com-
pelled to find £l6OO worth of securities before he
can print his paper at all. The £l6OO bond is os-
tensibly a guarantee that the paper will not
publish blasphemous or seditious matter, but is
really a sort of guarantee that a libelled personage
shall be able to collect his verdict if successful in a
libel suit. Just why a man who obtains a verdict
against a newspaper should be placed in a more
advantageous position as regards damages than a
man who gets a verdict against a printer of books
and pamphlets, or a tradesman, is a mystery.
Also, why a man who prints a newspaper should
be compelled to enter into a money-bond that he
won t libel anyone, when a man who lays bricks,
or a Dobson who drives about in a gilded chariot,
finds no security that he won’t drop bricks on any-
body or drive over anybody, is as big, if not a
bigger, mystery. But it is the law, as it survives
in the land of Dobson, and as it, till recently,
survived in some other States that were un.
afflicted with Dobson. But, despite its age,
origin and breeding, and notwithstanding its
severity, the finding of bondsmen to the tune of
£l6OO does not work as well as persons in
authority in Tasmania could wish. The bonds-
men may be financially good enough for £l6OO on
the day of registration, but in six months or less
they may be stone-broke. If a successful
litigant in a libel-suit comes in at this juncture,
and the printing-press and the editorial paste-
pot fail to satisfy the verdict, the successful
litigant may be grievously disappointed to find
that the bond of £l6OO isn’t worth tuppence.
Naturally enough the successful litigant doesn’t,
under these circumstancs, consider himself
so successful as he ought to be, and howls
accordingly. It is the echo of that howl that now
prompts Lewis, Premier and Attorney-General
of Tasmania, to think about making the law more
stringently effective as a leg-iron for the criminal
who runs a newspaper and criticises “Us” ad-
versely. So it has been seriously suggested in
Dobson’s isle to make it compulsory for every
newspaper proprietor to deposit with the
Government something like £lOOO in hard
cash, and to empower the authorities to
hand the money to any aggrieved person
who may get a verdict for damages against the
newspaper. As the locking up of £lOOO, where it
cannot be used as business capital, would practi-
cally mean the closing up of all but the wealthiest
newspapers, the “Freedom of the Press” looks
like being considerably curtailed in Tasmania.
