“ Kiwi” on trades-union law :
I fully endorse all you say (8., 4/10/’O2) as to the far-
reaching consequences of the decision in the Taff Yale
case to trades-unionism in Biitain. But it does not seem
generally known that the soundness of the decision is
strongly questioned by some of the greatest lawyers, in-
cluding Sir F. Pollock. The Taff Vale decision is based
on the principle that anyone “seducing”—that is the
term generally used—one of the parties to a contract to
break his bargain commits an actionable wrong. The
first case in point occurred in London a few years back,
when the owner of a theatre induced a lady singer, who
had contracted to sing at a rival theatre, to break her
contract. The injured party sued the “seducer” and
recovered damages. This was the celebrated case
Lu.mley v. Gye, and it took the lawyer world by surprise.
But it was upheld, nevertheless, by the House of
Lords, and it has since become a settled principle
with all the leading law writers (save Pollock) that
while a contract primarily affects only the legal status of
the parties thereto, still there is an obligation on every-
one to refrain from interfering therewith. One pungent
critic pointed out in an English paper some time ago
that, since a promise of marriage is a contract in law,
the kind friend who advises a giddy girl to jilt an unsuit-
able lover is liable to damages, and such is certainly one
of the consequences of this remarkable decision. The
learned Judges in the Taff Yale case looked on the rich
company and the grimy laborers as contractors, and the
trades-unionists who tried to persuade the miners to stand
up for their rights by a strike, as “seducers,” who can be
restrained by injunction, and landed in damages. It is a
scandalous stretch of judicial whimsicality to call workmen
who have, perforoe of circumstances, to take the first job
that presents itself “contractors,” for one of the first
essentials to a valid contract is that the parties thereto
should be free agents. But such a judicial one-eyedness
is not new. You could not find anywhere a more
atrocious doctrine than that of “common employment”;
which meant that a workman couldn’t recover compen-
sation for injuries received through the negligence of a
fellow-worker, because it was a risk which he had “in
contemplation” when he agreed to work along with
mates. This shameful principle has been practically
killed by statutory provision, and the Taff Yale decision
may be remedied by an Act of Parliament.
♦
Federal Treasurer Turner, recently address-
ing a Brighton (Vic.) audience on a sociable
occasion, urged the public not to be down-hearted
—“ there had been a heavier deficit before; in
fact, he had seen a deficit of over a million, and
yet they had pulled through, and they would pull
through again.” Exactly the same remark has
been heard from all the States, and in most cases
the remark is bosh. Victoria didn’t pull through;
it owes that old deficit yet, and now another big
one has come on top of it. N.S.W. didn’t pull
through; since 1886 nine successive Treasurers
have saved the country, and the deficit is a vast
amount larger now than it was in 1886. Queens-
land and S.A. pulled through by adding the
shortage to the public debt, and then starting new
shortages. As a rule, an Australian State has six
deficits, running to anything from £500,000 to
£3,000,000; then it has one surplus of £lB and,
with all the old liability still owing, everybody
shakes hands and says the country has “pulled
through.”

S.M. Herald— maundering about Chamberlain’s
projected S’African trip :
It is not likely that the verdict of the war would be
anyway changed .... but it is quite apparent that any-
thing of the sort could hardly fail to increase the
difficulties of Government and administration, and delay
the satisfactory adjustment of the problem left by the
war.
What is the verdict of the war ? It would puzzle
the Herald to say. As yet it is by no means
certain how the case will finally be settled.
Granny goes on :
The Imperial policy in S’Africa is so to administer
affairs there as not to encourage the formation of another
Imperial sore. That is the problem in a nutshell.
It is—and Chamberlain thinks he can crack that
nut by making a sort of royal progress through
S’Africa, with Milner as buttoner, or barracker.
How the scheme will work will depend on the
chapter of accidents. It is quite on the cards that
an inveterate schemer like Chamberlain may
take it into his head to upset the “ verdict of the
war,” and pose as a “Pro-Boer.” There are
difficulties to be faced by the S’African Govern-
ment, the responsibility for which Milner is
doubtless very ready to shift on to Chamberlain’s
shoulders. There is little credit to be got in
administration, with such a jumble of contending
factions, and he is a lucky man who can escape
responsibility for it. That seems to ‘be Milner’s
idea, and Chamberlain comes in handy. But
whether Joseph will upset the “verdict of the
war ” or lose what character he has left in trying
to maintain it, is a question which only time will
answer.
*
It would almost melt the heart of a stone—the
“Protest against the attempt to establish a new
dynamite monopoly in S’Africa,” which reached
Australia by a recent mail. The protest is by cer-
tain British manufacturers against “the wealthy
and powerful influences at present directed in an
inimical spirit against English trade.” It sets
forth that “ the old dynamite monopoly in the
Transvaal, if not directly the cause of the war,
was one of the chief grievances which led up to
it,” and now—
It is not difficult to arrive at the conclusion that an
attempt is being made to secure protection for a new ring
of S’African manufacturers of explosives, to be composed
of the proprietors of the Modderfontein factory, other-
wise the Nobel Dynamite Trust, and the De Beers Com-
pany.
In view of this, the Protest sobs :
One is tempted to ask whether the British manufac-
turer patiently and cheerfully bore the taxation necessary
to meet the heavy expenses of the war, simply to find
himself excluded from the trade of the new colonies in
the interests of a ring of foreign and South African
capitalists ?
Of course, “one is tempted to ask ” ; and then one
will ask; and that will be all the satisfaction! one

*
“ The Derby Winner,” staged on Saturday
at Sydney Lyceum, is a bright, ridiculous drama
of high life and low conduct, with a considerable
amount of dancing and divorce in it, not to
mention ah erratic duchess. The dialogue and
humor are above the average, so that one forgives
Mary, the housemaid, for coming into the gilded
drawing room of Brackenhurst Hall in order to
Say good-bye to all the earls and countesses therein
assembled. Douglas, Earl of Desborough, has a
little chat with Mary before the assembly, and
Mary’s homely father, front-named Joe, tells the
countesses that his daughter is as good as any
society lady in the land, which, as Duchess Mrs.
Bland Holt remarks, is not saying much. Baker,
as the blue-blooded Douglas, is more than usually
imbecile, with something of the prig thrown
in. He discharges from his employ the suspected
cause of Mary’s blighted character, and later on
is himself suspected of the blight. He attempts
to hustle away Harrie Ireland, an adventuress
whose presence is an “insult to the Countess,” for
reasons connected with his own Past. And
therewith he becomes suspected of more Past.
On the authority of a doctored letter and the
word of the wicked Major, Countess Muriel de-
nounces her husband and the adventuress in an
unnecessarily public manner, then rushes off in
her ball-dress with Major Mostyn, who is to be a
brother to her. The cold world, not understand-
ing brothers who are thus hurriedly adopted in
evening-dress, divorces Muriel; and her little son,
a large-small boy, played by Miss Ada Ingersole,
is taken away from her, and there is generally
much anguish. Having lost his wife, Baker
nearly loses his horses, and is about re-
duced to bankruptcy, when, as usual, he
wins the Derby, this end being attained by
Mary’s jockey sweetheart riding Baker’s horse at
the last moment through hearing that it was the
Major, not the Earl, who blighted Mary. That
arrant damsel is then called forward among the
assembled peers and sundries, and has her lost
character mentioned publicly, but this she does
not seem to mind.
* * • *
Regarding the acting, Mr. Baker and Miss
Ross are sufficiently unlike genuine specimens of
the aristocracy to make a favorable impression on
the public. Miss Ross is particularly successful.
Mrs. Bland Holt is also in her element as an up-to-
date duchess, who says “What price bed!” as a hint
for her illustrious company to retire; while Bland
does well as a retired army surgeon, whom three
or four nice women, for no particular reason, want
to marry. Villain Albert Norman is mainly
occupied in getting innocent people into the
Divorce Court, and in playing into the hands of
Harrie Ireland, a rather rowdier adventuress than
usual.
** * *
“ Cyrano de Bergerac” withdrew his nose
from public view at Sydney Palace last week.
The poetic gentleman was a victim to various cir-
cumstances, including the fact that Sydney, from
a long course of alleged musical comedy such as
“The Chinese Honeymoon” and “The Messen-
ger Boy ” and of gory yellow-drama, has lost its
taste for any play with a literary flavor (the
Broughs found this out, too). On the opening
night many were present who knew Henry Lee
only as part of a variety company, and expected
him to cake-walk; and who knew Manager
Anderson only as providing extra-lurid murder
and wicked - woman plays; and who felt
as happy on seeing “Cyrano” as a coal-
lumper straying for a good feed into a French
eating-house. They became restless on the first
night. Then a battle scene with 15 varied death-
contortions come along, and whetted their appe-
tite for gore, and raised their expectations. When
the last act opened in a convent close, the gallery
waited eagerly for a great fire scene, or for a
steam-roller to come on the stage. And when
nothing of that sort happened, they “guyed,”
and “Cyrano,” doomed ever to a futile career, was
done for so far as Sydney was concerned.
* * * *
Last Saturday the gallery was gratified to
find the Palace stage given up to something
understandable. “The Worst Woman in London ”
is not quite so weirdly improbable as the title
suggests, and is relieved by one absolutely real bit
of life—the drunken old father of the worst woman,
capitally acted by George Carey. The cast is
strong throughout. Miss Duggan plays the
name part with a laudable restraint, Mr. Diver
is an entirely natural and artistic scoun-
drel, and Mr. Frank Crossley and Miss
Leighford do good work. If a people’s plays
reflect a people’s character, ‘ ‘ The Worst Woman
in London ” shows a marked falling off in the
chivalry of the patrons of melodrama. At one
time the most popular “gag” of such a piece was
to the effect that “ the ir,an who laid his hand on
a woman except by way of kindness didn’t deserve
the name of Englishman.” The “W.W.” relies
for some of its comedy on a couple of policemen
arresting a woman vagrant by lifting her off her
feet and carrying her violently about the stage ;
and the W.W. herself is subjected to frequent
violence and finally murdered by a madman to
loud applause, and then gets a condemnatory
obituary from the “hero” to more applause.
*# * *
For a very few nights more Sherlock
Holmes will smash the lamp in the Professor’s
den of infamy, and depart while the myrmidons
rush headlong at the hot end of his cigar on the
mantelpiece. The next production at Sydney
Royal will be “ If I were King”—the striking
title of which should, in itself, command atten-
tion. The author, when he invented that head-
line, certainly opened up a large subject. Almost
every man has wondered what he would do if he
were king. Many have also wondered what they
would do if they were dead. Nobody that this
paper knows has wondered what he would do if
he were the devil, though that opens up about
the largest and weirdest problem of all. The
writer’s conviction is that if he were the devil he
would irrigate his territory and open the window
at the top to let in fresh air.
** * *
Sheridan’s “ Christopher Columbus ” goes
off Sydney Criterion stage this week, and until
his pantomime is ready, Sheridan intends to
afflict the country with a musical version of
“Uncle Tom’s Cabin,” for which he may possibly
be forgiven in the great Hereafter.
** * *
Sydney Tivoli greeted the holiday audience
with a very much improved programme—the ever-
freen Nawns (the best sketch people this theatre
as seen), Sandow, the Harbecks with their grace-
ful juggling, Mark Anthony, back with a better
budget of songs than before, and many other old
favorites. In addition, there was “Professor
Maccann, the Concertina King,” a stout monarch
who makes that generally despised instrument
vocal with all kinds of music.
** * *
There was a suspicion of wearisomeness
about the first series of Melba concerts as soon as
the excitement of novelty had subsided, but the
fact that the Star shone brightest (in the Mad
Scene from “Hamlet”) on the third and fifth occa-
sions prevented the suspicion from deepening into
certainty. The Cup-week concert was a little bit
wearisome, without a doubt. People arrived not
later than a quarter to eight o’clock, most of them,
and they were all desired by the management to
stay in their seats until the vice-regal personages
had left the hall. Well, the vice-regality didn’t
drop into its extra-special, highly-upholstered
chairs until 8.30 or so, consequently the common
or guinea variety of audience had to roost upon
crowded cane furniture for three hours, or longer,
in some cases. Apart from the contribu-
tions of Madame Melba, the programme
was not interesting. The orchestra, under
Slapoffski—a satisfactory “scratch” orchestra —
played the overture to “ Mignon ” in an unviva-
cious spirit, also a rather indigestible prelude by
Humperdinck, and Corven’s “Language of
Flowers,” which latter was so slight and fragile
that it nearly got an encore. Signor Bensaude
gave a feeble rendering of the same old “Pagliacci ”
prologue—any one of half-a-dozen local baritones
could have done it quite as effectively. The little
harpist played a “solo” with orchestra, and was
overwhelmed by accompaniment. Louis Arens
had one air to sing. When he had sung it as
pleasingly as his voice permitted, Arens made
himself ridiculous by giving as an encore Lohen-
grin’s “Farewell to the Swan,” entirely on his
own. Walked up to the piano, struck his key-
note, piped the first half of his Wagnerian
excerpt, struck another note to reassure himself,
and completed his exhibition of vanity. Melba
sang, in her second-best voice, a hymn-like
aria by Mozart, the gay “Sevillana” of
Massenet, and Elsa’s Dream from “Lohengrin.”
She was charming, of course, but the orchestra
didn’t increase the effect. Melba last week was
comparatively disappointing. One would rather
have heard her repeat her favorite opera airs and
Handel’s “ Sweet Bird ” to the same light accom-
paniments as before. And one wants a quicker
and slicker entertainment anyhow. These wire-
drawn concerts have been a great advertisement
for Dolores, whose recitals, assisted by pianist
Newell, are all prizes and no blanks.
** * *
“The Sorrows of Satan,” alleviated by
40 minutes of interval between the acts, appears
to bo doing not too badly at Melbourne Princess’s.
It is an absurd drama, but it gets some serious
applause from persons whose sense of humor has
been sadly neglected, and who accept the preten-
tious twaddle of Marie Corelli’s hero as food for
their immortal souls. The programme doesn’t
mention the name of the fiend responsible for
this theatrical structure, but it credits the founda-
tion theory to Sala’s story of “ Margaret Forster,”
and “Marie Corelli’s famous novel.” Margaret
Forster, whatever she may have been
in the long - forgotten story, is an impossible
spinster in the play. She abides with her
unsober she-male mother and her artless illegi-
timate offspring in a dirty place of residence,
where the attic is the best room in the house.
Mother (played by Alfred Tapping) bemoans her
luck, like the ordinary pantomime man in petti-
coats. She has no visible means of subsistence,
save the lodger who rents the attic, and he, being
a journalist, doesn’t pay his weekly three-and-
sixpence. Daughter Margaret has added to the
family troubles for the past five years by assisting
the insolvent lodger. She was a sinner aforetime,
but is living with mother now. Mother “sold
her” to a pompous worldling named Grafton
Lyle, to whom she, the gentle Margaret, is
indebted for a child of uncertain age, and the
child has, by the mysterious agency of Satan,
become heiress to a fortune of £BO,OOO, which
will fall due when she is 21. Satan seems to
have tied up the mysterious estate in a mystic
manner. If the infant daughter .of Shame dies
before she is 21 her wicked illegitimate father is
to get the money. The parental Grafton has
business instinct strongly developed. In the
third or fourth act he connives with old Mother
Tapping to drown the poor little ragged heiress
in the river, but when the deed is done Satan
prompts Mother Tapping to turn King’s evidence.
This by the way.
* * * *
The attic journalist doesn’t at first love
Margaret as he should. A penitent spinster with
a daughter of her own and a deplorable mother to
nag at her ought to have matrimonial attractions
for any hungry member of the press, yet Paul
Carruthers loses his heart to a Lady Delila. Lady
D. and her mama, the Countess, preceded by the
evil Grafton who bought Margaret, come toiling
up the stairs to the attic in order to give the
journalist a job. They commission him to write
a novel for £l5, and send it to Lady Delila’s
publishers. She wishes to shine as an authoress,
so she orders the young man to make the book
brilliant, but she doesn’t leave him any of the £l5
on account. Then he decides to take poison for
love of Delila, and this is the cue for a sudden
appearance of Satan. Satan presents Paul, the
journalist, with' two million pounds sterling,
and—but why dilate on what at present is
merely nonsense without a tableau ? Nor are
the Musgrove co. the right people to make a
drama of this sort look less foolish than it is.
None of the parts are played with any picturesque
force and passion to speak of. Harcourt Beatty,
as Satan, is the principal figure, but by no means
the principal failure. The names of the principal
failures are withheld from publication.

mat it contains sufficient aconite to hang his

The Bijou advt., which threatened a sud-
den cessation of “In the Soup” some time ago,
was merely a misprint and a delusion. Hawtrey
and Co. have carried their Soup comedy into a
third week, and a tired populace approves of the

at * at at
Pagel, the Strong Man from up-country,
came to town at Fitzgeralds’ Circus last Saturday.
Pagel is a fair-haired young fellow with whom no
self-respecting pressman would care to work up a
heated discussion. A blow from Panel’s arm is
guaranteed to change the political views of the
wisest legislator as soon as he recovers conscious-
ness, and a kick from either of Pagel’s supporting
pillars would cure every known disease if applied
to the patient’s stummick. The younger Hercules
differs from Sandow in all his muscular
proportions, probably. He strikes one as
having grown strong from his feet upwards.
His shoulders are only beginning to be worthy of
his thighs. In fact, Pagel is a comparatively
infant prodigy. At present his most remarkable
feats are walking up a ladder—or, rather, two
ladders—with a full-sized horse dangling from his
waist, carrying a pony and child-rider around the
arena, and lifting a youthful, %>ut substantial, lion
high above his head, even as a father raiseth his
offspring on processional occasions. _ These are
the little things that make Pagel an interesting
figure just now. Saturday’s audience saw no
reason to question his claim to the title of “Un-
rivalled,” but if Professor Miller or gigantic Jim
Kelly would care to dispute it, they can come
along and toy with the lion any evening until
further notice.

Next Saturday the delightful Dolores will
begin a farewell series of song recitals at Melb.
Town Hall, and all previous admirers of that
accomplished artist are rejoicing in the news.
Also it appears from the recent Dolores furore in
Sydney that Mdlle.’s admirers have been sound-
ing her praises with a new enthusiasm. People
who never troubled to hear her of yore are deter-
mined to hear her this trip, and the name of
Dolores looms larger than ever.

Melba's looked-for appearances in grand
opera will happen at Melbourne Princess’s some-
what earlier than was expected. Saturdays
Nov. 22nd and 29th, and the intervening
Wednesday, 26th, are the dates fixed, and mana-
ger Musgrove commits himself to the “positive
statement ” that those three dates are all he has
to offer. Three different operas or “ mixed
bills ” (the advt. gives no names) are being pre-
pared for the three nights. The price for first
choice of reserved seats is 3 guineas, second choice
costs 2 guineas, after which the humble candidate
with 1 guinea aspirations will get his chance to be
present. The person who doesn’t yearn to be
present when Melba sings in opera is socially non-
existent. He or she may be regarded as quite
dead, and of no importance.

The Adelaide Royal and Tivoli have both
been unoccupied ; the first time that such a thing
has happened to both together since F. H. Pollock
had control of the one and Rickards of the other.
This was the wily amateur’s opportunity, and one
co. of him played Gilbert’s “Broken Hearts,”
having the nerve to rechristen it ‘ ‘ The Island of
Love,” and suppress the author’s name ! Another
crowd, greatly daring, took on “Much Ado about
Nothing ” at the Ro> al on the Birthday holiday,
which also saw a farcical effusion, very fittingly
named “ Mixed,” tried at the Tivoli.

This can’t be described as an extra-cheerful set of
figures. In most cases the trouble arises through the
steady reduction in the customs receipts. This is a
matter which could have been easily foreseen, and
which will grow worse instead of better. When the
supply of foreign loans falls off, Australia will need to cut
about another £7,000,000 off its imports in order that
exports may so far exceed imports that the difference
will cover interest on the foreign debt. This in itself
will greatly reduce the customs revenue. Then the loss
of border customs and the abolition of the tea and kero-
sene duties are all so much less taxes contributed by the
people, and the loss of these items, and the refusal of
most of the State Parliaments to impose sufficient new
taxes to make up the shortage, are making great havoc
of the finances.

If all the States would publish complete monthly accounts
of revenue, expenditure, and loan expenditure, and
would do it with the same promptness that N.S.W. and
Queensland show, it would be a blessing in a statistical
sense. When a huge, straggling State like Queensland
can get out its complete figures under all these headings
two days after the month closes, it is absurd to suppose
that other States can’t do likewise. The policy of Vic-
toria and Tasmania in refusing any information, and the
straggling, belated quarterly accounts of SA., which often
aren’t complete till weeks after date, are archaic devices.
The Treasurer was smarter than that in Babylon.

How N.S.W. and Queensland are likely to pan out at the
end of the financial year, next June, is a matter for much
speculation. They both closed last year with a deficit ;
and Queensland is £180,170 worse off, and N.S.W.
£415,411 worse off at the end of the first four months of
the new financial year than they were in 1901, Also,
they have both more interest to pay than they had last
year. N.S.W., with £707,004 more expenditure than
revenue in the short space of four months, presents a
wonderful prospect.
Loan expenditure is known only as regards two

Again New South Wales shows up badly.
Treasurer Shiels, of Victoria, has issued another
lugubrious forecast re the next deficit—his fifth or sixth
forecast, and each of them more lugubrious than its pre-
decessor. Shiels how talks of a possible shortage of
£1,032,000. Seeing that the revenue has, so far, fallen by
only £45,212, and Shiels talks of nearly £600,000 more
deficit than there was last year, that long and miserable
person must intend to spend a heap of money in the
course of his retrenchment.

Complaints of stagnation in the engineering
trade are as rife as ever, but, judging by the large stock
of Refrigerating and Ice-making machinery now at the
engineering works of Mephan Ferguson, Melbourne, he
must expect to do a large business in these machines
during the coming summer season. The prices for all
sizes have been greatly reduced, and purchasers are
invited to inspect and test these Refrigerators before
purchasing. This firm has also introduced up-to-date
machinery for manufacturing bolts and nuts, rivett,. Jog
spikes and every discription of ironwork.*

The best investment in Australia is buying land
at Esperance, Western Australia, the natural port of the
Kalgoorlie goldfields. With a railway, Esperance will
become one of our busiest ports, as the trade with the
goldfields will pass through it. A permanent survey is
now being made. The railway is a certainty. Get in
early and make a fortune by purchasing a few blocks
before the great rush sets in. Price, £2O block; terms,
£1 a month. Write for plans and particulars to The
Perth Land Company, 101 Swanston-sbreet, Melbourne.*
• • * *
Many doubtful claims have been advanced on
behalf of duplex pumps. Some makers state that their
duplex pumps are “ double the capacity of single pumps
of same diameter and stroke.” An A. S. Cameron single-
acting pump can be run safely and comfortably at a speed
that would knock a duplex pump to pieces. But even at
a low speed, duplex pumps seldom attain even their
theoretical capacities, and the reason for this can be
readily explained. Apply to the Agents, Messrs. W. and
J. Lempriere, 506 Little Collins Street, Melbourne, for
further particulars. The A. S. Cameron pumps are noted
for their simplicity, durability, and absence of outside
valve gear.*
* » • *
New patents of great importance have lately
been granted to the Wilfley Ore Concentrator Syndicate
Limited, and all Wilfley tables after this date will embody
same. The Austral Otis Company of South Melbourne,
who are the sole Australasian manufacturers, are now
building the new pattern, which are considerably
stronger than the old pattern. Improvements have
been made in the head motion, which is stronger and
simpler, the working table is now mounted on an
intermediate frame, with wedge adjustments at either
side so arranged that the movement of the adjusting
lever raises one side and lowers the other at the same
time. The table top can now either be laid quite flat or
inclined at a very considerable angle. Futher improve-
ments have been made in the feed distributing box and
wash water launder, and also in the middlings launder
and elevator wheel; these improvements increase the
capacity of the table, and the concentrating work is
more efficient.*

From Yodda Valley, B.N. Guinea, Sept. 26:
Interesting record for Yodda goldfield for the last eight
days—one natural death, two suicides, and 12 departures.
Population now dwindled to 45 or 46. Outside natives
(“ Bishop’s lambs ”) looting miners’ camps daily. Must
be a burst-up shortly among natives or miners—which ?
Govt, still sitting tight in its “ masterly inactivity.”
Why doesn’t the Commonwealth put the retrenchment
idea into force in this benighted land ? Something would
be done for the country then. The place is used at
present merely as a dumping-ground for favored indivi-
duals whose only qualification is influence.

Apropos Labor-member Griffith’s Bill in N.S.W.
Assembly to authorise the Savings Bank trustees to lend
on first mortgage. Of £3,942,032 at the disposal of the
S.A. Savings Bank at the end of its last year, £1,359,350
was out on mortgage. The bank started to lend in ’4B ;
for several years it got 10 per cent., but now the rate is
down to 4. The bank, which has a reserve fund of
£121,000, is remarkably popular; it has no less than
111,537 depositors in a total population of 364,000, as
against N.S. Wales’ 306,311 in a total of 1,379,700. Its
record, in the teeth of S. Australia’s gloomy history, is
all in favor of Griffith’s bill.
The decadence of the port of London is
marked. The P. and O. Co. will shortly abandon
London as a home port. At present the co. is
having four vessels built on the Clyde, and two at
Belfast, which will be unable to enter the Royal
Albert dock. This will mean a loss to London
of the despatch of 100 vessels a year. Twenty
years ago the P. and O. Co. left Southampton in
favor of London, owing to inadequacy of wharfage
facilities, but since then Southampton has pro-
gressed and London has stood still. Now, the co.
will go back to Southampton, where the docking
facilities are unequalled, and the dues 50 per cent,
less. The Thames will lose other steamship ter-
minal advantages as soon as existing contracts
expire.

With this co., like many other ruins of the boom, as the
concern grows smaller, and the assets less, and the
deposits are paid off, the business becomes too diminu-
tive to justify office expenses, and the gas bill and taxes
and sundries eat up the income. If a dozen or two of
the liquidating concerns in either Sydney or Melbourne
could agree to share an office and a manager and a gas
bill among them, it would be a great economy. When
things get down so far that it costs £1062 in office charges
to earn £509 net income, liquidation is a costly business.

During the last seven years Australasia has
carried its load of debt with comparative ease—by bor-
rowing and spending on public works almost sufficient
money to pay the interest. It is always easy to pay the
interest when the money to pay it with is borrowed.
There are degrees in profligacy, however, and the follow-
ing table shows, as nearly as possible, to what extent the
various States met their obligations, or didn’t meet them,
out of their own resources :
Seven Years— lB96 to 1902 Inclusive.
Interest, &c.,
Interest and Loan paid out
Sinking Fund Expendi- of State’s
paid. ture. own
Victoria .. £13,426,624 £4,596,931 £8,829,693
S.A 6,925,408 3,387,287 3,538,121
Queensland.. 9,436,191 7,288,729 2,147,462
Maoriland .. 12,220,074 10,948,792 1,271,282
Tasmania .. 2,247,467 1,316,590 930,877

Westralia .. 2,719,237 10,108,655 7,389,418
£64,024,214 £53,940,860 £10,083,354
These figures (compiled from Ooghlan’s “ Statistics of the
Six States of Australia and Maoriland ”) show that only
£10,083,354, out of £64,024,214 due on interest and sinking
fund during the last seven years, was paid out of the
country’s own resources. When the borrowing boom
collapses, the seven States must find nearly £8,000,000
more per annum than they have been doing of late —
must export £8,000,000 worth more, which means an
immense amount more work ; or must import £8,000,000
worth less, which means a great deal less luxury.
Victoria paid more of its liabilities out of its
own earnings than all the other States put together ;
while, among the six States which paid anything at all,
N.S.W. paid the least. Of course, Victoria has had a
rather hard time—the small State which pays £8,829,693
can’t be reasonably expected to feel so affluent as the
big one, which pays only £755,337 and owes the rest.
Any semblance of prosperity which has been created in
N.S.W. is almost wholly through postponing payment of
the interest-bill (for borrowing the money to pay it with is
postponing it), though it is cheerfully set down by the
Reids and M'Millans, and Joe Cooks and Smiths (Bruce
and Pumpkin), to the beneficent effects left behind by
the State’s alleged Freetrade policy. The course of Aus-
tralia’s alleged prosperity can almost be traced in these
figures. The greatest increase of population is in the
States which borrowed most money with which to supply
temporary artificial employment for their people, and
prevent them going elsewhere ; the smallest increase was
where there was least artificial employment. The biggest
boom and the biggest borrowing went together—the boom
was where the people paid least of their liabilities by hard
work. When the supply of loans gives out, two, at least,
of the States—Westralia and N.S.W.—have an awfnl
distance to fall, and a pretty hard place to fall on.
• * • *
Premier Barton explains that while in England
he engaged an actuary to report on the question of
federalising the State debts. His report isn’t expected
for some months. It is a pity Mr. Barton doesn’t explain
what use an English actuary can be in such a case. The
only thing that he can report on is the possible conver-
sion of the debts—conversion being, as a rule, a process
by which the amount of the debt is increased in order to
secure a reduction of interest, and that means that,
indirectly, a certain portion of the interest is added to
the total of the debt. It is, in almost every case, another
version of the swindle of passing the bill on to posterity.
The simple, honest process requires no elaborate
actuarial report. That is:
To take over the State debts as they fall due.
To convert them, as they become due, into 3 per cent.
Federal stock fixed for, say, 25 years, and repayable
thereafter at the option of the Commonwealth.
To establish a substantial sinking fund for the repay-
ment of the Federal debt thus created, this being made
part of the bargain with the stock holders.
To make it a further condition that, except in case of
war, the Federal Government will incur no new debt
until the liability created by the taking over of the State
debts is paid off. That would make them a perpetual
first charge on the Federal revenue, and on that basis
they could be converted as they fall due at a, very cheap
rate.
Australia doc ;’*• require just now any expensive
actuary to devise a new financial fake. All it needs is a
fixed resolve to be honest, courageous and energetic, and
to pay its way for the future out of its own earnings.

The long-continued drought has hit the Sydney
softgoods houses badly. But the prevailing stagnation is
ascribed to Federation and the tariff, for your calico man
tries his hardest to believe that things are all right in the
country ; neither he nor his principals in England want to
believe anything else. At this time last year the ware-
houses were inordinately busy disposing of goods that
had been housed prior to the tariff, and in a great num-
ber of cases these “free lines” were sold with the duty
added—sometimes with the duty added two or three
times over. Big turnovers at that time, and the continued
drought since, have enormously curtailed the volume of
this year’s trade, and now retrenchment is the order of
the day. One warehouse has recalled two travellers (each
traveller with his railway-fares, expenses, freights, salary
and commission costs his firm £lOOO a year) and dismissed
several indoor hands. Another house has made a big cut
on all the high salaries, while from a third a departmental
head has been despatched post haste to Maoriland to try
and dispose of heavy surplus stock.

Broken Hill: B.H. Prop., straining to keep up
its half-yearly div., is sending record quantities of stuff
to the smelters, and its pay-sheet was never heavier.
The South has started its new shaft at last. By a strange
coincidence, a decline in the value of ore sent to the mill
is announced at the same time—a gentle hint to the eon-
tribs., perhaps, that the directors still look to them to pay
the bill. But, in spite of the poorness of its ore for
silver, the South is a great mine. Two years
ago lead was £l7 15s. ; now it is anything between
£lO 12s. 6d. and £lO 17s. 6d. But spelter, which
was £lB 17s. two years ago, is steady at £l9 6s. 3d.
now. This, the lowering of freights, and a fair amount of
success in experimenting with magnetic concentration at
the German zinc works, have led to the shipping of
another plant to Australia, and a start may be made again
at zinc-saving about April next. For the present the
mining cos. themselves are doing nothing with their
“ zincs,” though it is said the Sulphide Corporation has
plans for the future. These German metallurgists, by
the way, deserve success. Apart from continuous ex-
periments in Europe they have kept their works and a
staff going on the spot for nearly three years.— —
Miners’ member Estell has a Bill before N.S.W. Parliament,
probably suggested by a recent incident, and calculated
to make the mine-manager who “ hasn’t had no book-
learning” squirm. Under the Coal Mines Regulation
Act, a manager or an under-manager must have either a
service or an examination certificate. Estell proposes
that within three years the man with the service paper
shall be required to also have one of the other sort.
A little, rare black opal was got at White Cliffs (N.S.W.)
lately. Good specimens are worth from 10 to 15 times
their weight in gold.
• * * »
Work has been suspended at the Sydney Harbor
Collieries, Balmain, pending the raising of additional
capital. £115,000 has been spent, and it is estimated
that £85,000 more will be required. It is assumed that
there will be no difficulty on this score. Co. will have
to be reconstructed, so that the men who now stand on
velvet may be made to pay their fair proportion of the
cost, or debentures will have to be issued, in which case
the men who have already subscribed over £lOO,OOO may
be “jockeyed” out of the concern. Co. is in 50,000
shares of £lO each, 34,000 of which are “ preference,”
which evidently means that they were issued as paid-up
and preferential in the matter of profits. No considera-
tion for these privileges is disclosed ; it was probably
merely the transfer of the right to mine for coal under
the harbor. The holders of these shares are the men who
should now be made put up some cash or retire.
* * * *
All Cobar mines, except the copper mine, are
again at a standstill for want of water. Occidental
managed to run its mill for 21 days for 380 oz. gold, not
including cyanide return. Some day the politicians will
realise that Oobar is a place of sufficient importance to
warrant provision being made for a permanent supply of
water from one of the rivers, or from artesian bores. The
present calamity evokes no interest in political circles,
but if a question as to the slag-wheelers’ pay were raised
there would be no end of fuss. All the sympathy is for
the few men who are in work, and not for the hundreds
for whom work would be available were the field provided
with an adequate water supply.

Island Eureka (W.A.) return for Oct., 420 tons
for 5510 z., is up to the usual average. There has been a
big slump in the value of shares, for which no satisfactory
reason has been assigned. On official reports this looked
like a show which was good for at least a three years’
purchase at the current rate of div. Stock exchanges
have been trying for official information as to the con-
dition of the mine, but so far have failed to get any satis-
faction ; there seems to be more circumlocution in con-
nection with co.’s business than there is in any Govern-
ment department.

Lady Shenton, Menzies (W. A.), for year ended
March 31, treated 14,516 tons for 22,5780 z. of gold, worth
£75,301; working expenses, £67,201, or £4 12s. Bd. per
ton. Value of a ton of ore was £5 3s. 10d., so net profit
was £BlOO, or Us. 2d. per ton. By taking £5OOO from re-
serve and adding £ll,BOO brought forward, the credit
balance was increased to £24,900, out of which two divs.,
totalling £12,000, were paid. Had it not been for accu-
mulated tailings, the year would have ended with a loss
on operations of £5700, as 13,600 tons of old tail-
ings yielded 46000 z., worth £13,800. After treating
this lot, another 13,000 tons remained for current year.
Amount written off for depreciation was quite inadequate,
as the plant, besides being obsolete, is now only fit for
the scrap heap. This is self-evident from the fact that
the 33-head mill treated but 1210 tons monthly, or a daily
duty per stamp of 1.3 tons only, probably the poorest
showing of any battery in the State. A London journal
suggests the firing out of the three colonial directors, on
the plea that “ although admittedly, in many respects,
estimable gentlemen, they seem deficient in technical
knowledge of mining.” Has any one of the London
Board ever seen a gold-mine in his life, or does any of
them possess any knowledge whatever of mining ? 00.
has declared its 16th div. of Is. per share, or £BOOO, pay-
able on Nov. sth, also ,the result of treating old tailings
now practically exhausted. 00. is on the verge of bank-
ruptcy, as its assets are grossly over-valued, consisting as
they do of worn-out machinery, depleted stopes, and
various holes in the ground. Without the old tailings,
the monthly yield will only reach 12000 z., or barely loz.,
or £3 7s. 6d. per ton, while the costs will be nearly £1
more, judging from past figures.
W » * •
r Golden Link Consols, Kalgoorlie(W.A.), having
failed in its attempt to issue debentures, is to reconstruct
once more. New co. will have 500,000 shares of £1 each,
paid up to 18s. The assessment of 25., if fully met, will
realise £50,000, which directors “ confidently anticipate
will lead to divs.”—not liquidation divs. When the issue
of debentures was decided upon, a short time ago, a new
reef was conveniently found on the Eclipse lease, and the
May plant was installed to treat the ore. Since then,
little has been heard of the reef, and the May plant has
proved a white elephant. To assist the reconstruction
scheme similar tactics are now being pursued, and rumors
of a nice find in the Golden Link lease are being circu-
lated. Already over £250,000 has been spent by the co.
without practical result, except to prove the property
a “ cat.” Co. has been in existence in various forms
since Sept., 1894, and, on an average, over £30,000 a year
has been spent since then. Golden Sink would be an ap-
propriate name for the mine.
Great efforts are being made to keep Horatio
Bottomley’s Associated Financial Co. above ground.
During the last 12 months £126,000 out of the authorised
issue of £160,000 debentures have been placed, and
attempts are being made to place the remaining £24,000.
As an inducement, the £lOO scrip is offered at £7O, to-
gether with 50 fully-paid £1 shares in the Associated Mt.
Jackson, W.A. D. D. Rosew f arne, the consulting
engineer of the co., some three months ago reported:
“ Mill trial satisfactory ; three shifts to be run in future.”
Since then no returns have been published, but Rose-
warne now says “that co. intend erecting a cyanide
plant, and removing and renovating the battery.” The

Boulder Perseverance, after a secret existence
of over seven years, has at last been thrown open to a
pressman. As six levels, extending to about 1J miles,
were negotiated inside a couple of hours, and the infor-
mation published was supplied en bloc by the mine
officials, the report, as an expression of outside opinion,
is absolutely valueless. So far as can be ascertained, bj r
leakage, the mine is looking as well as, or even better
than, ever, and the recent big drop in scrip must be
attributed to London manipulation.
* • • •
Long Reef, Lennonville (W.A.), controlled by-
Bewick, Moreing and Co., has again come to the end of
its tether. The mill was closed down in March after
depletion of reserves, but was re-started in June on ore
from the Galtee More, acquired from Kearney for £3400
cash and 16,600 shares, after 8., M. and Co. had abandoned
an option on the lease at a much lower figure. Since its
acquisition about 8000 tons have been treated for 4000 oz.
B.M. and Co. are now said to be negotiating with Kearney
for his Wheel of Fortune North lease in same district. As
crushings from this property have ranged from 7oz. to
lOoz. per ton for large parcels, it will need a fortune to
buy it. At the 300 ft. level the ore is so rich that the face
has been boarded up till Kearney’s return from his honey-
moon trip to Ireland.
* • » *
Lake View manager states that the mine is only
about to begin its career as a gold-producer. He bases
this opinion on development in several sections of the
mine. But this is a somewhat familiar story. It is not
so long since Lake View was to produce at least half-
a-ton of gold per month and to continue to do so for
several years. The performance did not equal the
promise for as many months.
« * * •
Great Boulder cut the lode at 1500 ft., 9ft. wide,
assaying 35dwts. At 1600 ft. the diamond drill proved
over lift, of ore, worth about 25dwts. per ton. The lode
is nearer the shaft at 1600 ft. than it is at 1500 ft. This is
an important feature, as it removes the fear that the
lode would dip out of the Boulder into the Horseshoe.
It is not intended to crosscut for the lode at 1600 ft. at
present. There could be no more solid proof than this
that the co. must have very extensive ore bodies in
reserve in the levels above.

Latest cable from London reports sales of Briseis
at 11s. as against local sales at 14s. Evident that London
knows more than has been told to Australian share-
holders. Local report is that cost of removing over-
burden is nearly double original estimate, and now it is
semi-officially stated that the tin drift is not as easily dis-
integrated as was anticipated. All that is needed now to
knock timid holders out of Briseis is a decline in the value
of tin, and then the smart men will have their chance to
scoop the pool.
• • • •
Victoria Caledonia is responsible for a little
scandal which has shocked all Charters Towers pro-
prieties. Directors admit that gold was kept back from
Aug. and Sept, crushings to average later returns, some
of which, instead of being lfoz. per ton, as reported, were
less than ljoz. There is nothing very objectionable in
this system of averaging returns, provided shareholders
are made aware of the fact. V.C. directors would have
escaped much bitter criticism had they not observed a
policy of secrecy. A few weeks ago shares were worth
345. ; now they stand at about 11s.

* • * *
Mr. Trent, ex-manager of N. Lyell, forwards to
Tub Bulletin from Salt Lake, U.S.A., copy of a lengthy
“ final report ” which he has addressed to the London
shareholders. It is a lively epistle which tends to convey
the impression that there was only one honest, capable
man ever associated with the show, and if he had been
allowed to exercise his judgment it would now be a
big profit-earner instead of being practically bankrupt.
The report bristles with references to “ rascals,”
“thieves,” “lunatics,” “vampires,” “vultures,” “sharks,”
“drunkards,” “lazy, worthless, religious partisans,” and
altogether is an aggregation of serious allegations the like
of which, probably, were never made against any other
co. “I charged crime and malfeasance enough in my
reports to have filled Newgate with scoundrels.” If one
third of the charges recited could be proved in a court of
law this assertion would be fully warranted. “An im-
partial investigation of the affairs of the co. will reveal
a condition of management that would startle and
amaze the most expert mine and market-rigger.”
With such a sensational document before them, it is
hardly conceivable that London shareholders will remain
inactive, more particularly as Mr. Trent challenges
inquiry—“ lam quite prepared to appear before the most
capable metallurgists and engineers of Great Britain and
defend my position, and I have no fear of their verdict.”
Trent describes the mine as a “splendid one, without
peer in Australasia, and if the works designed by me were
restored to their original and intended condition, and
operated by experienced men, and the mine economically
managed, it could produce copper as cheaply as any mine
in the world; the entire cost should not exceed £37105. per
ton of copper sold in London. Nothing but the unusual and
overwhelmingly bad management has prevented the
mine from yielding liberal divs. in spite of the disastrous
railway, ship, and port expenditures, and the continual
bleeding by the vampires and sharks.”
• * * *
On the strength of statements made at the Star
of the East meeting on 30th September last, Bulletin
congratulated Ballarat on the prospect of having a per-
manent productive mine different from the usual will-o-
the-wisp concern—divs. to-day and calls to-morrow.
It now seems that the congratulation was pre-
mature. It was stated that there were 70,000
tons of ore of satisfactory grade in sight, but
successive crushings since have shown a diminution in
quality down to the point which is only payable, and
further, the inevitable cross-course is introduced to
account for falling off in quantity of ore available. Shares
have had a big fall, and scrip holders who voted a hand-
some premium to the chairman in contemplation of the
alleged excellent prospects must feel rather sick. Never
be grateful before the div.

The great tobacco fight in England is over— the
American Tobacco Go. has been absorbed by the Imperial
Tobacco Go. The agreements were signed on 30th Sept.,
and by them the whole of the British interests of the
American co. (including Ogdens, Ltd.) have been bought
by the English co. The two co’s. have entered into a
compact re the tobacco trade of the rest of the world,
and the export business of both will be amalgamated
under the name of the British-American Tobacco Co.,
Ltd., with a capital of £6,000,000.

“One striking feature of the gold-dredging
industry of California is the fact that stock in a gold-
dredging company has never been offered to the general
public before or after operations were begun. All com-
panies are close corporations, all have been intelligently
and efficiently managed, and all appear to have been
financially successful so far.” So says an American paper.
No Australian or Maoriland paper could truthfully make
similar statements.

Society
The ethics of the jury-room have been
under hot discussion at Broken Hill. In a big
and sensational case 11 were for acquittal and
one wasn’t, so, the jury disagreeing, accused was
bound over for another trial, and another trial out
there means at least £2OO, even though the man
should be found not guilty. The 11 therefore
debated whether they ought not to send the facts
to the Attorney-General before he decided on a
second trial, or whether they should “ respect the
secrecy of the jury-room,” and let ordinary pro-
cesses go on. And, of course, when they started
to debate it the public soon knew all about the
division, including the odd man’s name ; where-
upon everybody in the district with a name any-
thing like that advertised that he wasn’t the
person referred to, and the person himself replied
that it wasn’t at all likely these others would be
mistaken for him. So one crowd is declaring that
it’s an awful thing wlien jury-room confidences are
broken and conscientious jurors are intimidated ;
and another crowd is declaring just as vehemently
that it’s another awful thing when a man is
refused his liberty and fined £2OO because of the
obstinacy of one man in 12.

Congested court business is a periodic
trouble in N.S.W., and has been so since the
earliest times. The generation of judges just
passed away used to know few holidays in the
twelvemonth. Some of them sat all the year round.
Still earlier the congestion was greater. Judge-
Advocate Collins taking his seat on the bench
used almost daily to complain of the quantity
of business, and in desperation finally struck out a
new method of work. When the cases were listed
he ran through them with his pen, setting down
convicts and ex-convicts to be tried by magistrates
and reserving for his own court charges against
freemen, or as he termed them “ respectable per-
sons.” Even charges of murder were thus tried
by magistrates in Judge Collins’ days.
Haines, the man shot dead in Centennial
Park, Sydney, t’other night, seems to have de-
served his fate. Evidence given at the inquest
showed that he was one of a band of alleged men
who prowled round the park of nights ‘ ‘ foxing ”
amorous couples. Though only a carter (getting,
probably, about £2 a week), Mr. Haines seems to
have been able to maintain a good deal of style : he
kept a cavalry charger and was in the fashion-
able Lancers’ regiment (which now, by the way,
threatens to more or less disband if it is robbed of
its gaudy trappings by a retrenching Govern-
ment). There’s no suggestion to give Haines a
military funeral; though he died bravely—crawl-
ing towards a Centennial Park kopje to recon-
noitre and listen for the voice of Love.
This “ foxing ” was quite a business. Strangely
enough some of the beings engaged in it came for-
ward to give evidence at the inquest. One said
that there were eight or nine of them in one band;
they watched the couples for amusement and made
no money out of it (a likely yarn, that!); they
averaged three nights a week at the business.
Several of them owned up to having been fired at
now and again ; they seemed to look upon that as
part of the legitimate risk.
One of the “ foxing” witnesses at the Centennial
Park inquest evoked a neat retort from a juryman :
Witness : He had been touting about 12 months, and
never thought his life was in danger. He Hsed to crawl
on his hands and knees to a couple, and then he would
crawl away again.
A juryman : Like a reptile.
The reptiles, by the way, were known as “The
Social Purity Society of the Centennial Park.”
* * *■> * •
“ L.W.” on the “ foxer ” :
The sudden death (per bullet) of Lancer Haines in
Sydney Centennial Park has aroused a widespread public
interest in the business of “foxing.” Most people have
a fixed impression that the foxer is always a blackmailer.
That isn’t true, though, unfortunately for the posthumous
reputation of Mr. Haines, there seems much reason to
surmise that he belonged to the smaller section of
the great order of foxers which makes money out
of the pastime. But there is another class of foxers
created by the insatiable greed to look upon the
hidden thing's of life which is so common a trait. ’Twas
that which made Zola a “great” novelist, and makes
John Norton a power in the land. It fills to overflowing
the six-women-in-tights-and-a-lewd-joke theatre whilst
the artistic comedy co. goes empty away. It sells the
crude and indecent transparency for a guinea a dozen
while a whole book of beautiful and clean artistic studies
brings only a miserable half-crown. Few people are
really free from the morbid craving, and many of these
are free only with the freedom of satiety. TheSdifference
between the man who sneaks along on his Reptilian
waistcoat to watch the amorous couple under the fig tree
and the other disreputable citizen who gloats over the
detailed descriptions of sin in a police-oourt-and-legs
organ is only one of courage. The man in the park risks
his own life ; the other fellow, in the deck chair on the
balcony, risks those of newspaper employees. Stroll
around the dark places of Sydney Domain (which are full of
iniquity every night) between 8 and 9.30, there will be dis-
covered a number of portly citizens, smoking choice cigars,
wearing slouch hats and armed with night-glasses. The
copper on the beat touches his hat to them, and never
interferes with the couples in their vicinity. They smoke
silently, the night-glasses sweep the shaded spots beneath
the trees, and the atmosphere is full of suppressed
chucklings and the smacking of old, thick lips. Those
portly citizens are gentlemen of wealth and influence.
Yes, foxing is a most disreputable practice, but if the
names of all the people who practise it were published,
many would declare that there must be something laud-
able about a pastime that is under such distinguished
patronage.

A sample of Minister Bent’s alleged
Vic. railway reforms is shown on the Ararat
to Portland line. Although wool and chaff
■were fairly pouring in to the various stations
and “ starvers ” were being unloaded in thousands,
the binjied blusterer stopped two ordinary daily
trains. Results —first, blocked-up stations, then
a stream of “ specials ” to get the line clear.
With the cancellation of the ordinaries the station-
master’s assistants were also withdrawn and now
weary-eyed S. M’s—the night-duty is incessant —
stumble blindly about scarcely comprehending
whether they are pulling the right lever or not.
This line, which a ganger avers is the rottenest he
ever worked on, is already notorious for minor
accidents.
• • » *
A Victorian railway employee writes to
the Abgus :
I have the honor to be employed in the Railway De-
partment, and my hours of labor are as follows : —Early
shift—don’t forget, early shift—half-past 5 a.m. till a
quarter to 12 a.m., and 4 p.m. till 7 p.m., first week;
second week, late shift, half-past 1 p.m. till the finish,
which might be 11 p.m., half-past 11 p.m., or 1 a.m. ;
third week, half-past 5 a.m. to half-past 12 p.m. I never
get away till a quarter-past 1 p.m. on this shift. Then
back again from 4 p.m. till 6 p.m. Fourth week, half-
past 1 p.m. till finish. While I am working these small
hours I am followed about by a stationmaster, head
porter, and platform inspector. We receive 6s. per day,
five days a week. I have a wife and four children ; pay 10s.
a week rent, Is. 3d. lodge ; I am compelled to pay £2 12s.
per year compulsory insurances, and Bs. a month fares. I
ask any fair-minded man if he thinks we are over-paid or
under-worked at this station.
And this is the class that Thomas of the Stomach
is retrenching ?

An interesting feature of the recent
* ‘ Selina Sangal ” meeting in Sydney Town Hall
was Dr. M‘Carthy’s advancement of the hypothesis
that Selina Sangal suffered from nymphomania,
supported by quotations from Taylor’s Medioal
Jurisprudence. Taylor is not the latest —perhaps
not the best —writer on legal medicine; but he,
like all the rest, maintains that criminals are all
more or less insane. When that lesson shall have
been learned, we shall have the whole science of
penology in a nutshell. There is not another
word to be said about it.
• • • •
Melbourne Herald, which practically
engineered a local movement for Selina Sangal's re-
prieve, was shocked by the fact that while most of
its male correspondents favored the course of
mercy, nine women out of ten badly wanted Selina
hanged. Many yearned to give the rope an extra
jerk themselves, while a few favored an antici-
patory course of torture. Some ministering angels
wanted the new-born babe chloroformed or
strangled also!
• * • s *
Wise’s N.S.W. State Children Bill com-
promises on what is known as Section XVI. This
clause in the 1900 Act, allows the
S.O. Board to pay allowances to
“ deserted wives, and deserving
widows ” for their children.
Under this provision consider-
able imposition has been prac-
tised, and it is difficult, more
especially in the cases of the
deserted wives, to check it. A
man earning 255. a week, with
five children to keep, knows
that if he clears out the State
will pay, say, 12s. 6d. a week
to his deserted spouse, and
that fact very often invites
him to go. So in the new Bill,
all these cases come before a
special and private court (estab-
lished by the Bill) when evi-
dence is given on oath. An
attempt was made in the
Leg. Council to exempt
widows from this court busi-
ness, but unsuccessfully.
Some very queer cases come
under this clause. Recently a
woman with a large family applied
for aid. She had been married
twice, the second time believing
her first husband dead. After some time he re-
appeared and, Enoch Arden-like, disappeared again
at the sight of hubby No. 2. The woman has now
conscientious objections to returning to the second
one, and he objects to supporting a house and
home where admittance on the footing, of a hus-
band is refused. Query: Is the applicant a
“deserted wife”?
« * • •
The bewitching variableness of woman
again. At Broken Hill she hadn’t been all she
should be, and her lord, who sells ice-cream,
picked up a horribly blunt tomahawk and broke
in the side of her face—a ghastly disfigurement.
Yet when, five weeks later, they lifted her out of
the hospital into the court, she said, quietly,
“I have forgiven him,” and would go no further.
The man in the dock fell in a helpless heap.
South Australia’s latest horror supplies another
case. They had decided to break up the un-
happy home, and were dividing the furniture.
There was a deadlock, an angry interchange, and
the man reached for a gun. ‘ ‘The wretch would
shoot me,” she said; and he did. “Jessie,” he
was saying inside a few seconds—“ Jessie, give
me one kiss more before you go ! And forgive
me.” And the dying woman kissed and forgave.
N.S.W. Railway Commis-
sioners hope to have the zig-zag on
the Western line cut out next year.
The zig-zag is a source of grave
danger; the fact that a big accident
has not occurred as yet is to be attri-
buted to good luck and almost super-
human care. The Commissioners
have taken all kinds of precau-
tionary measures; the whole of the
track is sprinkled with “sprags” to
block the wheels of carriages and
trucks in the event of a break-away.
In addition a ganger precedes every
passenger-train that goes over the
zig-zag. But the Commissioners
cannot eliminate the element of
“human fallibility,” and a zig-zag
catastrophe is certain one day if the
line remains. On several occasions
recently trucks have slipped over
the embankment, and only recently
a goods train, which had got beyond
control, smashed into the dead-end
of the line, and, going beyond it,
the engine stopped with one portion
hanging over a precipice; happily
for driver and fireman the bulk of
the weight was on the side of safety.
On a recent holiday a great boulder
fell against the rear of a passenger-
train on the zig-zag. This caused
no more harm than delay to traffic,
but a few seconds difference in the
time of fall and the passengers
aboard would have had little leisure
to say their prayers. Only a few
weeks ago the engine of the mail
train, in shunting from front to
rear of the train, missed the points,
and instead of going on the other
line, bumped heavily against its
own train. Fortunately it was going slowly, and
the train was standing on an up-grade, and little
damage was done, though some of the travellers
felt cold and sick. These are but a few zig-zag
happenings. The suggested deviation should
certainly be hurried up.
• * • •
A curious—and very hard— case is that of
a man who, at Carlton, Melbourne, was lately
hauled up for ‘ ‘ neglecting to contribute towards
the support of his ohild in the Neglected Children’s
Department.” The youngster, it turned out, was
only technically defendant’s offspring, the actual
father being the former husband of a divorcee
whom defendant had married. The lady had gone
back to her divorced husband, leaving her child
behind, and the second husband thus, instead of
the wife he did own, had the child lie didn’t.
There are several fine large morals sticking out
just about here.

“Theta ” sends an eye-opener on an un-
savory but sausageous subject. Ptomaine poison-
ing, by the way, is just now a very current theme,
and * ‘ slink ” veal is an essential ingredient m
common “ pork ’’-sausages:—
“ F.H.” (8., 30/10/’O2) is such a cocksure contradictor
that it seems a pity to disturb his equanamity. “The
country forwarding-agent who sent 700 slinks to Mel-
bourne. . . did the impossible.” Did he? Writer is
sitting within a stone’s throw of the railway' station
where it was done. At said station calves are trucked by
dairymen, who supply four butter factories with, at
present, about 30,000ga1. of milk daily. This means
about 11,000 cows, most of which had calves this season.
Out of these calves, at the very outside only 2000
were reared. The vast majority were sent to Melbourne,
during the months of July, Aug. and Sept, chiefly at
ages varying from one hour to one week, with an incon-
siderable fraction of “ vealers,” i.e., calves a few weeks
older. This station is one of several along the line from
Geelong to Warrnambool, and though it sends, perhaps,
the most slinks to Melbourne, it has two very honor-
able rivals. The slinks are sent away in loads by
(“ F.H.” notwithstanding) forwarding agents, who see
to their dispatch and delivery, while the money
is sent direct to the dairymen. “ F.H.” again:
“ The statement that many of them are not a
week old is pure rubbish.” The phrase should have
been “ most of them are not a week old.” During the
calving season, in the months named, the dairyman takes
his slinks to the station once a week, as a rule ; and, on
the day he goes in he slaughters all born since his last
trip, right up to those calved that morning, unless the
latter are particularly small. A dairyman who read
F.H’s remarks nearly choked himself laughing. “ Why,”
he said, “ many’s the calf I’ve killed with ‘ the yellow
on (i.e., before its mother had cleaned it) and sent to
Melbourne.” Finally, regarding weight, F.H. apparently
doesn’t know the big, well-fed, western district cows—or
their calves. It is a poor W.D. calf that won’t draw the
■lOl b. when a week old, and any number of them are born
that weight. By the way, a few days ago a bull that was
so diseased that it was impossible to put him in a sale-
yard, brought the owner £8 ss. as dead meat in Mel
bourne. Yes, it’s a beautiful industry, and F.H. is a
fitting defender of it.

Recent cases of ptomaine poisoning in
Australia recall that the Buenos Ayres health-
authorities absolutely prohibit the sale of any
form of pig-meat during the summer months.
Yeal is, also, classed as out of season.
A CITY LUXURY. .
“Tram Victims.— A return has
been laid on the table of N.S.W.
Leg. Assembly giving the number
of people who have been killed and
wounded by Sydney trams from
Ist Jan., 1900, to 30th Sept. last.
During that period no less than 83
people have been killed, 28 being
passengers, and 55 other than
passengers. The roll of the
wounded amounts to the almost
incredible total of 564.”
After last week’s revelation the victims of
Wesleyan Sunday-school picnics will deserve no
pity. If they run the risk they must take the
internal consequences. It seems that the Wes-
leyan tucker provided for a Cup Day picnic at
Werribee (Vic.), was principally sandwiches, lined
with ham mince-meat. The ham was minced on
the sultry Monday night, and the sandwiches
were then packed together in damp cloths to keep
the bread nice and moist. Thus the cured flesh of
the late lamented pig was—in the words of
medical science—“exposed to the vera causa
of putrefaction or kindred agents,” and subsequent
exposure to directsun-heatcompleted the ideal con-
ditions for the development of poisonous organisms.
Useless for any gentle shepherd to ask the Lord’s
blessing on sandwiches prepared in reckless
defiance of Biblical teaching. What is the good
of believing in Moses and the Prophets if you are
going to chop the forbidden ham into mincemeat
and invite the flies of the air to settle thereon
before making it up into damped sandwiches ?
Down at Flemington, the ungodly bought what
they could get at Skinner’s bars, and the Lord
mercifully said that it was good enough ; whereas
the Werribee picnickers took what was offered
them in a meek and lowly spirit, and it gave them
Sheol.
* * * •
This year’s Cup crowd at Flemington was
remarkable for the large proportion of women who
stood around on the slopes in front of the grand-
stand and mingled with the men on the outskirts
of the betting paddock. Take them altogether,
they were a most tired and disconsolate-looking
lot. Some had seemingly come “on their own,”
and were sorry for it.” Others, as the day
advanced, were engaged as a rule in waiting at
sundry places for their truant Jims, who had
dropped their money and gone to look for some-
body who might “know something good ” for the
next race. Wonderful how the girls—of the rural
variety, as a rule—rush into discomfort for the sake
of “seeing the Cup,” and then don’t see it because
they are not tall enough, and go away disgusted
with their outing—to return in larger numbers than
ever the following year. It is an awful thing to
be a woman who can’t find a seat in either of the
Stands. Perhaps it is more awful to be a male
escort on whom the distressed female depends for
companionship and refreshment.
A distinct percentage of well-dressed Chinamen
—pure, or half-bred—was another Cup feature.
One party of Chows came armed with cameras and
started to take photos from the Lawn before the
racing began. Presumably these alien camera
fiends send views of the Melbourne Carnival to
their relatives in the Flowery Land, together
with a graphic description of how the Clistian
multitude pays tribute to the Hebrew spoilers.
* * * *
Be the condonation by Sydney Unitarian
Church of its ex-pastor Worthington’s American
and Maoriland villainies, it’s the old story over
again—“ More joy over one sinner,” &c. Had
Worthington not been a sinner, there would have
been no need for reformation, and he would have
had no special interest. Also, there were his
“earnestness of purpose” and “brilliancy as a
platform speaker.” He was (or rather, might
have been) a very good investment for the church,
and, after all, was really no worse than scores of
other clerical bad eggs who have perfumed the
Sydney air. And the nonsense he talked about
Isis and Osiris was a good deal pleasanter, and
had just about as much fact behind it, as the
ordinary parson’s talk about the G-olden Shore
and the Jasper Sea and Sheol and the Devil—to
say nothing of Job’s boils and Jonah’s whale.

Tendency in Australia now is to treat the
returned cohentingenter as something much less
than a citizen. One brought back £5O from
S’Africa and put it in a bank. Cheque followed
cheque at his hotel until he signed one cheque too
many. Then the police grabbed him, and,
although he pleaded that he thought he had some-
thing behind or coming to him, the bench
sentenced him to a month’s hard. Many times a
week politician’s cheques come back. Many times
a day business men’s cheques are temporised in
Sydney banks. This Boer-killer really had an
account current; he seems to have been gaoled on
general principles because he was a “ returned
empty.” ....
A stout Sydneyite, at La Perouse
recently, met an affable colored person
who offered him a fine bunch of Christmas
bells, and after a long chat told him he
could get plenty in the paddock, pointing
towards the hospital fence. “ But,” said
the visitor, “that’s the lazarette—l
couldn’t go in there.” “What ? are you
afraid of lepers?” asked the darkie.
‘Why, I’m one; I’ve just come out of
there.” Then the flowers were dropped
hurriedly, and a scared fat person made a bee-
line for the nearest water.
» * • *
Parson Dill Macky has fallen into a shock-
ing orifice. For months past he has been raging
with extraordinary muscular vigour about how
the Roman Catholics monopolise the N.S.W.
Civil Service; and in a rash moment he seems to
have incited a member to ask for an official return
of the religions of the civil service by way of
demonstration. The return shows, apparently,
that there is a conspiracy among Anglicans, Pres-
byterians, and Unitarians to monopolise public
offices; at least these sects, especially the Pres-
byterians, who are a far-reaching race and very
swift after the bawbee, have more than their fair
share of the public billets, while R.C’s. have far
less than they should have if jobs went on
a population basis. But Parson Dill doesn’t
admit that he has fallen in. He says that he
refuses to accept the figures, which is an illogical
way that parsons and women have. Parson Dill,
by the way, adds this (D.T. report):
The Roman Catholics only occupied the positions not
requiring brains. Although the education system was
condemned by Catholics it was significant that 60 per
cent, of the teachers were Catholics.
Dill evidently holds that it doesn’t require brains
to be a teacher ! And perhaps he is right. It
might also be right to say that it doesn’t require
brains to be a bigoted “ religious” incendiary.
* » * *
Of the 300 odd resurrected Chinese skele-
tons just sunk in the Pacific in the foundered
s.s. Ventnor, all but one were accommodated
in the hold with their heads towards the
bow of the ship. The odd corpse who
achieved the distinction of setting out for
home in a sort of holy state of decomposition
was one Sew Hoy, a wealthy Chow who, in this
life, wore a silk hat and fine linen, and was in one of
the upper circles of Scotchbyterian Dunedin. He
had a separate sanctuary rigged up on the deck of
the ship. His son saw personally to the nailing-
down of his parent’s debris, and despatched
the old man in great style. The son, by rhe way,
is an ex-student of Otago University. But young
Sew Hoy (his full name is Mr. Kuin Boy Sew
Hoy, which sounds like weighing the anchor) is
a member of the Chong Shin Tong Society, which
shows that it takes more than an ordinary Uni-
versity to knock the Tong tenets and the joss and
Confucius and general Rumtifoo out of the Celes-
tial mind, and that a course of Shakespeare has no
effect on the Chow pigtail, any more than the
regimen of the Dunedin Medical School changes
the sanitary notions of Kum Boy and Ah Fat.
• * • *
John Norton, in the course*of a twaddly
speech on the Sunday Trading Bill, in N.S.W.
Parliament:
What is this infernal tintinnabulation of bells on
Sunday morning that we hear, breaking the rest of the
over-worked citizen who comes from his labors late on
Saturday night, and only gets to bed in the early hours of
Sunday morning ? What is this blasted brazen babel of
bells, ringing on Sunday morning? Does it not disturb
the sick? I wonder it is permitted in a Christian com-
munity—this banging and clanging of bells.
Considering how the Sabbath morning calm is
wrecked by the demoniacal yells of the malefactors
who hawk John’s paper, his suddenly-aroused
consideration for the sick and the weary is most
refreshing.

Daring the trial of the Kenniff case in
Brisbane one of the jury, weary of being locked up,
asked the Judge for leave to employ a barber to
give him a shave —as he had neither shave nor
bath since the trial began. The Judge consented *
enjoining the juryman on no account to talk with
the barber. But how about silencing the barber ?

A member of the Sydney Unitarian Church
writes to the daily press to explain to the public
how
such a depraved scoundrel as Worthington should have
been selected as a channel for spiritual life by a number
of truth-seeking and straight-going people.
The “explanation” is very unconvincing, and
would have been better left unpublished. Writer
admits
members of the church committee, together with num-
bers of the regular Sunday frequenters, heard all about
his American villainies, together with his Maoriland
roguery, but his apparent earnestness of purpose and
brilliancy as a platform speaker disarmed all suspicion
with most of us, and, in fact, induced his many hearers
to believe he had really reformed.
In short, they trusted the shepherd—even though
he hadn’t quite picked his teeth of his last lamb—
because of his sweet smile. A church, especially
a church that puts on airs as being a logical, scien-
tific, rational, non-superstitious, non-credulous
church, needs to be run with a little more horse-
sense than that.

Despite the fact that the present is the
most prosperous season Tasmanian producers
have experienced in modern times the mainland
drought and inter-State freetrade having pro-
vided a good market for the exceptionally heavy
crops—the poverty in the towns gets no less.
Week before last Hobart Benevolent Society
relieved 230 families, totalling 670 persons. In
addition, the “ Invalids’Home,” at New Town,
is crowded with indigent aged of both sexes. The
Tasmanian Govt, carefully keeps the sexes apart
in their workhouse, giddy youngsters of 70 being
separated from their wives in the interests of
morality !
A cornstalk landed in Geelong (Vic.)
t’other day in quest of treasure trove.
He was after the proceeds of a gold
escort robbery “committed in the
Black Forest near Ballarat early in
the 50’s.” A mate of his, an old man
who lately died at Wagga, had con-
fessed to having had a hand in the
robbery, and the gold, he said,
was planted in a cellar under
the old British dining-rooms
in Corio-st., Geelong, the
gang never having been
able to recover it. The
police of Sleepy Hol-
low have been con-
fided in, and they
find that the
cellar in question
has been filled in
for years. A pros-
pecting party is be-
ing formed to fossick
for the loot. The con-
fession of the Wagga
veteran is quite a likely
story excepting for a
few details. The only
escort robbery on a large
sgale was the Mclvor
robbery. That was done
in the Black Forest in ’54.
It is well known that the
men divided their booty,
but an individual share in
the takings would be a fat
parcel. The British dining-
rooms was then a popular
resort with immigrants and
miners. The Black Forest
was not near Ballarat, but on
the road from Melbourne, be-
ginning just about where
Flemington course now is.
Portion of the Mclvor loot is
'***' popularly supposed to be
buried near Sandringham, and has often been
dug for.

* • * •
Campbell, the gentleman who wanted
immense damages for being allegedly dropped out
of a Queensland train, received a sentence of seven
years, at Brisbane. He took it lying on his
stretcher, professedly paralysed as the result of
professedly falling on to the permanent way, and
the judge, who was evidently in a staggered state
of mind, expressed a hope that the gaol authorities
would make sure whether his affliction was real
before setting him to hard labor. If Campbell
isn’t really paralysed, then he is an ingenious and
most persevering fraud; if he is, and if he didn’t
fall out of the train to cause the injury, then what
did he fall out of ? Anyhow, the alleged patient
spoiled a dramatic finish by his persistence.
When sentence was passed he should have arisen,
and kicked the stretcher half-way across the court,
arid remarked that the game was up and he was
ready for some healthy stone-breaking exercise if
his Honor had a hammer about him.
