The growth of the Scotchbyterian con-
science in Melbourne is luridly illustrated by the
fact that the local Commercial Travellers’ club
has just had a terrible “rastle” with itself over
the question of playing ping-pong on Sundays !
Commercials, who used to make the continent
reek with the blue stories sent out from the
London offices of their firms (specially manufac-
tured there by well-paid pressmen) ! After a
stormy meeting of two hours, the Sabbatarians
were defeated, but only after all the opposition
had protested that their respect for the blessed
Sawbath was equal to anybody’s.
• * * •
Mr. Wise’s new State Children’s Bill
attempts to provide ways and means for making
well-to-do relatives support, or assist to support,
their family connections dependent on the State.
Exactly where to stop is a puzzling question, and
the Leg. Council got somewhat muddled in
discussing the proper definitions of a “near
relative.” F. B. Suttor pathetically said, for
instance :
There were cases—he had known of them himself—
where a woman had several illegitimate children, and
when they were grown up, perhaps 20 years afterwards,
she married again. Was it not hard that a person marry-
ing such a woman, perhaps in ignorance of the existence
of these children, should be called upon to support them?
It would be hard indeed to be called upon to rear
and educate the “indiscretions ” of one’s blushing
bride, born to her some score of years before one’s
wedding. Some bridegrooms might even expect
the “ indisoretions ” to support them.
OULPA MEA.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Hush thee ! O hush ! It comes a Rain.”
“ What comes, O heart 1 my heart?
It was the wind upon the pane.
That waked thee with a start.”
“ Nay, but I know ’twas not the wind ;
No wind could shake me thus.”
“ It was but the trembling of the blind
What could there come to us ? ”
“ What could there come ? O Life entwined 1
Something that cries within—
Something that moaned, and turned, and whined.
And mocked me with my sin."
MARIQUITA.
SPRING IN THE SUBURBS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The little urchins wrangle
With spirit in their squalls,
And there’s music in the mangle,
While the tinware seems to spangle
In its jangle on the walls.
There’s sweetness in the snapping
Of the father at the kids,
And there’s softness in the flapping
Of the mother’s hand a-slapping
For faults that she forbids.
The jam-tins gleam and glisten
As they linger in the lane ;
And it’s beautiful to listen
To the gab of that or this ’un
As he tells his ache or pain.
There’s gladness in the shutter
As it dances in the sun,
And the onion in the gutter
Seems to say “ let’s have a flutter ! ”
To the cheerful scrap of bun.
Oh, everything’s attractive,
And oh, everything’s a-cheer ;
The fleas are getting active—
And I know the Spring is here !
NARRANGIII BOORI.
DE PROFUNDIS CLAMAVI.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Gaunt Earth’s dry breast with ashes strewed,
The mother in her foiled,
No suckling grass to show,
As though she were a wanton lewd.
In rage to God I cried, and saw
The sky quick cleft apart
And straightway understood—
God sat in tears beneath the Law.
F.R.

Dalby (Q.) Herald, 5/11/’O2 :
Our foreman printer recently measured up the space
occupied by obituary notices in the Herald during the
last couple of months or so, and found it made 3§ yards.
This is so much dead loss to the paper, and, if a fatal
epidemic struck the town, ruin would stare us in the
face. We have, therefore, decided in future to charge for
such notices. So, when people feel like dying and want
something pleasant said concerning themselves, we hope
they will give directions to their next of kin in respect of
paying for the same. Plain obituary, £1 Is. : ditto with
biography £1 11s. 6d ; ditto, ditto, highly ornate and
eulogistic, £3 3s. Latin quotations extra.
A graceful form and well-moulded bust
are essential to perfect beauty. Lacti, the
wonderful “Skin Food” is nature’s remedy for
giving back those essentials when lost through
sickness or action of time, and for supplying them
when constitutionally deficient. It fills all hollows,
develops and gives a charming roundness to neck
and arms. Price 7s. 6d. per jar; post Bs. 3d.
Westall Guest, Toilet Specialist, 229 Bourke-st.
Melbourne. Wholesale, Elliott Bros., Sydney.*
A
GOOD TIP.
CHIMES HEIOSIECH
CHAMPAGNE.
A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, November 11, 1902.
My Dear Arini.—
The ladies of “the wealthy lower orders ’’indig-
nantly refuse to record their votes, and their ser-
vant-maids giggle at the poor police who are
compiling the roll. There are earnest appeals in
the daily press for all women, even though
they loathe the franchise, to suspend their white-
violet modesty and register their names, other-
wise the balance of voting power will fall to the
McTaggarts and the Muldoons. As a matter of
fact, most women do not understand the meaning
of tbe franchise. Even to a number of those who
are taking the vote as they would a new bangle,
politics is a word of no meaning. Women will
have to learn to use their new power intelligently,
and study the interests of their class. At the first
election the female vote will be merely a duplica-
tion of that of their husbands and fathers; but by
the time the second election comes round the
woman voter will possibly know where she stands,
and stand at the door of the polling booth telling
the voters that her husband is the best husband
in the world, but, poor dear man, knows nothing
of the real needs of the country.
When I see women, travelling on trams and
steamboats, buy newspapers and open them at
last night’s discussion on the Estimates, I’ll
have hope for practical results of womanhood
suffrage. As for the suffrage despisers, those
ladies “who go to Govt. House and are
sarcastic or indignant” with the collectors, as
soon as they learn that Lady and Miss Rawson
and Lady Beaumont (the last has always been an
earnest advocate of the franchise) have recorded
their votes they will do the same. One anti-
suffragist harebell writes to the press about
“pushing her way to the polling-booth.” Well,
it is unlikely that the struggle near the polling-
booth will be as notable as that which occurs
around the refreshment tables at a State Govt, or
Admiralty House “At Home.” Fear of changing
the old order, non-progressiveness, is stronger in
woman than in men, and as the late laureate has
it: “ All the past of Time reveals a bridal dawn
of thunder-peals wherever Thought has wedded
Fact.”
No wonder the working woman classes are
rushing the franchise, knowing what it has done
in Maoriland. Previous to the granting of female
franchise, domestic servants were carefully ex-
empted from all factory or labor legislation. Then
the vote came along and the domestic servants,
who were most numerous in aristocratic city
electorates, found that they often held at their
mercy the candidate who had beaten organised
Labor over and over again. The climb-down was
marvellous. Now the servant-girl must get a
half-holiday, her hours of labor are limited, she
has a minimum wage, and she cannot be dismissed
without proper notice or compensation, and her
wages are a first charge on the estate in case the
social star for whom she works suddenly collapses.
Ditto in re other forms of female work. The
voting-power of the factory-girl made her worth
the consideration of the haughtiest legislator in
the land, and when she discovered the art of
giving a block-vote, she bounded into a position
of importance and respectability, and became one
of the joints in the backbone of the country.
London Daily Express on Edmund Barton:
“ He would regard in a favorable light the offer
of some official position in this country. In this
he is known to be seconded by Lady and Miss
Barton.” As obvious as that the salmon loves a
clear lake better than being canned; or that a
bird prefers an open sky to a oage. Have any
Australians ever had such a time as was given
those who visited London as “guests of the
nation” during Coronation time? Instead of
exchanging reminiscences of the “ general ” and
h. and p. maid with mayoresses, alderman-
esses and squatteresses, our Prime Minister’s
wife and daughter purred with duchesses,
countesses and royal princesses. If a foreigner
called it would not be John, the vegetable
man, but a Count von Hochderkaiser; the
person half fawning, half peremptory, not
a man with a gas-bill, but a be-diamonded
exquisite from the Colonial Office. Nobody
dunned them for a billet or for any-
thing but the pleasure of their company. The
great ones of the earth, and the pride
and brains thereof, and the “ Emperor Butter-
fly ” railway specials and the royal carriages
were at their disposal. The world went very well
then, and Australia is now as ashes in the
mouth. Small wonder that Mr. Barton is
“ seconded ” by his family. But, whoever becomes
the figure-head of the Commonwealth, as Mr.
Copeland calls the High Commissioner, he and
his family will find their mistake if, when they go
back to London, they think to pick up the rose-
chain at the link at which it is dropped and con-
tinue to weave it with exotics. They will have to
start afresh with plain marigolds and jonquils.
Mrs. Curlewis, who so excellently organised the
Sydney meeting of protest against the Selina
Sangal execution, is of such a retiring disposition
that she had never attended a meeting in her life
before last week, and, indeed, had never been
previously inside the Town Hall! Her humanity,
however, was so worked upon by the contempla-
tion of the horror in question that she left no
stone unturned until she had brought about a con-
certed protest of Sydney citizens. One of Mrs.
Curlewis’s sons is the husband of that admirable
writer and charming woman, Ethel Turner.
On Monday afternoon, last week, on the stage
of the Sydney Lyceum, the members of the
orchestra presented Mr. Bland Holt with a hand-
some American roll-top office desk, bearing a
silver shield suitably inscribed. Conductor Percy
Kehoe made the presentation, and spoke of the
hearty esteem felt by the orchestra for Mr.
Holt. Bland was, of course, surprised at the
gift. Who ever before heard of the band—the
frozen-hearted, mercenary band-exuding the
milk of human sympathy and a roll-top desk ?
Dolores has always drawn good houses in
Sydney, but the city never cast its silk hat at
her feet and worshipped her till the “Queen of
Song ” came here, with very indifferent backing,
and charged a guinea for four songs. Then Sydney
began to realise what a great artist was Dolores,
who. gave anything between 15 and 18 songs
for five shillings. So when she returned, hot on
Melba’s heels, Sydney’s municipal building held
great crowds, who had suddenly awakened to
the worth of the guest who came among us so
unobtrusively and without any fanfaronade
of midnight booking advt. The audiences heaped
the flowers around Dolores, filled her carriage
with them, bore away fragments of her floral
tributes as souvenirs, and she walked down to her
carriage between lines of cheering people. For
an Australian city, it was a most extraordinary
outburst. And how it must delight Melba!
A section of Sydney society is very altruistic—
with other persons’ earnings. Mdlle. Dolores,
who went hence last week trailing clouds of glory
and with a fattened bank account (her farewell
matinee was a record-breaker), has been called a
“screw” by many worthy persons because her
concerts were practically all Dolores. She should,
they say, have engaged some local musical
twinklers to travel within her orbit. The brilliant
Dolores used to sing us 18 songs in a night when
she might, by getting little Miss Muffit to pad
the programme, have put us off with four. But
she would not have had crowded halls.
Admirers of the Dolores are grieved to see her
growing more and more puff-pastry like. They
fear that some of her chubbiness may get into her
singing organ and spoil it. The pretty French-
woman doesn’t seem to have enough personal
vanity. She wants to train down a stone, display
some taste in dress, and do her hair in a style
other than the most trying to a full, round face.
What quiverfuls most of the old N.S.W. identi-
ties had. Contrast the scantily-filled fashionable
homes of to-day. Late squatter P. Osborne and
the late Capt. O’Sullivan, for instance, had some-
where about twelve children each.
How weary one grows in one’s petty travelling
about the city of the stupidity of the other person.
The stupidity of the woman who comes to a
sudden stop on the gangway of a ferry steamer,
perhaps on a pouring wet day, blocking dozens
of people in her rear, while with ber eyes sbe
searches the upper deck for a friend. Then of tbe
woman who blocks the turnstile while sbe fishes a
penny from the bottom of her Dorothy bag.
Then there’s the man—l always put him down as
a remittance man—who crawls on to tbe tram just
about to start, blocking a lot of flurried women,
as if he were a snail going up his own particular
cabbage, without fear of ducks or gardeners.
Rejected-picture exhibitors have abated none of
their ardor to see their bantlings hung. The
difficulty so far has been to secure rooms, but now
comes the news that the Art Soc. is going to
vacate its chamber and take the gallery in
Vickery’s Chambers where late Soo. of Artists
used to show. So the Rejected, wbo are presided
over by the president of the A.N.A., will pro-
bably secure the eyrie.
Some Tenterfield (N.S.W.) girl enthusiasts got
a lien on Melba with a big buneb of roses when
the star’s train paused at their station en route
from Brisbane to Sydney. She came on to tbe
platform, took the flowers, the stammering com-
Eliments, and the adoration with frank pleasure ;
ut when they attempted questions such as
“What Australian city do you like best?”
“Shall we hear you in opera?” her answers had a
touch of the snub. Also, when one of the party
suggested : “ Everyone who goes to England likes
Australia best! ” Madame replied : “If they say
so they don’t mean it.” Then, glancing around
her, the World’s Voice asked: “What place is
this ? ” The enthusing gushers, who really believe
that the earth’s axis sticks out at Tenterfield, are
laid up with severe wounds —to their vanity.
Men must have “well-defined waists ” in future;
that is decreed by the tailors in London. To
secure the appearance of a slender meridian “ the
hips will be padded,” as were those of the early
Victorian dandies. This applies more particularly
when evening dress and overcoats are worn.
“ Really fashionable men” have already visited
corse tiers. “Waisters”—the advertisements of
national degeneracy!
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“The Governor-General’s dance on Monday
night was a hot engagement, and, despite an
official whisper that the ballroom had been re-
frigerated and kept in funereal gloom all day, the
800 guests soon made matters steam. The Acting
G.-G., in the early stage of a Royal complaint,
rested on one intact limb as the guests entered.
He was in pain, and wore a more impassive face
than heretofore. So his amiable consort smiled
for two.
“Melba was there in exuberant spirits and a
silk-and-lace creation, over which some orchids
sprawled and many gems sat winking. Tbe
diamond shoulder - straps, the medley of
precious stones circling the neck, and the
diamonds, fore and aft the brownish head, rep-
resented a fortune. Lord Dick was the first to
tick off a dance. Mr. Albert Miller hooked
Melba’s disengaged arm for the first square dance,
but on once letting go he was left high and dry in
the ranks, while others rushed to her side. “ But
where is my young man ? ” protested Melba with
a sweet seventeen pout, and the elderly Albert
paddled towards her. “Hurry up !” she en-
joined him, moving ahead.
“Looking sadly on at Melba’s lamb friskings
(with caper sauce) stood Signor Bensaude and
that small ebony-cane of a figure—the Welsh
flautist. They knew no one, and they looked
even glad at last to see Mr. Arens, who shook
them noisily by the hand and asked in convales-
cent English, ‘ What do I ? Is it to greet my
lady ? Do I shake the hand ? ’
“The surprise of the first Lancers was the re-
vival of Attorney-General ] )eakin. All the nerve
trouble had disappeared. He was gay, with" a
tendency to friskiness. Gov. Rawson suggested a
trophy-stand—a show-figure for decorations.
Medals ran along his coat side, depended from his
pockets, shone on his vest and his starched hoard-
ing, while a brilliant red order-sash struck across
his horizon. He danced most solemnly.
“ John Forrest’s * dancing ’ was a most
impressive bit of biz. His partner was Lady
Turner, and round and round her the big man
jog-trotted tirelessly, clumsily, but true to time, in
gait and deportment the very image of a circus
elephant waltzing ponderously round his trainer.
Bigjohn is said to have come back from ’Ome a
soda-water and cold-tea drinker, and is a shade
less fat and flushed for his hygienic diet.
“Lady Forrest, whose Coronation fun was
damped by hubby’s indifferent health, also looked
slighter than formerly in a rather bob-tailed gown
of guipure over silk and chiffon, the gentle color
of milky cold-tea. She had a quiet partner in
Treasurer Turner. Later on, that financier took
his stand near Melba with quite an excited
gleam in his round eyes as he drank in the
smiles, shrugs, nods and pouts of the vivacious
diva. Plain Bill Lyne sampled the same ambrosia.
Sydney’s Lyne would turn in his grave, they
say, if an attractive deceased lady settled down in
the lot next door. One rarely sees him when he
isn’t squiring some delicate dame or damsel. At
the Derby, he drew near to catch a spark or two
from Carrie Moore’s brown eyes. At Govt.
House, he basked in Melba’s diamond blaze, and
talked ‘ dear old Sydney ’ with a dashing press-
woman from the Harbor City.
“ Host Tennyson was too ill to dance. State-
Governoress Clarke (Vic.) was the most stately
dancer in the set. Her silver hair crowned a
midnight minuet, expressed sartorially in oyster-
satin, Limerick-lace and pearls, and her partner,
Gov. Rawson (N.S.W.), seemed quite proud of
piloting so well-found a ship. Lady Tennyson, in
blue moire, shared the solemnity with State-Gov.
Clarke (Vic.), who had the air of incessantly try-
ing to toss an invisible ping-pong ball on his nose.
It’s the way we do in the army, when we want to
look tall.
“Gov’nor Havelock is constantly on the trail
of a joke. His mouth purses up and his monocle
drops down with a simultaneous spring jerk.
Lady Havelock is of another species—spare of
words, smiles, and figure. She was a not unsuitable
Lancers’ partner for stiff little Sammy Way,
who always seems to have just come from the
Equity Court; his specs, have an official air ; his
little cross face an air of disapproval of all
frivolity.
“ Reporters labelled the Cup of 1902 the ‘Melba
Cup,’ but Melba was not present. ‘The Hot
Cup ’is the name that would suit it best. The
heat drove people off the lawns into the stand,
where they overcrowded one another miserably,
ate crumby lunches, and accused their neighbors
sotto voce of having used cocoanut-oil on their hair.
“ Melbourne Cup was mostly a vapor bath and
a dust-storm, certainly not half the show it should
have been. The weather made rags of chiffon.
Most wore linens, muslins, crashes, silks that
were light, cool, durable. A fine dress was worn
by a tall, willowy dress-designer, who got tremen-
dous effect out of all-cream —the skirt escaping in
frothy ebullitions, the bodice built-down heavily
with massive embroidery and handsome applique,
the sleeves distending into sudden chiffon gushing;
the hat crowning the effect with cherry-ripe
ribbons.
“ Premier Barton’s dame set an example in the
absenceof gew-gaw ornament. Her ungloved h ands,
at the Cup, showed only a long-service wedding-
ring and worn keeper on the most important of
all digits. One other lady, in contrast, wore
several turquoise and diamond rings, a long chain
with myriad charms, a diamond flight of swallows,
a coral necklet, and trinketed bangles of every
description. She jingled like a host of baby coral-
bells with every breath, and set up a heavy chime
when she moved. In the Barton and M'Eacharn
families the men put on the chief frill. Malcolm
and his son and Toby and his son were a highly-
glazed, highly-groomed quartette.
“Sydney’s Mayoress (Mrs. T. Hughes) wore
some smart clothes at the week's junkets, likewise
another Sydneyite—Mrs. H. M‘Neil. From the
other States Lady Way represented Adelaide
well; and Miss May Braddon, Hobart.
“Lady Tennyson looked cool, if sparse, in
biscuit, touched with black. Ladies Rawson
(N.S.W.), Clarke (Vic.), and Way (S.A.) rang the
changes on white, veiled with gauzy black. The
girls of the group wore simple muslin. A de-
lightfully cool-looking hat was worn by S.A.
Judge Way’s stepdaughter, a broad-brimmed
scroll of softest snowy crinoline, with big, white
bow and touch of black. Mrs. Stewart Brunton,
formerly Miss Florrie Strong, of Melb., re-appeared
after her brief honeymoon trip, in cream voile,
hat to match, and touch of turquoise blue.
Another bride in evidence was Mrs. Littlewood
Dawson, formerly Miss Louie Morrell, who went
’Ome to get her voice trained, arid got a husband
to train instead. The tall, dashing, returned
wanderer did the Cup in an exquisite blend of
ivory silk, insertion, and lace, made with long,
narrow train.
“Cup-day saw a bold sight in the refreshment
room, where men and women fought their way to
the B. and S. bar, and smart girls quaffed their
spirits without a spasm of conscience. The
smart girl of the day declares ‘ she won’t scorch
her innards ’ with boiling tea or bad coffee and
openly seeks a ‘reviver.’
“Chiffons had spreading room Oaks day.
Melba’s arrival was a fussier affair than on Derby
day, when the crowd was not found in the least
troublesome to the Diva’s bashfulness. Her jolly
figure was espied perched on the box-seat of the
Miller drag, males on all sides of her, only Miss
Shylie Blue’s millinery peeping from the back-
ground of silk beavers. Twenty hands rushed to
assist the Woman of Much Importance from her
lofty foothold, but she cleared the lot and brought
her 14 stone to the ground lightly. The Clarke
trio stretched necks from their stand stall to for-
ward their welcome smiles, and take her tenderly
into the fold. Miss Clarke wore the smartest
turn-out she has yet sported in these parts—a
golden syrup-colored silk crepe with lace railing.
“ Again a Collins-st. dressmaker carried off the
Oaks cake. She did it in a rig-out of cream silk,
with a bas-relief of pleated chiffons, and auriferous
streaks down the skirt of rose and gold-flowered
silk, the top-storey being raised to equal richness
by a matchless collar or shoulder cape of ivory and
cigarette-colored applique. Rose-and-gold tones
were reproduced in the top tier. There was some
talk as to the unfairness of dressmakers reserving
their best ideas for their own turn-outs, and
portioning out second-rate ones for the clients who
left everything in their hands. The race gathering
showed the ‘ shop ’ queens reigning supreme on
the terrace while their wealthy and fashionable
customers wilted in more commonplace dresses.
Fitting-room storms are brewing.
“At the Stonington Oaks party in the evening
Melba wore her opaline scale garb, with its patches
of purple-toned orchids and its diamond interrup-
tions. The florid Song Queen has made a pet of
little Miss Clarke, and this night, as at Tenny-
son’s hop, left all her male adorers to hook her
innocent arm and take her off for a ramble. A
bombastic swaggerer was a military gentleman
who made a profound fuss over every pretty girl
in the room, to the intense amusement of his
charming wife.
- “The average Yictorienne is none the more
attractive for her love of gambling. Worrying
over mining shares or wrangling about bets and
sweeps is bad for feminine looks, manners and
tempers. Woman, as yet, has not learned to
‘ play the game and play it fair.’ It’s money she
wants, not sport! There are fair fashionables
pencilling their dainty betting books at Fleming-
ton, who, so their intimates say, turn a dishonest
penny at the expense of any Society pigeon polite
or silly enough to submit to plucking. Lower in
the social scale, the she-sport, in losing moments, is
apt to stand revealed a shrew as well as a sharper.
When the Cup was won, a well-dressed woman in
the stand scolded Hubby, ten minutes on end,
because he had advised her against Victory.
“At the Bowes-Kelly’s afternoon crush last
Monday, little sister Dolly Castles warbled
‘ Good-Bye ’ with a fresh voice, stacks of confi-
dence, and an artful touch of tenderness. The
fair Dolly has twice the individuality and assur-
ance of her sister. Asked by a patronising adult
if she would like to follow in Amy’s footsteps, she
replied, with superior emphasis : ‘ Yes; I mean to
come out as a singer.’ It is not difficult to come
out. The trouble is to stop out.
“The first couple to take their appointed places
in the crescent shaped front space arranged for
vice-royalty, at the Melba concert, on Wednesday,
were Bigjohn and Dame Forrest. They played at
being hosts, and greeted the elect who came there.
The Huttons brought their two girl visitors in
tow. Lady Hutton was as beautiful as a picture,
very decollete, and in a charming mauve turn-out.
But Mrs. Waxellency beat all in the matronly
beauty line. She wore a picturesque blue.
“As Melba warbled with intoxicating charm
that famous Spanish dance-song, “Sevillana,” to
the throbbing of ’cellos and the quivering of
violins, Gov. Havelock was an appreciative study.
He sat with his arms resting on his chair’s sides,
his finger-tips keeping time, his monocle out of
dock, his merry eyes twinkling, his lips moving,
delight in his animated face.
“The State Governor’s garden party was spoilt
by showers. Busses, arriving with late-comers,
were rushed by returning guests, anxious to save
their flounces from the rain. The inmates pleaded
hard to be let out first, but only that there hap-
pened to be some muscular men among the
besieged, who repelled the furbelowed Huns, the
weaker vessels on board would have been taken
back to town without being allowed to dismount.
All rubbish about clothes and civilisation going
hand in hand ! A woman in fear for her finery
has more of the savage in her than her black sister
mid nodings on. The most impressive toilette
worn at the garden party was Mrs. Albert Miller’s
costly blend of black and white. Mrs. Edwin
Millar trailed over the wet grass in pale-blue
magnificence, inserted with medallions and similar
lace motifs characteristic of present modes, and
punctuated with clasps of gold and blue enamel.
Judge Williams’ wife had much twine-colored
lace mingled with her ivory silk, and a dash of
green in ber black hat.
“Melba never shrinks from taking or giving
knocks. In Sydney, they were counting the
takings of the first concert. , An enthusiastic
pressman bustled in, and addressing the personage
of the group, said, ‘ Madame wants you to come
back to lunch.’ ‘Tell Madame to go to —Rock-
hampton ! ’ replied the invited. ‘ She’ll never ask
you again!’ ‘Can’t help that.’ ‘I can’t take
such a message.’ * Butter it over as thick as you
like, that’s the gist of it.’ The true story ends
here, but intimates of Melba say that, had the
message been delivered in the rough, she would
simply have replied, ‘ Tell him to go to—Rock-
hampton, himself.’
“ ‘ Bicycle face’ isn’t in it with the appearance
of the Melb. lady golfer. You can tell her a mile
off, even without the implements. She wears a
hard expression, tries to talk like a man, and culti-
vates a stride like a camel’s. She is occasionally
pretty, but never ‘prepossessing.’ The same
trouble seems to apply to all followers of sports
which have not got down to common level. In
the earlier cycling days the feminine devotee of
the wheel was generally hard-faced, angular, and
unthrilling, but she has given place to the nice
round girl with an alluring smile and her hair care-
fully arranged on the latest kind of ‘ wad.’ When
the nice, round girl with the other recom-
mendations is to be seen a little more frequently
on the links, presumably the pioneers of feminine
sport will find someother leisure-filling occupation.
Yours affectionately,
AKENEHI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Mr. Flannery, private secretary to Minis-
ter R. E. O’Connor, is in hospital, seriously ill
from an operation for appendicitis.
Said that Iceberg Irvine hopes to stick to
office until he moves on to the Supreme Court
Bench ; the retirement of Mr. Justice Williams is
expected at an early date.
Senator ’Siah Symon, who sat in the chair
while Drink-cure Caulfield gave alcohol a bad time
in Adelaide, owns about the biggest wine-making
vineyard in the State. But, then, he is also a
lawyer.
Vice-Admiral Rawson, Governorof N.S. W.,
and President Lancelot Stirling, of S.A. Legisla-
tive Council, were both born on Guy Fawkes
Day, 1843, so that each completed his 59th year
this month.
If State Agents-General are to be continued
the next representative of N.S.W. will be Dr.
MacLaurin. A little push would have put the
canny Scotsman ahead of Yorkshire Copeland at
Hst picking,
Sydney University’s new Professor of
Mathematics, Mr. H. S. Carslaw, is, of course, a
Scotchman, but it is understood that he already
speaks several words of English. At latest dates
be could say “Porritch ” quite plainly.
Died recently in Queensland, John John-
son—-known as “Woolshed Johnson”—an old
mining pioneer, about whom was told (incorrectly)
the story that he once shod his horse with gold.
He was a sturdy democrat and a typical digger.
Speaker Holder, Trade-Minister Kingston
and Deputy-Opposition leader M‘Millan all date
from 1850. The last named will be 52 on Nov. 14.
Members of the Kingston Ministry in S.A. were
nearly all the same age for (in addition to Holder)
Coekburn, Gordon and Butler first saw the light
in 1850.
Reported from London that Mr. Toby
Barton is to be Federal High Commissioner, and
that the appointment would be “ very satisfactory
to Mr. Chamberlain.” Doubtless. Mr. Barton is
a big, soft man with an appetite ; Mr. Chamber-
lain is a small, hard, angular man with no hunger
except for getting his own way.
“ P.” : Is Australia undergoing a cycle of
“mediocrity in office ” ? Look at the present-day
State Premiers: Irvine (Vic.), See (N.S.W.),
Philp(Q.), Jenkins (S.A.), James (W.A.), Lewis
(Tas.). Who are they ? Ask the average citizen
in one State to name the Premier of another—or
even his own—and you will see.
While N.S.W. has for years past had
monosyllabic Premiers, in the persons of See,
Lyne, Reid, Dibbs, and Parkes, Victoria has had
a long succession of dissyllabic men heading its
Governments. The last Vic. Premier with a one-
syllable surname was Shiels, and even his name
seems as undetermined as his politics.
HE WAS SOFT.
“See you in town to-night, old boy?”
“Not till half-past eleven. The doctor won’t let
me out at night till then.”
“What an extraordinary thing! But why half-
past eleven? ”
“ Because that’s when the pubs, are all closed .”
Kow that Capt. Wallington has a billet as
rubber-down to the Dukelet, the cosy corner that
was made for him in the Australian Civil Service
is open to somebody else. Rumor says that the
somebody else will be Mr. Steward, the Tas-
manian, now chief clerk in the External Affairs
Dept., and certainly the most energetic goer in
the Federal service.
N.S.W. Auditor-General Rennie is to be
succeeded by Treasury accountant Vernon, who,
Treasurer Waddell says, “ understands the Audit
Act thoroughly.” The great point about the
Audit Act is that it practically prohibits criticism
of the Treasurer’s account. The barometer seems
to be “ set fair ” for N.S.W. Treasurers now, until
the Great Crash comes.
Minister John Forrest has made a bad
beginning in letting Colonel Tom Price escape
the operation of Gen. Hutton’s cast-iron “age
regulation.” Regulations are made to be en-
forced ; and, if one man is to be exempt from
their operation for no better reason than that
some person (unnamed) has thought fit to “under-
stand ” that he “ was to remain for two years,” dis-
cipline is at an end.
That colossal fraud, Pastor Worthington,
mployed a maid each for his three small infants,
averring that “every tender human flower”
requires individual cultivation. He would have
no “ crude personality ” about the children, and
made it his own care to select fitting attendants.
He ought to have been a fairly good judge of a
woman, crude or polished. In revenge for her
questioning as to the truth of the Maoriland stories
about him, Worthington showed a lady of his con-
gregation an anonymous and peculiarly vile letter,
which he said had been sent to him about her.
The lady immediately suspected that her revered
pastor had written it, and kept the letter, though
he assured her that “ the only place for such filth
is the fire.” Two or three days before his arrest
Worthington wrote asking her to return it; bub
she refused his request, not knowing what hanky-
panky he might have in contemplation.
Clement Wragge has just arranged fora
lecturing tour of Australia and Maoriland. If
Wragge gets some level-headed person to edit his
discourses there should be money in it, for
thousands would like to see the quaint, wild-
whiskered personage who holds communion with
the clouds and sends along prose poems about the
cyclonic disturbance called Torrey and the gentle
rain from Heaven that goes by the name of
Melba.
Kyrle Bellew is flourishing his interesting
personality in print again, and tells naively of the
rough time he had in an Australian township
when he and a mate were saved from starvation
by the timely arrival of a fever epidemic.
“Curly” started a private graveyard, and he
and his mate made a pile by burying the dead
and reading the service over them. Kyrle
always Bellew his own trumpet, and he has a good
advertiser’s contempt for fact.
Late Rev. James Benvie, Presbyterian
minister of West Maitland, was canny, ye ken.
His estate was valued at close on £lO,OOO, and in
his will he counselled his wife to claim the
annuity due to her from the Presbyterian Widows’
Fund, “ like any other'minister’s widow.” The
document further instructed his wife and daugh-
ters, the two beneficiaries, to settle by friendly
arbitration any differences of opinion: either of
them going to law was to forfeit £3OO out of the
estate.
Although the Anglican dioceses of Mel-
bourne and Adelaide were both established in
1847 (Newcastle, N.S.W., bishopric was founded
on the same day), and each has had three bishops,
only one child (Dr. Harmer’s) has been born to
the six prelates. Dr. Perry, Dr. Moorhouse and
Dr. Coe (Melbourne), and Dr. Short and Dr.
Kennion (Adelaide) were all childless, with the
exception of Dr. Short, and his family came into
the world ere he was raised to the episcopal bench.
Dr. Clarke has several children.

Premier of Tasmania, is only 83 (as stated in 8.,
25/10/’O2), he surely cannot be, as also stated
there, father of the fine old lawyer lately killed in
Adelaide. The age of the S.A. Villeneuve was a

derfully “well-preserved” look; but common

very well to say that Smith, of Tasmania, can’t be

The numerous (apparently female) com-
mentators upon Pastor Worthington’s facial
beauty have in all cases failed to notice the size
of that slim sensualist’s ears. They are prodigious
appendages, extremely long and narrow, and
perched more on a level with the mouth than the
eyes. Rightly or wrongly, some criminologists
assume that ears of this sort are characteristic of
depravity. Also, Worthington hasn’t an im-
pressive forehead, and shorn by the prison barber,
is _ said to make one of the most pronounced
’riminal types to be found in Pentridge.
An elderly Sydneyite of Great Importance
recently had a deplorable experience in a suburban
barber’s shop. Wandering in late one night he
flopped into the chair, grunted “shave,” closed
his eyes, and fell into a troubled snooze from which
he soon awoke with a smothered yell—to behold a
wild-eyed barber who clutched him firmly by the
nose with one hand and brandished a razor in
the other. The elderly gentleman hastily spat
out a mouthful of soap, struggled, choked and
gasped, “ Leggo, dammit! leggo ! ” The barber’s
grip on his distinguished ancient’s nose tightened
as he stared scornfully at his snorting victim
“ You fool !” he gurgled, “ carn’t y’see—if I leggo
—l'll fall!”
Up to now, Tom Mann has not been
appreciated in Australia at half his true worth.
Ben Tillett had twice the audiences everywhere ;
yet there seems to be much more solid stuff in
Mann; he is more accurate, and personally
seems more lovable. Little Ben Tillett laid
down the law; Mann is modest and deferen-
tial, and asks _ questions, and when he ex-
presses an opinion about things this end,
asks you not to think him “saucy”—a
favorite word of his. So far Australia’s failure
to conserve its water strikes him as about the
most conspicuous thing on this continent. In his
speeches there is good arrangement; but he
hasn t Tillett’s word-painting power. From the
popular view point, the best of them is his por-
traiture of t’other-end Labor leaders.
Mrs. Elizabeth Scott, the first woman
hanged in Victoria (in the ’6o’s), was convicted of
murdering her husband with the assistance of two
paramours. As one of these was a negro, she
could scarcely be expected to be a woman of
superfine sensibilities; yet what bothered her
most, after her sentence at Beechworth, was that
the step of the conveyance, ready to take her from
the courthouse, was so high as to expose her ankles
to the gaze of the crowd. She jibbed stubbornly
against entering the vehicle in the fashion pro-
vided, and, fearing a, “scene” if an attempt were
made to bustle her in forcibly, the police had to
borrow a box from a neighboring shop to allow her
to start for the condemned cell with her skirts un-
disturbed.
CAPTAIN VALLANGE,
Vic. Governor Clarke’s private secretary.
Ex-N.S.W. M.L.C. Cornrie, who died the
other day, was a very good philanthropist of the
Y.M.C. A. order, but not the judge of books many
people suppose. For a bushinan—and his way of
living at Kurrajong was bushmanlike—his tastes
might be called bookish ; but whether in pur-
chasing or donating, he looked more to quantity
and binding than to quality of contents. Although
60 years in Sydney or thereabouts, his idea of
literature was of the Scotch-catechism order, and
if he patronised fiction it was that of the numerous
females sweated by the English tract societies.
His politics were also arid, though well-meaning.
“Boheme ” : Be the Fergus Hume “ Old
Man of the Sea ” story. I knew Hume well.
He, his brother Jim, and Phil. Beck lived in
East Melbourne, and Mary Hume, who was a
singer, kept house for the lot. One night, at a
supper, Hume announced to a band of friends
that he was writing a book. The announcement
was received with good-natured derision, but
Fergus, nothing abashed, proceeded to give a
brief outline of the plot of the “ Hansom Gab ”
yarn. “ And,” he wound up, with his character-
istic lisp, “I’m going to put on the local color
with a spade.” He did, too.
“ Saw one-time Sydney pressman Donald
Murray’s typewriting telegraph,” writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, “working
perfectly to Edinburgh. G-.P.0. officials say it
has been a great success and will be universally
used. It means sacking of operators, for a mere
typewritist can run it, and one skilled man in a
room of 50 instruments will be all the trained
assistance needed. Murray is on the road to for-
tune. He has wisely taken no partners, the
United States have bought the local rights, and
England, Austria, Germany and France are in
treaty. Another score for the Kangaroo.”
George Higinbotham impressed the public
imagination of Victoria as none other of its
statesmen has ever done. His name is never
mentioned now but with a respect bordering on
reverence. Victoria cherishes towards Higin-
botham at least some of the feeling which
Americans entertain towards George Washington.
He was a lawyer by accident rather than by bent
of natural character. As Aegus editor he lacked
adaptability, and was hopelessly unpractical. As
a Judge, he was not a great Judge, but he eom-
manded'the affection and reverence of all about
him. Of his memory little remains now but
splendid obscurity.
Curious N. Queensland advertisement : —<
NOTICE.
'LI ROM this date I resume practice at the Bar.
Seven years ago I relinquished my practice in
Court chiefly because I was tempted to exaggerate
my client’s side of the case, and to suppress a favor-
able point on the side of my opponent. From the
light I had I am convinced that I then and since
acted wisely. Believing now, however, that by
Divine aid 1 can overcome those defects of character,
and that cases arise where truth and justice demand
all the advocacy they can get, I now see it to
be my duty to return to active practice just as I
then saw it to be my duty to relinquish it. I pub-
lish this explanation in justice to myself, and for the
information of my friends and the public.
ALEXANDER COSTELLO.
Charters Towers,
16th October, 1902.
The many friends of B. E. Minns will be
sorry to hear that he writes from England that
the last operation performed on him was not a
success, and that he will have to undergo yet
another—the third in a year.
The Booth-Clibborns (daughter and son-
in-law of Prophet Booth) who lately abjured the
church and the red jersey of the parental humbug,
and sought refuge with Dowie, have found the
new fraud no more satisfying than the old one.
They have quitted Zion, and are now going round
in search of a creed that can stand inspection.
Rev. Matthew Walker, the Lucknow
(N.S.W.) clergyman who was some nonths ago
sentenced to six months’ imprisonment for per-
jury in connection with a libel case, was last week
sent up for two months extra by Acting-Judge
Hamilton for contempt of court in refusing to
give evidence in a case connected with his own.
Walker had had enough of swearing.
What a long, slow animal is tne Law!
One Brown had an action for slander against
a N.S.W. Life Assurance Co. At first trial a ver-
dict for plaintiff with damages. Appeal, then a
new trial, at which plaintiff again got verdiot
with increased damages. Appeal again, and new
trial at which plaintiff again succeeded, with again
increased damages (£650). Appeal for new trial
refused. Plaintiff therefore (barring the prospect
of a Privy Council appeal which, however, is
threatened) gets the fruits of his verdict, pro-
vided they haven’t all gone in expenses.
Price, M.L.A. for Gloucester, and hono-
rary Minister Bennett, of the adjoining elec-
torate of Durham, are rival N.S.W. forestry ex-
perts. A short time ago Mr. Price wrote a series
of articles on forests in the Evening News, and
Mr. Bennett politely acknowledged their merit in
Parliament:—
S >ine very valuable articles have recently appeared in
America on forestry matters. By a singular coincidence
the writers of these .articles appear to have pirated the
practical ideas and scientific knowledge of the hon.
member for Gloucester. When he saw Mr. Price’s articles
in the News he felt sorry to think that the hon. member
had been anticipated by those American pirates.
N.S. W. Works Committee is a more or less
effective safeguard against things being done as
the local politician wants, and not as the officers
advise; but in connection with works under
£20,000 there is no such check upon the Minister,
as small works don’t go before the Committee. It
would be interesting to know how many times the
Owe’Sullivan has rejected the advice of his
officers and acted •according to the whisperings of
the local politician. As the smallest possible pre-
caution, every public work which does not come
under the scrutiny of the Public Works Com-
mittee should be paid for solely out of revenue.
This item appears in the report of the
Queensland Agricultural Department for the year
just closed :
Though Queensland nas agricultural capabilities supe-
rior to those of any other State of the Commonwealth,
the superiority seems insufficient to countervail the effects
of her greater distance from the London market and the
greater difficulty her producer has in reaching the most
profitable customers.
The Courier heads this “a Confession of Failure.”
It is rather a damning indictment of the Free-
trade doctrine that the Courier professes.
Queensland requires to send its produce to London
mostly to pay for the boots, clothes, etc., it buys
there—-and it seems that it can’t do this profitably
owing to the distance. Therefore, let it make the
boots, clothes, etc., in its own towns, and send
its produce to supply operatives there instead ol
in London—and the problem of distance vanishes,
and the enormous cost of sea freight is saved, and
the difficulty which wrecks Queensland’s agri-
culture is solved. If Queensland can’t grow grain
and send it profitably to the weaver and boot-
maker in England, then the natural course is tc
fetch the weaver and bootmaker to Queensland.

RANDY.
Aboriginalities.
To Contributors. — In the case o/ ethnological and
natural history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience ; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific
value.
Australia’s champion buckjumper is un-
doubtedly “Dargin’s Grey,” an outlaw bred at
Capertee, N.S.W., who, last month, at Brisbane,
easily hoisted both contestants in a match for
championship honors. For years past he has
thrown every self-styled champion who tried to
ride him. One who did sit him was the late
Harry Morant (“ The Breaker ”) at Hawkesbury
Show ; but Dargin had already unseated 16 alleged
champions, and was about knocked up.
*
Out-back many good stockmen believe the
Australian drought is producing a sort of drought-
resistant sheep Anyhow, they say they can take
risks now which, a few years ago, they dared not.
Mounted on bikes, Tapio (far west N.S.W.) men
lately, in hot weather, drove 4000 sheep a hard
four days without water. And on Birdsville (Q.)
track, coming down to Adelaide, S. Field got 8000
wethers over a hundred-mile dry stage in 93 hours.
Sid. Kidman, to whom the stock belonged, and
who gives the figures, believes this latter a record
—an i there are not many men who know more
than he about what has been done on the dry
stock-roads.
» * * »
“ Rypo ” : How long can a sheep live
without food or water ? Last summer, in Western
Vic., I saw a sheep which had been blinded and
half-roasted by a bushfire, lying in a hole for
about 10 days before it died. I passed it daily in
the train, miles from any station, so could not put
a merciful end to the miserable creature.
» * * •
To Menindie, on the lower Darling, where
ten years ago or so a company built preserving
and boiling-down works to get rid of the surplus
stock, they are positively now carrying meat by
parcels-post. The works ran for only two years,
and then at but half-pressure.

The Barrier silverfield, having got within
sight of its 20th birthday, gathered up, the other
day, the remnants of its still-resident old-timers.
It wasn’t a particularly “influential gathering,”
and when, the talk over, the chairman suggested
a shilling in and a shout, it took a good many of

them some prospecting to find even that modest
slug of silver. All those who made anything out
of their hard pioneering, and had the luck to keep
it, have also sense enough to live somewhere else ;
those who didn’t are mostly either dead-beat now
or keep a pub. Of those at the meeting—abso-
lutely the oldest hand —he has had over 19 years
of it —now earns a precarious two-drinks-and-a-
sandwich by bell-ringing at auction sales.

A drought item :—“ Last season four sheds
in Trangie (N.S.W.) district shore 290,000 sheep ;
but this season they only put through 87,000,
showing a loss of 203,000. One shed alone lost
92,000, and another shed had no shearing at all.”
» * * *
Drought-losses (since 1900) on Gregory
(Q.) district stations, as set out in Charleville
Times : Mt. Margaret, 7500 cattle, 86 000 sheep,
319 horses. Congie, 1857 cattle, 117 horses.
Kihee, 8944 cattle, 174 horses. Kynbra, 4500
cattle, 35,633 sheep, 310 horses. Thylungra,
13,847 cattle, 473 horses. Keerongoohoo, 6380
cattle, 22,250 sheep, 200 horses. Galway Downs,
6226 cattle, 146 horses. Mt. Hewitt, 14,500 cattle.
Nickatunga, 27,987 cattle. On Currawella sta-
tion, beyond Windorah, there were 3.35 in. of rain
in 15 months, and cattle died like flies, and their
bodies lay round the bog holes as close together
as the keys of a piano. In one waterhole there
were counted over 100 beasts in a similar number
of yards, and down tlie Cooper, in one nf the long
waterholes, upwards of 1000 beasts lay dead within
the mile ; there was practically a total extinction
of the herds.
* * * *
Be that alleged shooting of aborigines some
weeks ago in Cape York Peninsula, Attorney-
General Rutledge minutes that he is “perfectly
satisfied that the deaths of at least two (and
probably four) aborigines, whose remains were
found, were caused by the native troopers who
accompanied the constable.” The Home Secretary
has since minuted that the constable be discharged
and the troopers not allowed to return North.
Murdering niggers is a cheap pastime.
• * * *
The administration of the Q. Aborigines’
Protection Act, under Home Secretary Foxton
and Dr. Roth, went through the ordeal of a Par-
liamentary attack during the passage of the Esti-
mates. Rumors of a practical break-down in the
administration of the Act had been flying round,
and it was said that Dr. Roth was in for a bad
time. Asa matter of fact the Northern Pro-
tector came out of the ordeal with flying colors.
The Opposition supported him almost unani-
mously; theGovernmentmembers(with oneexcep-
tion) applauded his administration ; and the House
applauded vociferously the statement that Home
Secretary Foxton, whatever his faults might be,
had battled strenuously and honestly on behalf of
the aborigines generally, and the poor nigger
slaves of the beche-de-mer men in particular. It
was high time somebody interfered on bshalf of
the Northern aborigine, for, as a rule, he is fed
like a brute, kicked like a dog, worked like a
horse, and left to die at last by the roadside.
• * * •
“Boko”: When a poor devil of a fruit-
hawker works a horse that’s got a bit of a sore
back the Animals Protection people drop on him
sharp. But when are they going to prosecute the
station-owner whose over-stocking causes the most
horrible miseries to thousands of sheep ? In one
case at Orange, recently, of 2000 sheep left in
yard overnight, 1300 were dead in the morning.
They were freshly shorn, and had been trucked
from the warm Western lands to bitter cold
Orange. What about looking up the people con-
cerned in that horror for a start ?
• • • •
Alleged that the so-called “bunyip” has
been seen several times recently in the Murrum-
bidgee River, N.S.W. The description given of
the creature accords pretty well with various
previous accounts, which seems to indicate the
existence in Australia of a hitherto unclassified
aquatic mammal, of moderate dimensions, and of
a character generally unsuited to the prevalent
notions of the mysterious monster of aboriginal
tradition.
* * * *
A young man named Attrill had a re-
markably close call t’other day on Deep
Creek-Casino (N.S.W.) road. _ He was riding
leisurely along, smoking his pipo, when a dead
tree, without warning, fell across the horse’s neck
and killed it instantly. A branch struck the
pipe in Attrill’s mouth, breaking it off, besides
fracturing his left arm. The rebound threw the
dead horse across the tree and pinned the man
under its carcase. His coo-ees brought a neigh-
bour to the rescue, and mounting another horse,
he rode on to Casino.
• • • •
Said a man of (bare) acres, recently : “In
’95 I had seven stacks of straw, which would to-
day be worth over £I6OO. Couldn’t see ahead
worth a cent, and didn’t save them. Easy to keep
straw wholesome and clean for a lifetime, but I
didn’t bother, and rain got in and rotted them.
Farmers for miles around lost theirs in same way.
Straw not in demand then at our distance from
market, and we weren’t pressed for feed. Never
once thought of putting the wheat-leavings by.
Burnt mountains. Couldn’t see gold in anything
bar bags of corn.” Then the train for Gippsland,
with his “ starvers ” aboard, moved out.

“ A.P.R.” : Recently saw what is appar-
ently a cross of cat and rabbit. Body, head, and
fur of a cat, but hindlegs exactly those of a bunny.
Tail about £in. long ; gait and reclining posture
those of a rabbit. Forelegs pointed in middle,
lower part resting on ground and turning inwards,
The claws could grasp like a cat’s, but could not
retain their grip. Color jet-black. The animal
is carnivorous, and mews like the ordinary feline.
[The Australian Museum people would no doubt
like to see this alleged hybrid. Hitherto no in-
stance is on record of hybridisation between a
carnivorous and a non-carnivorous animal. Present
scribe has often heard of a cat-’possum cross, but
has great doubts of the possibility ; and, in any
case,* the ’possum is multivorous.]
* • » *
Cattle-duffing is very prevalent in Central
Queensland just now. The C.Q. cattle-duffers are
a calm, cool, collected race, who will tell you
brazenly how many cattle they have “duffed ” in
the last 12 months, at the very moment that they
give you your own beef to eat while the billy is
boiling. Every man of them is a splendid bushman
with a fine contempt for the police and trackers.
“O.”: Undoubted indications of volcanic
activity exist within some hitherto little known
caves near Mandurama (N.S.\V.). inese
caves contain both hot and cold springs,
and in some of the lower compartments
the air is quite hot, in others warm,
in others cold. These caves are not
far distant from the Canobl isMoun-
tain, a formerly active volcano.
DOWN FROM THE MALLEE.
SMALL BOY (watching the street-sprinkler in
Collins-street): “Holy smoke! what’s that
there fool wastin’ his water for? You couldn’t
grow burrs in land like that!”
At Geelong, the other day, two Syrians were
charged with having eight live sparrows attached
to two sticks, to prove the adhesive qualities of
the “real Turkish bird-lime” they were selling.
It was urged, in their defence, that sparrows were
vermin, for the capture of which bonuses were
officially offered, and it would, therefore, be ridi-
culous to penalise defendants. One of the Syrians
informed the Bench, as the case was dismissed,
that in 19 days lie had caught 12,000 sparrows for
the Maffra Shire, Gippsland. How about sparrow-
pie?

“Glenara” : Yefc another addition to the
shearing-shed subscription-curse. A Victorian
squatter’s wife, in a letter to the Argus, suggests,
as a “happy idea,” that shearers and shed-hands
be invited to subscribe to the “Distressed Mallee
Farmers’ Fund.” Seeing how many calls the
shearer has to meet —hospitals, crippled mates,
orphaned children, shearers’ races, station cricket-
club, etc. —the squatteress in question would be
better employed trying to get the southern land-
holders to reduce the exorbitant rents they are
asking from these same “distressed Mallee far-
mers ” for horse and cattle pastures.

“F.H.P.S." : Be red snow on Mt. Buller
Vie. (B. 18/10/’O2). I think rain brought down
the red dust and discolored the snow, instead of
the snow bringing down the dust with it. I
was at Mts. Feathertop and Hotham (Baldy)
within the past fortnight, and snow which
had fallen during the winter, and was
still lying on the south side of the
mountains was also discolored with red
dust, blown from the Riverina and
brought down by rain. Whilst cycling
along the road past Mt. Buffalo, I
noticed a peculiar mist which almost
obscured the ranges, and was told by
residents that this was N.S.W. dust
that had been hanging about for
weeks.
Hailstorms are the latest curse of the
N.S.W. agriculturist. T’other day, for the third
successive year, a hailstorm devastated portion of
Junee district, and in an hour all that remained
of £IO,OOO worth of crops and orchards was a little
chaff and numerous tree-stumps. Corrugated*
iron-roofs were riddled, gum-trees completely
stripped, sheep, horses and cattle maimed and
killed, whilst dead birds of every variety strewed
the ground for miles. Now, here is a chance for
Wragge and his vaunted Stiger-Vortex guns 1

H. Stockdalc, re sheep-slaying horses :
Many otherwise quiet horses, if well-fed and “ above
themselves,” will chase and attack sheep, calves, dogs,
etc., biting, kicking, and striking-down with the fore-
feet. This, however, is due less to vice than to friskiness
from high-feeding and short work. Some of the quietest
horses—especially those reared as pets-will “stick-up”
(by dint of kicking and striking with their forefeet) a
person carrying them their feed, and thus compel it to
be given up to them, unless the bearer has a whip.
“ Maiuoose” : 7?e persistency of suicides.
I knew a man up Croydon (Q.) way whose first
attempt was throwing himself off a hotel
balcony—result a simple shaking. His
second essay was down an 80ft. shaft
(in which I was backing him), but
a few months’ hospital patched
him up. His next effort was
to blow the top of his head
off with dynamite, and this
being the “lucky ” third time,
his success was complete.

The experiment of travel-
ling sheep across from S. Aus-
tralia to the Westralian gold-
fields has lately been tried
with only fair results. The
sheep, 1820 of them, were sent
from A. G. Downer’s Nan-
warra station, with five whites,
helped by four blacks, in
charge. In the sandy desert
country on the S.A. side of
Eucla 700 were dropped; after
that few troubles were struck
and only another 30 or 40 fell
out. But when the fields were
reached the trouble was to find
“free” buyers, and in the end
the lot had to go for 7s. Id. a
head. The trip covered just five
months.
About nine miles from
Mandurama (N.S.W.), on
the Blayney-Harden line,
there is a stream known as
Up-Hill Creek. Limestone
Creek, a mile-and-a-half
distant, and crossed by the
Canowindra - Mandurama -
road, runs almost due
North, whilst Up - Hill
Creek, crossed by the same
road, runs due South. The Canowindra-Man-
durama mail-driver wins many a wager made
with passengers, upon approaching the creek, as
to the direction in which the stream runs.

At Tibooburra (N.S.W.), recently, died
Fat Tommy, probably the “heftiest” nigger in
Australia. Tommy weighed between 18 and 20st.
—2O per cent, of which was an old mother
hydatid, and Heaven only knows how many thou-
sand daughters. Tommy was famed for his appe-
tite ; he could eat 61b. of sheep at a sitting.
* • * *
Marsupial hunting in Bananaland is a
profitable pursuit just now. Young Hupeden, a
well-known local shot, has accounted for 22,150
this year between Rodd’s Bay and Turkey, making
upwards of £5 per week. The shooters have no
fixed camp, but travel straight on from early
morning, often covering 30 miles before night.
Bears form the biggest part of the bag, wallabies
and ’roos being met with only at intervals.

Country newspaper report:
One of the most fashionable and interesting weddings that
has taken place in Lismore (N.S.W.). ... A royal visitor
was present, too, in the person of Benghi, the aboriginal
king of Wyrallah, and he had the honor of being photo-
graphed with the wedding group, being a specially old
friend of the family.

“F.S.S.": “Banjo,” dis-
coursing of dogs, doesn’t em-
phasise the distinction
between the “yard” sheep-
dog and the “track” sheep-
dog. The chief qualification
of the former is his bark,
whereas it is a grievous mis-
demeanor for the latter to
open his mouth. A first-class
drover’s dog, indeed, works in
absolute silence. I once in-
tentionally exasperated a
drover (who had been lauding
his dog sky-high), by asking
“Does he bark well?”
Bark ?” he exclaimed. ‘‘ If
he whimpered once I’d hang
him to the first tree.’’
“ Banjo’s” final warning not-
withstanding, how is this for
a “faithfulness” record:
Near Mortlake, (Vic.), three
years ago, a drover was caught
by a bush-fire, and had to leave
his sheep. But his dog stayed
with them, and, after the fire
had passed, the brave little
bitch limped home, driving
the remnant of the flock,
with her feet blistered and
most of her hair burnt off.
Her owner tended her like a
sick child, and in a few weeks
set her on her feet again.
* * *
‘ ‘ Banco ” : Re Murray-
cod nesting. The nest
mounds described by your
correspondent are made by
the jew or catfish of the inland
waters. Jew spawn from Dec.
to Feb. They guard tl icir
nests, swimming around them
day and night. Any foreign
substance thrown on the nest
is removed—a small bulk by
the mouth, and a largo bulk
by means of the nose. A
jew’s nest contains coarse
gravel in centre and fine
sand on the edge.
The cod spawns in deep
sheltered water, generally on
a mud bottom.

A horse that died recently at Karyrik,
Vic., was found to have in the “second stomach ”
801 b. of sand; presumed to have been gradually
taken-in with the grass-roots for which the animal
had been burrowing. Other horses in the district
are similarly affected.
* * * *
In the grounds of Bishopscourt (Melb.),
which it is proposed to remove from its present
site, stands a venerable gum tree which, on the
authority of the late Baron Von Mueller, was a
sapling when King Edward VI. put forth the
prayers of the Church in the vernacular in the
year 1549, and local members of the Anglican
Church regard it as almost sacrilege to lay the axe
to its roots in the Coronation year of King
Edward VII.

man in his employ, up Tumberumba way, who
upended a bridge corbel (13ft. long, Gift, of it 12
x 12 -and the rest 14 x 12), got it on his shoulder
single-handed, and carried it some 50yds. This
was green urabbi, which is heavier than ironbark
and was reckoned little short of half a ton. This
man stood 6ft. lin., and was the smallest of five
brothers. His mother died soon after his birth
and as he was brought up on artificial food, he
used to excuse his midget proportions by telling
people that “he was reared a poddy.”
Many Vic. dairy-farmers, who
installed milking-machines at consider-
able expense, are proving them costly
failures,” writes a Bulletin correspon-
dent. “It is found that after a short
time the cows take a dislike to the
machines and refuse to give down more
milk than they can help. Hand-trials
alongside the machines have given 33
per cent, better results.”
* * * *
It is time that these large sums
of money which the public-house man
says he lent to the drunken prospector
or shearer were made irrecoverable at
law. As a case in point, a Kalgoorlie
miner, after a decent little crushing, had
a four days’ celebration, and, by his
own account, saw snakes for a week
after. Then the snakes were scared
away by a summons for £32 for board,
lodging, and money lent. Why pub.-
lieepers should lend money, without
acknowledgment and without witnesses,
to men who are too drunk to remember
that they got it, is a problem. But, by
them own account, they are always doing
foolish things like that.

Estimated that one Queensland
district alone —the Peak Downs—con-
sumes over £2OOO worth of opium per
annum. Official report dwells upon the
way m which the opium habit is spread-
ing among the whites in W.Q. This is
attributed to the “ total absence of all
cheerful social conditions, and the
dreary, deadly monotony of the environ-
ment.”

There is lying in the hands of
Queensland Government (according to
the last report on Pacific Island Immi-
gration) a sum of £36,773 Bs. 6d., de-
rived from the estates of deceased Islanders.
There is also much distress just now in the Isis
district among the unemployed “free” Poly-
nesians. Would it (asks a correspondent) be un-
reasonable to suggest that the fund above men-
tioned might be made a means of relief for these
unfortunates ?

Tests, running over four years, show that
at Broken Hill waterworks sun and wind suck
up an annual 7ft. l|in. The loss in smaller station
tanks and dams is much greater. At theß.H.
works a loss of half-an-inc*h a day is common in
summer; the consumption never reaches one-
seventh of that.
ENCORE! ENCORE!!
A REFINED DIVERSION.
“Mr. Irvine and his colleagues can be trusted to decide
upon their duty, in the Sangal case, without either help
or hindrance from Premier See. The less guilty has
perished on the scaffold and the more guilty still sur-
vives.”—ARGUS.
Re a par. (8., l/11/’O2) as to Mr. W.
Clarke, a N.S.W. ex-Minister for Justice, The
Bulletin is informed by his brother in Sydney
that Mr. Clarke is a confirmed invalid, and,
though in S’Africa, is not “ running a boarding-
house ” there.
“ J.8.M.” writes :
/ have read the Witton petition which has been posted
to some of us ; also your remarks thereon (B. Oct. 18).
Have also talked to dozens of returned contingenters on
matter, and I find this curious state of things. In S’Africa
it was difficult to find anyone who did not applaud the
numerous Boer-murdering stories which were current,
and the man who suggested that the Boers were deserving
of ordinary humane treatment was looked upon as a
freak. But now, in talking to returned soldiers in Aus-
tralia, I have not come across one who does not admit that
the Bushveldt crowd, Witton included, deserved ail they
got. The only comment oil their sentence I have heard
is that they should have all been hanged instead of being
shot or imprisoned for life. The Bushveldters, they say,
“ went too far.” All honor to Mr. Witton for his efforts
to release his unfortunate brother. But we who know
what went on in S’Africa are also entitled to form a judg-
ment. We know that hundreds of Boers have been mur-
dered in cold blood. One of the worst gangs of murderers
was brought to justice. Dozens of other gangs have
escaped scot free, and the highly-placed officers who
encouraged these proceedings, which will be an eternal
blot on the British name, are being honored. Is this any
reason for sympathy with those who have been laid by the
heels, or shall we forgive them for committing some of
the most atrocious and debased crimes of the day because
they thought they would not get into trouble over them ?
I do not propose to traverse the petition (to my mind a
string of fallacies and sDecious arguments). I would only
say that the plea that Witton was directly responsible for
the killing of one Boer climaxes the matter. A row of
surrendered Boers are about to be murdered—by soldiers,
forsooth, to whose honor they trusted—in cold blood.’
One of the unfortunate victims, absolutely unarmed,
knowing what is about to happen, rushes out to get in a
last blow before he is killed. The “amiable young giant”
draws his revolver and shoots the Boer. Enough 1 If we
are to turn loose any of the Bushveldt murderers, then
let us at once revise our standards of right and wrong,
and let us release all our murderers.

They heard, though the blue heaven shut
Her far dull ear,
And languid-eyed the sick earth reel’d
Round the tired year.
They heard, these common toiling men,
Listened and heard,
Diviner than the heavens, helped
With deed and word.
They heard, who from the forest smoothed
A smiling land,
Where the sleek gracious flocks
Knee-covered stand.
They heard, for oh ! the human heart
Is more divine
Than the cold heavens set
In fixed star-shine.
They heard, for in a kindred toil
Kinship is born,
Sympathy is the heart’s sweat
And they that mourn—
They hear more quickly sobs
From other throats,
And, like .Eolian harps,
Thrill at the notes.
They heard, though the strange heaven shut
Her cold, dull ear;
They are the gods in truth
Who help and hear.
M.E.F.

Owing to the Australian and S’African
demand for live cattle, the canning and chilling
works projected by Smalcolm M'Eacharn and
friends in north Westralia are not being pushed
just now. It is said that no less than 3600 heifers
have lately been shipped from that district to
Durban, with a loss of only 200, mainly through
the use of a double keel; and other lots follow.
Price, from £ll to £lB.
Indigestion from
Birth
“My little girl can now digest
anything ”
One of the most difficult of home prob-
lems is how to get food to suit the baby—it
is twice as difficult if the child has weak-
ness of the digestive organs. Here is the
solution in both oases :
“ Nelson Street, Whittington Moor,
“ Chesterfield, England, March 28th, 1901.
“ Dear Sirs, —My daughter, Olive, aged
two and a half years, had suffered from
acute indigestion from birth and caused us
great trouble and anxiety. She has cost
me pounds for various remedies that have
been tried, but all proved of no avail, for
nothing suited her or made her thrive. Her
food was rejected almost as soon as taken,
and the poor little thing suffered great
distress. We almost gave up all hope of
rearing her, and knowing how she suffered,
her release would almost have been a
OLIVE SMITH.
(From a photograph.)
blessing. I decided to try Scott’s Emulsion.
I was much surprised and greatly delighted
to notice a radical change for the better in
my child’s condition shortly after giving
her your preparation. She was able to
take and retain her food, and what is more,
it certainly appeared to do her good, for
she soon gained in both flesh and strength.
I have now given Scott’s Emulsion a fair
trial, and am thankful to say my little
girl has been completely cured and can
now eat and digest anything, and I must
give Scott’s Emulsion the credit. I feel
it is not too much to say that we owe her
life to your splendid remedy.—Yours very
sincerely,
“ (Signed) Chas. Smith.”
You know enough of the ebb and flow of
health in children to be aware that the child
is going on well when it “ can eat and digest
anything.” You will have noticed from the
day the child was born that it was to the
question of food the doctor paid the most
attention. It is in just such cases as these
—impaired digestion—that Scott’s Emul-
sion is of the first importance.
It acts always as it did in the case of
Olive Smith : as a medicine in removing the
cause of the indigestion, as a food in adding
flesh and strength. This double action of
Scott’s Emulsion, unique among remedies,
is just what all substitutes and imitations
fail to secure. "You can therefore realise
the difference between Scott’s Emulsion
which cures and “ other things ” which
always produce “ other results.” There is
nothing “ just as good ” as Scott’s Emulsion.
Scott’s Emulsion has none of the ob-
jectionable features of common cod-liver
oil, is taken by children with eagerness.
Comparing common cod-liver oil with
Scott’s Emulsion a distinguished physician
writes us : “ Your Emulsion i area times
as efficacious as a similar quantity of
common cod-liver oil.”
•On the wrapper of every bottle of Scott’s
Emulsion is a picture of a man carrying a
big fish—if he and the fish are absent it jo
not Scott’s Emul= !^r>
You can obtain a sample of Scott’s Emulsion
by sending fourpenee to Scott & Bowne, Ltd.
483 Kent street, Sydney, and mentioning this
paper. All chemists sell Scott’s Emulsion.

The Fitzgibbon, as boss of Vic. Metropolitan
Board of Works, has made £13,000 in two years
out of the sheep bred and fed on the Board’s farm
at Werribee. He formerly dabbled in pastoralism
on his own account.
The most lovely concern in Bananaland
just now is the tank-engine scandal. Something
like £100,000 has been spent on strengthening
lines and bridges to bear these white elephants ;
platforms have had to be altered, and, in spite of
all the expense, these miraculous machines can
only run on a small section of the Southern lines,
and can’t run off on most sidings at all.
Writes “ Medi-cus ” from the Far West:
Who is going to pay the newspaper postage—the paper
people or the subscriber? I have received notice from
the proprietary of my daily paper that the price is to be
raised 4s. a year, owing to the imposition of postage. I
fail to see where that paper’s contribution to the revenue
comes in. Just take notice that I do not intend to pay
any postage on The Bulletin. I’ll stop the paper first
and then, let me ask you, what would you do ?
Well, the paper people will try to pass it on ; an ,
if their paper is good enough, they’ll succeed.
The daily papers howled against newspaper pc st-
age probably because they were uneasy on the
point. The Bulletin didn’t howl —why every-
one is at liberty to guess.
Old friend John Alexander Dowie is pro-
viding further matter for humorous reflection.
J.A.D., in his character of the New Elijah,
instructed a private disciple to pass over all his
worldly goods to the Zion Church. The original
Elijah, of yore, was at liberty to make converts
on the same terms, because he lived in an age of
Faith, when prophets were secure from news
paper exposures. But Elijah Dowie has to suffe-
tribulation that his historical namesake never
dreamed of. In this case the disciple’s next-ofr
kin applied to the Supreme Court of Chicago for
restoration of their deceased relative’s property,
and the court ordered Elijah to disgorge £10,000.
Re that monkey and weight (B. 25/10/’02) :
“ Lychnobite ” : This problem, according to Oolling-
wood’s “Life of Lewis Carroll,” was invented by the
author of “ Alice in Wonderland.’’ In Carroll’s diary
appears—“Got Prof. Clifton’s answer to the monkey anil
weight problem. It is very curious, the different views
taken by good mathematicians. Price says the weight
goes up, with increasing velocity ; Clifton (and Harcourt)
that it goes up at the same rate as the monkey ; while
Sampson says that it goes down.”
“P.T.”: To “J.S.W.’s” question (B., 25/10/’02) about
the rope and the monkey. Some hold that the weight
goes up at the same rate as the monkey ; others contend
that it goes down ; and others again maintain that the
weight rises or falls in inverse ratio to the speed with
which the monkey jerks the rope. My own opinion is
that the balance between the monkey and the weight
being very sensitive, the monkey, in climbing, will, by
his pulls, represent additional weight, causing the rope
to slide over the pulley-wheel, and thus increasing the
weight on the monkey's side till the whole of the rope
runs over to that side.
“Alex. M.” : lie the monkey-rope-weight question.
The result, in my opinion, would depend upon the
monkey’s style of climbing. If he should mount with
jerks, the rope would certainly “ overhaul ” with him,
causing the weight proportionately to ascend ; but if he I
should climb with gentle continuity, even the small
resistance of the block-sheave to revolution upon its pin
might allow the weight to remain stationary.
“ Titus Salt” : The matter will never be settled except
by actual experiment. I have a very good monkey which
I will lend for £1 a week, the experimenter to feed the
animal and keep it in repair, and leave a £5 deposit. I
will also supply the rope and other fixings for £2 10s., less
6 per cent, discount for cash. If the experiments take
place at my house, I will expect £3 for rent and wear and
tear of premises ; if at the experimenter’s own house,
freight on monkey will be an extra.
John Mitchell, leader of the 150,000
American hard-coal miners recently on strike
for about four months, and who has managed
things so. well that he has got most of the
public opinion on his side, is a smooth-faced,
black-coated man of little more than 30, courteous,
and filled with the idea that honor, and especially
a punctilious regard for contracts, is a union’s
most valuable asset. “ You can win strikes on a
big balance,” he says, “ but you can’t fix up agree-
ments and prevent strikes, which is a long way
better, unless they trust you.” In most respects
his methods don’t differ from those of other young
Americans who have organised big industrial con-
cerns. He is little of a talker, and was not able
to persuade the men, as he wanted to, that they
were not ready for a strike ; but, having settled
that much for themselves, they left the rest to
their president. “ This is just a matter of busi-
ness,” he says, and sits hard on any orator who
starts to play on the strikers’ passions. Already
he has won for the soft-coal miners what he is
asking for the “ hard-coalers.”
“ Galvo ” to The Bulletin :—
According to Bulletin (ll/10/’02), Brisbane Tramways
Oo. asserts that there is not sufficient current in any
single conductor to kill a human, and the par then in-
stances a ’cuted bullock, and the narrow escape of a ’bus-
driver “with his foot on the metal brake-lever,” who was
about to brush aside a dangling live-wire “ with his bare
hand,” the inference being that, had he done so, he would
have been killed.
The Tram Co.’s statement is dangerously misleading,
but the ’busman, for all that, wasn’t in much danger. The
risk is neither lessened nor increased by the conducting-
wire being single, double, or treble; neither does it
matter whether the wire is thin or thick. The danger
depends upon the amount of current which can be driven
through the body, and this again depends upon area of
contact and efficiency of connection between the con-
ductor and the body. About two years ago a workman
was killed in England by a pressure of 250 volts (until
then considered absolutely safe), and it was shown at the
inquest that he made particularly good contact across
the wires. On the other hand, I myself have had two
shocks from a 600-volt circuit (the voltage of Brisbane
tramways), and didn’t suffer any great inconvenience,
though the experience is not recommended as an amuse-
ment. The sensation was that of much heat and a violent
blow over the heart, followed by profuse perspiration.
In the case of the bullock mentioned, the animal pro-
bably stood on the negative conductor (the tram-rail),
and put his moist nose against the positive conductor
(the broken trolley-wire), thus receiving enough current
to kill him. With the ’bus-driver, however, it was dif-
ferent, because, although his foot was on the metal
brake lever, the circuit to earth was broken by the brake
“ shoe ” of wood, leather, or rubber—all efficient non-
conductors when dry. I have often seen line-repairers,
standing on a dry, wood-blocked road, handle a “ live ”
wire with bare hands, and when perched on their repair-
waggon, repairers always do so handle them.
By-the-way, why is there never any prosecution of the
managers of electrical concerns which have had many
deaths from electrocution ? It is customary to employ al-
most utterly unskilled men on underground-cable work,
because experienced men won’t work for the 7s. to 9s. a
day! The actual work is generally simple and easy
enough—merely the soldering or splicing together of a
coupie of wires"; but the skill lies in knowing which two
wires to join, and too many of the dividend-hunting co.’s
would rather let a man be killed in finding out than pay
one whose knowledge would keep him safe.

Elizabeth-st., Melbourne, is the largest buyer of
Opals, Sapphires, Pearls and other Australian
gems, and consignors will find it to their advantage
to send their parcels direct to him. The firm has
a reputation for straight-dealing, extending over a
period of 40 years, which is a sufficient guarantee
that they will be honorably treated. A trial par
cel, which will be followed by direct remittance
is solicited.
