STDiTBY.
POLITICAL
POINTS
Westralia being gay and cheerful with big
loans just now, the Assembly has accepted,
almost unanimously, a motion in favor of old-age
pensions.
Australia is*gefcfcing on so very well without
a Governor-General (there is said to be an acting
Gov.-Gen. somewhere, however) that really the
offioe might be abolished, and each of the State
Governors asked, in turn, to be acting G.-G. for a
year.
The most convincing fact to the average
man in the day labor v. contract controversy in
N.S.W. is that the Railway Commissioners, from
choice, get very much of their construction and
deviation work carried out by day labor, and save
money thereby.
Under three Loan Application Bills which
the Irvine-Shiels Govt, has introduced Vic.
Leg. Assembly it is proposed to spend £1,073,850.
Most of this money is going to placate the Kya-
bram country interests that have been calling so
loudly for retrenchment.
Melbourneites already see the fall of the
Frozen Ministry ahead. Who will be the next
Premier? Peacock is discredited ; Trenwith still
somewhat unpopular; Duggan and Morrissey
not liked. Those who think they know, say
Andrews or Donald Mackinnon.
Agent-General Copeland says that the
High Commissionership is bound to be “some-
thing of a sinecure.” Federal members must see
that it is not. No man should be accredited and
salaried by Australia to play kneebreeches,
swallowtails and pumps in England.
“Maitlander” writes to Sydney D.T.
(wonder whether the letter came by posit ?) a sort
of a don’t-nail-his-ears-to-the-pump complaint
that, because Premier Barton was not howled
down when he last spoke at Maitland, “it was
interpreted as enthusiastic approval.” Mr.
Barton is going to speak there again shortly, and
“ Maitlander ” hopes there will be no chance given
of another such interpretation. But, of course,
he doesn’t suggest howling down. Oh, dear no ;
in fact, he specifically deprecates it. Still, the
seed has been sown in good ground.
SWEET SARCASM.
“ You must have a great memory for faces if you recognised the lady after having only met her
once —and that two years ago.”
“ Oh, I didn’t recollect her face a hit, hut I recognised her hat.”
Mr. Cruickshank, the exceedingly rich
member for the Gwydir, has offered a 30gs.-trophy
for his electorate’s cricket club ; and there is justi-
fiable objection to this by-some of his constituents.
Mr. Cruidkshank’s motive is, doubtless, absolutely
honest and pure, but such gifts should be treated
by the law as bribes.
The British Secretary of State for India,
by way, apparently, of a hint re anti-cheap-
colored-labor legislation, says it is necessary to
remember the -great distinction between natives
of .India, “ with a civilisation older than our own,”
ai*d other colored races. This looks reasonable,
anyhow, and, just as soon as Britain recognises
that fine old Indian civilisation, bf giving the
Hindu person the same self-governing rights
as the Englishman enjoys, Australia will doubt-
lees re-consider the matter.
The news leaks out that the British
Government sent in to Bill Lyne a bill for £5OO for
alleged riot and damage done by Australian troops
in S’Africa. Oom Bill refused to pay or acknow-
ledge the facts of the case; in fact he seems to
hint, in his long slow way, that the British
Government isn’t telling the truth.
A Melb. man who knows (or ought to) :
Melb. Freetraders have had about enough of George
Dry Dog. His kowtow to the “ Pious and Immortal
Memory ” has lost him lots of votes in Yic. General
opinion is that it’s about time the people of Australia
gave Reid leisure to devote to providing for his family.
At any rate, the one-man leadership in Federal matters
seems to be dead ; the system of co-equal Houses has
killed it. Events point to the dual-leadership of the
Freetraders going to Symon and M'Millan.

Tommy Bent’s Vic. railway “ reforms "
are bearing fruit. At Alberton, a good-sized
station, where the revenue collected is over £6OOO
a year, Thomas cut down the staff from four to
one. The one man collapsed from overwork, and,
while Bent was considering whether he should be
boiled for insubordination or only beheaded,
Alberton station was left quite unattended and
took care of itself.
The Victorian House of Rich Landlords,
though its members are remunerated for their
“ services ” at their real value—nothing per annum
—nevertheless pays its officers a lordly wage.
Last session the President of the Leg. Council
pocketed £34 17s. Bd., and the Council Chairman
of Committees £lB ss. Id. per working day of 8
hours. Same session, Speaker Mason drew £7los.
per 8-hours working day.
“Rev. James Woolnough, J.P. ; Rev.
George Lane, J.P.” The See Government should
sit down in a cool place and think where it is
going. But if See and Co. are willing to stupidly
sell themselves for a trifle of sectarian advantage,
the churches should make a fixed stand against
any such union of clericalism and lay law. The
anathema of the thing is all over early Austra-
lian history. Botany Bay was accursed with parson
magistracy.
Strange reports of a huge influx of
Russian and Polish Jews and other old-clothes
dealers, come from S’Africa, and one local paper
lately alleged that up to that date 90 per cent, of
the names on permits to return to the Rand were
Hebraic. Cohen is coming into his own again.
The Carthaginians were Semites, and the race
made its great bid for the ownership of the world
when the noses followed Hannibal on his march
to Rome. Now the folk who lost North Africa
are going to the South for their mislaid pro-
perty.
The (S.A.) Labor Herald (Nov. 11) heads
an attack on the local Deutscher weekly with
these lines : —“A dirty, lying, German Tory rag,
the S.A. Zeitung, vilifies and misrepresents Aus-
tralia.” Strange to say, although in the Father-
land the Socialists are politically very strong,
nearly all the German settlers of S.A. —and there
are thousands on the rolls—are completely under
the thumb of the National Ass. They suck their
inspiration from the Zeitung, which has always
been aggressively Conservative, and which tells
them that the Labor party desires to confiscate
their farms.
Coghlan reports that, during 1901,
Westralia gained 11,541 people by immigration,
and that Queensland gained 3974 ; whilst S. A. lost
1596 ; Tasmania 1875; Victoria 3784 ; and N.S. W.
6744. This means that the total gain for the
Commonwealth was only 1516 people ; while little
Maoriland, where socialistic legislation has been
carried further than in any other country on earth,
gained 4123. Yet every Fat Man paper explains,
with the superior and greasy air of one who con-
descends to enlighten the ignorant, that it is
socialistic legislation which causes the falling off
in immigration.
“ GOING AHEAD."
Mr. KEAST: The cause of the present financial stress was that
Victoria had gone ahead too rapidly. The national debt of the
State was £52,000,000, with external borrowings amounting to
£87,000,000, and the interest already paid on this in London averaged
£72 per head for every man, woman and child in the community.
Joe Cook and Jawbone Neild do not run
together, though Reid does all he can to keep
them in step. They are valuable for his purposes
if they would obey the whip, but personalities
differ. Both are egotists, and each treats the
other’s egotism as an offensive encroachment. All
round, Reid’s is a fractious team.
Jenkins Iscariot’s Transcontinental Rail-
way Bill, for granting 90,000,000 acres, with all
minerals, and 10 years’ exemption from taxation,
to a syndicate, which is to build and retain a
cheap, shoddy, narrow-gauge line to Port Darwin
and supply one slow train a week, has passed
S.A. Parliament. But as Jenkins has quite for-
gotten to get the Federal Parliament to guarantee
that it won’t tax his syndicate, and as the Pederal
graduated land-tax will probably arrive before
long and strike the syndicate’s big steal to the
extent of 3d. or 4d. in the £, Jenkins isn’t nearly
so far ahead as he imagines.
Sydney D.T. missed the ’bus over the
appointment of Senate President Baker as official
Kow-Tow-er to the pow-wow at Delhi. The D.T.,
in its anxiety to prove itself behind the scenes, has
developed a passion for tipping Federal appoint-
ments. It has appointed half the legislature, and
all the Cabinet, at various times, to lucrative
positions—notably the High Commissionership
and the Federal Judiciary. It plumped on B. R.
Wise, of all people, for the Delhi trip, with
Malcolm M'Eacharn as a saver. And, when
Baker’s selection was announced, the paper came
out, unabashed, with a string of internal and ex-
ternal motives which decided the appointment,
just as if it had known them all along.
Jenkins Iscariot, of S.A., by way of de-
fending the proposed Ninety Million Acres land
grant and the proposed privately-owned trans-
continental railway, asked if private railway
ownership hadn’t been a success in Canada. May
the Lord preserve Australia from any such
success ! ! Canada gave to the Canadian Pacific
Railway Co. alone 25,000,000 acres of good land,
a finished railway 713 miles long, that had cost
the State £7,000,000, also £5,000,000 in cash,
and other pauper aids—about £60,000,000 in all.
And Canada’s private railways, with all this
and other assistance, and a far better country
than Australia to operate in, don’t earn over
per cent. Australia’s State-owned lines, drought
and all, do better than that.
The Victorian House of 48 Rich Landlords
has nominally agreed to the renewal of the Fac-
tories Act, but it made so many “amendments”
that it might almost as well have rejected
the Act altogether. The Landlords enacted
that the Act should expire on 30th Sept, next, so
it is really only renewed for ten months. No new
Wages Boards are to be appointed without the
assent of the House of Landlords, which assuredly
won’t assent—the old law was that they could be
created by either House. All recent decisions of
the Wages Boards are abolished; the decisions
that affect the fellmongery trade are abolished,
and the new Act doesn’t extend to certain dis-
tricts covered by the old one. And it was only
by one vote that the House of Landlords didn’t
decide to abolish everything that has been done
under the Act up to now. At time of writing
the only news about the Irvine Government’s atti-
tude was that it was considering the matter.
The farmers of Maoriland are a curious
lot. It was the Ballance-Seddon administration
which created them, by finding them the land to
settle on. It further assisted them with cheap
money under the Advances to Settlers Act.
Valuable stud animals were imported by the
State, and the farmers obtained their use at a
lower price than they had formerly paid for the
less valuable ones supplied by good old private
enterprise. Railway freights on agricultural pro-
ducts were cut down to a minimum, and
much more assistance given indirectly; but
because the trend of the Government legislation
was to make the now well-to-do farmers pay their
employees decent wages and give over sweating
their own children, these ingrates have turned
round and howled that the Seddon Government
did everything for the “lower orders” (the
workers for wages), and neglected the “backbone
of the country.” And the backbone is at present
a very indignant spine indeed.
A Westralian paper raises this
question : When any district is sup-
plied with a railway 400 miles long,
to drag its stuff to a remote port,
merely because some influential axe-
grinding, or some parochial pre-
iudice, demanded that the stuff
should go there instead of to an
equally suitable port only 200 miles
away, will the Inter-State Commis-
sion have power to order that the
railway freight shall be charged as
for 200 miles only ? In other words,
will it have power to declare that
the country producer shall nor be
made to pay for the grinding of city
axes ? If the Commission has such
a power and uses it, some old-
established swindles will fall
through badly.
A few weeks ago two Irish
M’s.P. were sent to gaol for want-
ing a local P .irliament such as we
have ; and l or objecting to great
absentee landlords and the accumu-
lation of property, in big estates.
In Ireland, Higinbotham and
Graham Berry, of Victoria, Balia-
nee, Seddon and John M‘Kenzie,
of Maoriland, Kingston, of South
Australia—in fact, every great reforming poli-
tician that Australia ever possessed, would nave
been gaoled with hard labor. It is exactly the
things these men did, and the things for doing
which the British Government offered them titles
and other trash, that politicians are gaoled for in
Ireland under the Balfour Government.
“E.M.P.'’ : Victoria just now consists of
about one-third of lean territory and two-thirds of
fat. In the lean part the people are howling for
Govt, assistance, and getting it. In the fat part
things generally are booming ; the big estates are
being subdivided, and the owners and occupiers of
the little ones, having made their piles selling
produce to the lean farmers up North, are investing
in pieces of the big estates, and are paying as high
as £2O an acre for some of the land that was origi-
nally dummied for the big proprietors at £1 an
acre—-deferred payment. Meanwhile the lean part
of the State is held up as a scarecrow to frighten
off the land-taxers, and the “ poor farmer ” is even
more than usually in evidence.
“H.H.” (Vic.): Recent investigations
show that a man who left Melbourne as a penni-
less village-settler in ’94 is now the owner of one
of the biggest farms in South Gippsland, and last
year paid income tax on £l2OO. The poor farmer
again ! Anybody who feels inclined to weep over
the troubles of the average agriculturist in decent
seasons should read the published wills and
bequests every day for a month. The farmers
average by far the highest of all callings. Never
men of immense wealth, they die in dozens,
leaving from £l5OO to £5OOO. How many city
workers of the same rank of intelligence and in-
dustry leave one quarter as much ? [Of course,
this is in regard to Vic., which has highly protected
its agricultural industry for years.— Ed. B.]
Tasmania’s Leg. Council has cast out
pretty well everything sent upstairs by the
rickety Lewis Ministry this session, but the most
violently cast-out proposal of all was the Succes-
sion Duties Bill. The ancient member Dodery
(lovely name!) upended himself, and expressed
the opinion that succession duties were of the
devil, and was horrified that the Govt, should
show so little respect for the dea,d as to levy
taxation on corpses! “Tax a man while he is
alive, not when he is dead and can’t defend him-
self,” said Dodery in effect. That settled the
question at once. The Bill was rejected almost
before the indignant Dodery tottered to his
seat. Any slight offered to the dead is taken as
a personal affront by members of Tasmania’s
House of Dry Bones.
S.A. Assembly made only one important
alteration in the Transoontinental 90,000,000-acres
steal Land Grant Railway Bill. That alteration
was put in to make it clear that the Government
may buy the railway at its full value any time after
construction, but the syndicate is to retain the whole
90,000,000 acres grant. Consider the beauty of
that! The co. is to build a railway costing
£4,000,000 or £5,000,000. It is to receive £4,000,000
or £5,000,000 worth of land, obviously on the plea
that the line will be worked at a loss in its early
days. It is proposed that the co. may sell the
line to the State at its full cost, and leave the
State to bear the loss, and keep the £4,000,000
or £5,000,000 worth of land. If the line is built
on the terms proposed it will pay the co. to buy
up three-fourths of S.A. Parliament at £lO,OOO a
member in order to engineer that sale It isn’t a
large Parliament.
A Bulletin correspondent suggests a plan
for diminishing sectarianism :
(1) When taking next census ignore religions com-
pletely. Why should the State go to great expense in
compiling religious returns ?
(2) In our State schools (they are supposedly secular)
altogether ignore religion. Why should teachers be com-
pelled to furnish returns as to the religion of each child ?
The only purpose it serves is to provide statistics for
bigots to fling at one another.
(3) When a person is entering the public service religion
not to be asked nor stated.
(4) When a man is put in gaol religion not to be taken
down ; it does not serve any purpose for future identifi-
cation.
The trouble is that when there were no reliable
religious statistics to fall back upon Dill-Macky
would shortly persuade his gang of howlers that
four-fifths of the people were Catholics, and then
in their frenzy about the approaching domination
of Rome they would set Sydney on fire.

TO HIS DAUGHTER’S
Cure of Lung Disease
BY
VITADATIO
——■
Singleton, N.S.W., May 30, 1901.
To Mr. S. A. PALMER—
Dear Sir, —My daughter has suffered with Lung Disease
since she was five years of age. She is now eleven and
in good health, thanks to your valuable medicine. After
trying a great many patent medicines, with varying
effect, I was persuaded to give VITADATIO. a trial, and
the improvement to her condition was at once noticed by
Mrs. Porter and myself. My daughter has been a great
sufferer, and was given up by the doctors after breaking
a blood vessel, about three years ago, the medical man
remarking that she could not live through the winter,
but VITADATIO willed otherwise, as she is now well.
Three years ago she had a bad attack, when she
frequently spat up blood, so I immediately purchased a
bottle of your medicine, with the result that it completely
cured her. You can make any use of this testimonial, as
I would like to see other sufferers benefit by your
appropriately named medicine—VITADATIO.
Yours trulv,
WILLIAM PORTER,
Bootmaker.

At Dawn and Dusk:
By V. J. Daley. In handsome cloth,
with portrait. Price 55.; postage, 4d.
Adelaide still has no abattoirs and no
effective meat-inspection. The city officer, who is
supposed to look after these things, went through
the t’otherside public slaughterhouses lately and
saw:—
A tubercular pig, with the “ condemned ” cross on it;
the carcase was slit all over. To the eye this pig
carried good condition; and I could not help being
struck with the idea that if it had been killed in a private
slaughter-yard, with the present price of meat, the
butcher who would totally destroy the carcase would
want to be very honest.
And yet, on the strength of having moderately
sweet gutters—they could hardly be anything else
in the climate Adelaide thinks herself the
cleanest city in Australia !
Federal-member Brown (N.S.W.) demands
that the N.S.W. Government shall abolish agri-
cultural and pastoral rents, supply seed wheat,
carry stock free, and furnish the drought-stricken
farmers with work or rations. No doubt the case
is urgent, but the lesson is urgent also. And that
lesson is as follows : An industry which wants so
much assistance shouldn’t pose as the backbone of
N.S. Wales. An industry which suffers from
such pitiable periodical collapse shouldn’t put on
such airs about being a “natural industry. J> A
drought-cursed country should aim rather at pro-
moting manufactures ; they are to a great extent
drought-proof, and the natural industries of a
country with little rain are those which can do
with little rain. Also, there should be less
hustling of the young man on to the land when
the land treats him like this.
The Veterinarian.

BUCHANAN WHISKY.
Can We Prove our Claims? Our Friends do it for us;
Bill Martin, who won last year’s “ Austral ” on a Red Birdj was tempted, whilst in America recently,
to try a few of the local machines. He says : “ I tried three or four, and broke them all up , and then I
thought I would go hack to the old Red Bird I took over with me to train on. I never had a
trouble afterwards, and nothing would induce me to try any more experiments. I shall ride my Red Bird
again this season.” , . . . . .
There are a few who think Bill knows a good bike when he sees one. Perhaps his experience may help
you to choose. ‘ .
Branches i. every state. GOOLP BICYCLE CO„ 64 King-st., Sydney.
The handicaps for the greatest cycling event in
Australasia—the Austral wheel race, were published up
to time on Monday morning last, and naturally Mr. G.
R Broadbent’s allotments of starts are this week the
subject of much discussion. The mark of honor is held
by last year’s winner, W.-Martin, with Beauchamp and
Morgan on ten yds. It is interesting to note that M.
Chappell, winner of the big Dunlop road race from
Warrnambool to Melbourne last August, receives 190 yds.
start. Racing men should note that acceptances close at
the Melbourne Bicycle Club rooms on the 15th inst.*
A pair of Sydney solicitors, ordered by
N.S.W. Full Court during the week to pay
over £169 14s. sd. to former clients within 48
hours, requested. their Honors to issue an order
suppressing their (the solicitors’) names. Their
Honors declined to make any such Order, Mr.
Justice Pring pointing out that if they did, the
order for the payment of the money within 48
hours might be taken to apply to any legal firm
in the city. Gents two evidently anticipated
kindlier treatment, and not without having many
precedents of judicial consideration to rely upon.
A person signing himself “J. Roberts' ”
writes to Sydney Telegraph to point out that if
the enfranchised women don’t put their names on
the roll “it will cause N.S.W. to fall her
proper share of representation in the Federal
Parliament, the number of our representatives
being based on the number of electors enrolled.
It is truly wonderful how little the average man
knows about the constitution of his own country,
and how gladly he rushes into print with his
ignorance. The simple, ordinary fact that rep-
resentation goes by population, and that if nobody
was on the roll at all the State would have just as
many Federal members as it has now, and if
every man, woman, child, parson and dog were
on, it would have no more, appears to be beyond
the ordinary man’s comprehension.
Maoriland reckons, on an average, to have,
on its jagged coast, about one bad wreck every
two years. Huddart Parker and Co.’s s.s. Elin-
gamite is the latest. She charged into that group
of rocks called the Three Kings, the far northern
point of Maoriland, on Monday. Vessels, as a rule,
crawl between the Three Kings a,nd the ma nland,
guided by the light on Cape Maria Van Diemen.
The currents are strong and unreliable thereabouts,
and the Elingamite seems to have failed to pick
up the light in the fog. There are few coasts of
equally small extent that can offer so many
chances of disaster as those of Maoriland. Ihe
present wreck, though the number of the drowned
is not yet known, is a small disaster for that
region. Compared to such wholesale tragedies as
those of the Tararua and Wairarapa it is hardly
worth mentioning.

When your bowels don’t move regularly you are

ailmeuts. Put up in amber bottles with full

The medical man, as a rule, has nothing
but disapproval to throw at any and every pro-
prietary medicine. When he breaks this rule, a,s
in the case of the testimonial given by Dr. Smeal,
of Vic., to Prof. Chris. Mudd’s “Oleac,” and
published on The Bulletin’s front cover, it is
because merit has been too convincing even for
scientific prejudices. Dr. Smeal’s testimonial
was not given until the “Oleac” cure had stood
a two years’ test in a case within his own
experience.*

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Backers of “form ” have three inglorious uncer-
tainties to consider, apart from the “ glorious uncer-
tainty ”of racing under the happiest conditions. Firstly,
the doubt as to whether the horse is “alive ” or “dead”—
a doubt that begins to sicken the public of meddling with
little races. Secondly, the chance of calculations being
upset by foul or reckless riding. Thirdly, the fear of the
race being thrown away through bad judgment on the
part of the jockey who ought to have pulled it off. Will
it “go” ? Will it get a “ fair go,” free from interference ?
And will it be wisely ridden ? The standard of ability in
Australian jockeyship is undoubtedly much lower than
it was 15 years ago, when men like Tom Hales, Mick
O’Brien and a few others brought a knowledge of pace
into the business, and served, in a measure, to enlighten
their duller rivals as to tactics. Nowadays we
find even the best and most experienced of the
jockeys getting beaten by circumstances over which they
might have had full control. The Oaks race this year looked
a “good thing” for the favorite, Eleanor, on the assump-
tion that Bob Lewis would give the filly a chance to tire
her only dangerous opponent, Fishery—a notorious non-
stayer. There were only four of them altogether, and
neither of the other two fillies had any pretentions to
make the running in a fast race, yet Lewis allowed a
third-rater named Fungus to show Eleanor the way for
about a mile. Then he brought his mount along for a
4-furlong sprint, and the speedy Fishery chased the
favorite and won by a head. Time, 2.45. Lewis lost the
race in allowing it to be fully ssec. slower than Eleanor
could have made it had he put her along from the start.
But then a run-away victory would not have been
“ clever,” in the orthodox sense of the term. Jockeys
like to get paragraphed for a clever finish.
• • * *
The boy who rode Abundance for Melb. Cup
had the appearance of being half-dazed by his sense of
responsibility when he left the paddock on the Derby
winner. Seemingly he was too nervous to know what
was happening in the race. An interviewer could get
nothing from him save the information that he was
“ fairly done.” The boy was not even alive to the fact
that Abundance had been considerably buffeted and
galloped on during the race. The colt lost a lot of
■ground through the mishap, but the boy was blind to his
misfortune.
* * * •
“ Patronus ! ’’ was the first name shouted by the
hopeful bookies when the Melbourne Cup horses made
the final turn for home. If Patronus was in front where
should The Persian have been ? Fielder’s “ certainty ”
beat the Patron horse handsomely over llfur. at Moonee
Valley a week earlier, and was now meeting him on 301 b.
better terms, but something happened to The Persian
before he had covered 11 furlongs on this occasion. The
horse that couldn’t lose was retiring to the rear on three
legs and'a dotty one.
Patronus, the long-time despised failure, put up
a record in his second win last week. He took the
Veteran Stakes (LJ miles) in 2.35, the fastest time ever
registered for 12 furlongs at Flemington. At Rand wick
it has been equalled, but not beaten, by Survivor, Fairy
Prince and San Fran. Only a day or two previously the
Melbourne dailies had classed Patronus as “ a plodder,”
but it is now evident he wanted only the “heart” to
justify his trainer’s belief that he would outstay stable-
companion The Victory in Melb. Cup.
William Day, in his “Reminiscences of the
Turf,” tells a story about an eccentric banker, named
Zachariah Simpson, who owned racehorses, but rarely had
a wager. As a principal partner in a private banking
business, he couldn’t afford to get a reputation for
gambling. So it happened, says William Day, that when
the cautious Zachariah took 8 to 1 about Macaroni for the
Derby, “ he never put the bet down in his pocket-book,
and, when he found Macaroni had won, wrote to ask if I
could tell him whom it washe had betted with. But I could
not; and he would have lost the money had not Mr. I ( .
Fisher, who heard the bet made, given him the name of
the layer, and he recovered his £800.” The Mr. F. Fisher
aforesaid (father of Edmund Fisher, an old contributor to
The Bulletin) was the first owner of Plebeian, a horse
that sired Beanfeast, the dam of Abundance. Winch
brings the 40-year-old story of Zachariah’s wager right
up to date.
r « * ****..** •
The Victory is essentially a battler —one of the
“ cut-and-come-again ” sort. His ribs bore the dried
furrow-marks of many spurrings before he went out for
the Cup, and when he returned to the bird-cage he
showed a fresh lot of honorable scars. At Maribyrnong,
a week earlier, Bob Lewis allowed Fairy States to get
about 25 lengths ahead of The Victory, before starting to
make up the gap between them, and the gaihe little mare
couldn’t get nearer than six lengths from the winner.
Then came the vain struggle to beat Wakeful in the
Melbourne Stakes. These two gruellings benefited The
Victory. They would have broken the heart of a weaker
animal.
* * • *
It doesn’t take a Sherlock Holmes to tell that
a Sydney D.T. syndicate backed a N.S.W. horse for the
Oup—and lost heavily. The D.T.’s Cup article was
mainly invective against Victoria and gloomy predictions
of the speedy decay of -the Cup as a “ racial institution.”
For the social charm has gone out of the Cup, writes the
reporter. It is hot the old Cup |it is not the Cup to
which scores of thousmds poured from every part of
Australia. ... It will become more and more an exclu-
sively Victorian institution, and no one will visit it
because it is the custom to come to Melbourne at Cup
time in preference to any other part of the year.
The Victory’s win was very popular in Adelaide,
where the “ place totes” only averaged about 10s. 6d. for
a 2s. 6d. investment. His owners have for some time been
on Adelaide Stock Exchange ; and his win in the S.A. St.
Leger and Birthday Cup, and second in Adelaide Cup,
made him a strong tip locally.
* » * *
It is more than time that the V.R.C. stewards
took practical steps to prevent “sport” at Flemington
from degenerating into a rough-and-tumble game of
chicanery and chance. On Derby Day a field of 20 horses
went jostling round the course for Coburg Stakes and
four came to grief, two horses being killed outright.
Again, in the Cup race (24 starters) one among the mob
of .cattle was buffeted so violently that its jockey fell
clean over the inner rail out of harm’s way, fortunately.
Another got into difficulties and broke a leg. The two
unfortunates were rank outsiders, but their disasters
might have happened just as easily to The Victory and
Vanity Fair. In fact the ultimate winner narrowly
escaped a knock-down when he collided with Sojourner.
Abundance met with at least one big bump, and The
Persian was twice interfered with. If the nervous gelding
ever had a chance of winning, the second interference
settled it. But, to writer’s way of thinking, Fielder,
junr., after reducing himself 181 b. in weight, was too
anxious and weak to have made the best of his mount in
any case. He looked doomed to defeat as he left the
paddock. * » •
Old Australian sports were glad to see the “ old
Australian ” blood assert itself in the Cup race in compe-
tition with two offspring of the imported sire, Pilgrim’s
Progress. The Victory, by The Admiral—The Charmer,
is the son of a V.R.C*. Derby winner whose sire, Rich-
mond, ran second for both Derby and Cup in 1875. Also
his dam, The Charmer, ran second for Caulfield Cup in
1889. On both sides of his pedigree the big-barrelled
little nag is raCy of the soil, whereas the previous five
Cup winners were either sired or grand-sired by new-
chums. The Endeavor, an own brother to The Victory,
won the 2-year-old Colt Stakes on Cup day, with little or
no public money on him. Not one punter in 600 heeded
the youngster’s breeding.
• • * •
Said the trainer of Victory to the V.R.C.
handicapper 30mins. before the race: “ I want another
fortnight, but if they don’t make it too fast he may have
a chance.” And he had.
* * * •
Nine of the 22 Melb. Cup starters hailed from
Sydney, but the nearest to winning was Abundance,
third.' Of 42 Cups 16 have come to Sydney, two to S.A.,
and the balance to Victoria. Of the winners 18 were
Cornstalk, three Tasmanian, two Maoriland, three S.A.,
and 16 Victorian nags.
♦ • • •
H. Bradfield, who trained The Victory, owned
Portsea when winner of the Australian Cup, Champion
Stakes, &c., also Patron, when he won the Melb. Cup in
’94, starting at 33 to 1, with elder brother Ruenalf favorite.
* • • •
It would be interesting to know exactly how
many bookmakers’ horses are running nowadays, under
the disguise of somebody else’s name.
* * * *
Owing to having lost so much on Caulfield Cup,
Sydney stood off Melbourne Cup considerably. Pat-
ronage, Abundance, and The Persian, Sydney nags, were
the choice picks at stol, or thereabouts. A few isolated
instances of from 25 to 50 to 1 about The Victory are
known, but 33 to 1 was available right up to the “death.”
Some Sydney sports in Melbourne wired The Victory as
an eleventh-hour tip, and a few managed to get on to
some purpose. When weights were first published, The
Victory was much fancied, but his subsequent failures
dropped him out, and he was a good horse for the Sydney
bookies, despite the fact that there were a few cases in
which he was laid to the full extent of the book.
* « * •
Owner of Merton Steel, who broke a leg in
Melb. Cup and was shot, magniloquently wired his Melb.
rep. morning of race : “To death or glory.” Death it
was!
• » * •
Some of the Sydney shops were struck for the
full amount of their Caulfield and Melbourne Cup
doubles. One in Red fern (Syd.) laid £5OO to £1 against
the winning pair, and the man who got it also took £lOO
to 2s. in several city shops. Notwithstanding, ’bis doubt-
ful if Mo has had to pay out more than £15,000 through
Victory’s win. The owners live in England, and are re-
ported to have made £5OOO. A large number of tars from
the warships laid a bit on The Victory when they heard
he was by The Admiral; and one Mo on the “ Hill ” paid
out £llO to sailors who backed the winner for small sums.
* • * *
The men caught in the recently-raided Sydney
betting shops were fined a fiver each, in default two
months “ hard.” Are the gentlemen who administer the
anti-betting laws only fools, or are they rogues ? It’s
certainly a case of one or the other : The man who puts
the boodle on in stacks can do so with impunity at either
of the two Tattersall’s Clubs, or a couple of proprietary
concerns which operate openly, and without fear or
hindrance. N.S.W. gaming laws are only for the punish-
ment of the humble individuals who speculate shillings
and half-crowns in “ shops.” If they speculate their small
coin in the big clubs they are not interfered with, and it
would appear that the police force of Sydney exists
largely to secure for the Moseses and Kohens of the big
Tatt.’s clubs a close monopoly of the gambling business
of the city. Night before the Melbourne Cup hordes of
big and little Sydney Tatt.’s fielders did a roaring busi-
ness on the open footpath in front of their headquarters.
Clients were there by the hundred, and the welshers
gathered in a splendid harvest. Somehow the police all
had urgent business elsewhere, and there were no prose-
cutions. Once more : Who is making the good thing out
of this disgraceful partizanship, and what is the good
thing worth ?
• * • •
At V.R.C. meet Sydney jock. Barden won six
races, including the Derby. Bob Lewis won four, in-
cluding the Cup; W. H. Smith, of Merloolas fame,
three; M. Mooney, F. Dunn and A. Richardson two
each. Concerning sires : The Admiral led with four ;
Pilgrim’s Progress and Haut Brion three each ; Patron,
Grafton, Trenton and Lochiel two each. Sweet Nell was
highest scoring nag with wins on Ist, 2nd and 3rd days.
Of 28 races Victoria won 20, N.S.W. 7, S.A. 1, Stake
money amounted to £18,605, of which Clark and Robin-
son (The Victory) took £5075 ; R. Phillips (Abundance)
£1561 ;F. Musgrave (Vanity Fair) £llOO. Anticipated
there will be nearly £4OOO decrease in profits as compared
with last year.
* * • *
S.A. Assembly has passed through all its
stages a Bill for the suppression of tote-shops,
and practically restricting betting to the racecourse.
Its supporters were partly racing men who want
to encourage the licensed machine, and partly
anti-gamblers who hope eventually to crush the
machine, too. The debate took place on the afternoon
of Cup-day, and it was freely stated that various sup-
porters of the measure had taken part in sweeps during
the morning. Two of them even admitted having visited
the tote-shops themselves.
* . » * *
Thirteen lined up for Williamstown Cup, now
£lOOO, Monday, and last year’s winner, Flagship, started
favorite, 7. to 4, with Vanity Fair and Progredior next,
and Cluster and Eleanor well up. Vanity Fair dead-
heated with Eleanor on the post. For the run-off sto 2
went on Vanity Fair. V.F. led for a mile, and then
Eleanor streaked down the straight and won easily. On
her showing Eleanor would have gathered in the Oaks
had the pace been strong ; as it was, Fishery only just
managed to squeeze home.
* * * *
Kiota, the handsome brother to Daimio, fulfilled
this scribe’s early prediction by winning the big 3 miles
Steeplechase on Saturday, after running third for the 2
miles ditto on Cup Day. Many people grumbled at the
running of top-weight Error, who dropped out of the
front rank a mile from home and come with a too-late
rush. In present writer’s opinion, Kiota had the race in
hand all the time, and it was only because the Ballarat
nag was winning easily that Error, hard ridden, appeared
to be going stronger at the finish. Kiota, anyhow, has
made every pound of the anticipated improvement since
he rah nowhere in Grand Nat. Steeple last July. On that
occasion Bay Eagle (Ist) and Error (2nd) were giving him
111 b. and 371 b. respectively. Last week they were trying
to give him 31b. and 241 b., and they failed.
* * * »
Game Chick, the 6 to 4 favorite for the Aidful
Stakes last Saturday, was a highly unsatisfactory per-
former. The youngster ran a good race on Cup Day,
when the flying Sweet Nell beat him by only half-a-length
or so. On Saturday, with young Richardson up, he took
a zig-zag course, to the disgust of his backers. Bookmaker
Sol Green is understood to be the owner of Game Chick.
* * * •
After Brakpan, with Barden up, had won the
Spring Handicap, finishing as gamely as one could wish,
the V.R.C. stewards held an inquiry into the colt’s
running for the Derby and Cup when Frank Kuhn rode
him. Result, Kuhn was disqualified for two years for
“ suspicious practices.”
* * * *
Heavy casualty list in Rosehill Steeple, Monday.
Of six starters three fell, Trumper somersaulting on to
his jockey, A. Gorman. Result: bad case of brain con-
cussion. Field was simply wretched, and favorite Saltoun
won after narrow escape from a cropper at last leap.

* * » *
Dan Creedon re-opened his Australian fighting
career auspiciously by outing ex-champion Bill Doherty
well inside of five rounds at Melbourne. Doherty was
certainly ahead on points by at least two to one, but
nevertheless the issue seemed certain from the middle of
the third round, so weak was the Westerner’s hitting ana
so confused were his tactics. Open-mouthed, staring,
and with the look of a man who has received heavy
body blows, he nevertheless scored in the exchanges,
sometimes landing Creedon about the head, but as often
missing him altogether. The blows that Creedon stopped
never disconcerted him for an instant. He seemed to
thrive on punishment, and opened the fifth round with a
light tap on Doherty’s face. 'I here were a couple of
seconds more spent in sparring, and then Doherty tell
prone, staggered up when counted out, and very nearly
took a header over the ropes. Nobody on one side of the
hall saw the blow that did the work at all. It was a
short, light hit and took Doherty between chin and neck.
Winner is matched against Yankee Scanlan.
* * * *
Sydney’s Golden Gate A.C. (wliat’s in a name, ye
gods 0 saw two gory, feature-flattening scraps on Satur-
day night. Coon Walcott and navvy Edwards, latter much
the heavier, spread themselves over each other in a wild-
beast fashion from the start. In the sixth round Edwards
chucked it, and the black got the verdict. That nigger
is the spunkiest of his hue seen here since the days Peter
Jackson sent fragments of humanity whirling through
the air. When the scraps had been removed, and
the floor nicely sanded (speaking metaphorically, of
course), Dovle' and “ Robbo” commenced a howling
Paddy's Market exhibition. “ Robbo,” the stronger and
heavier, did everything he could to his opponent bar
chewing his face and jumping on him with both feet.
The referee was apparently there only to pronounce
when life was extinct. Robbo tried somewhere about 17
different ways of killing his man, and apparently the
alleged referee reckoned every single one of them was
right. Doyle threw it up after eight rounds^
M.L. rat, Gray, bad a cyclonic scrap with Ted
Murphy at Sydney Gaiety A.C., Monday night. Favorite
Murphy made the pace, and the Maoriiander went faster
till an awful slug, suspiciously low, doubled Grey up in
the first round. Jack Conlon then faced artillery bands-
man Jack Thompson, and was sent to the floor repeatedly
till in the third round he decided to take the rest lying
down, and was counted out. Conlon should now take to
sausage-making, or some other harmless pursuit.
The strongest lawn-tennis club in Adelaide at
present is one composed simply of four brothers, Angel.
It has beaten the club previously holding the premier-
ship, the champion, Bowen, being also defeated in his
single. There is a fifth brother, nearly equal to the
four, as reserve-man.
• • • *
Australasian single-handed bowling supremacy,
contested under M.O.C.’s auspices, was won by South
Melb. orkek, G. H. Cooper, from 96 competitors. Albert
Park plaver, Slatter, opposed Cooper in the final, and for
21 heads'the v scored evenly. Figures were 18-17 in favor
of Slatter ; then he froze, and Cooper won by three.
Last season Cooper shaved a couple of big things very
closely, and was runner-up in the champion of champions,
and champion of Victoria contests.
• » • *
The Don Walker boom continued at N.S.W.
bike League Carnival on Saturday in the presence of 6000
people. In the seventh heat of Britannia Handicap (jm.)
Don caught Victorian Connell (20yds.) before latter got
fairly going, and one after the other he passed the rest
with abound and won in 58sec. In the final of the same
event Walker and Morgan were on scratch, with Walne
(10yds.) nearest, and the flower of 100 original starters
spread out to 55 yards in front. Morgan and Walne led for
a time, and then Walker got his head down and his heels
up, and a mighty roar went up as he seemed to
shoot forward with a vast shoot that landed
him. at the winning post two lengths in front of
everybody. Time, 57sec. Biggest things of the day
were five remaining preliminary heats of the Sydney Mile
Wheel Race, the £l5O handicap. Walker finished the
sixth heat with a tremendous rush that landed him inside
the previous Australasian best, in 2.0 3-5, after riding
round nine. Linton Plate (10m.), with 16 starters, saw
Walker go down for the first time in a sequence of eight
races, mainly through Randrup and Burton (M.L.), Pye
(Vic.), and Mutton (N.S.W.) getting away with a break
and keeping in front by well-judged and sustained pacing.
Pye just won, with Randrup second, and Burton third.
Mutton fell in the final sprint. Previous competition
record for the distance was broken, Pye doing it in 22.25,
which, in the heavy and blinding rain, was remarkably
good. Walker and several others withdrew when they
saw the case was hopeless.
• ¥ * •
Don Walker is 28 years old, a native of Victoria,
sft. llin. high, broad-shouldered and chested, with huge,
well-proportioned limbs. The big thigh, long foreleg,
and deep chest of the specially-created sprinter are all
there in a most aggravated form. And his sprint is a
fearful thing to behold from behind, on the track.

“What’s in a Name?”
Open
Saturday
Afternoons.
A good deal. A name on a note may make it worth 20s. in the £, or just the
reverse. The name on a bicycle shows the quality and speed value of the mount,
and carries the reputation of the firm with it.
“ MASSEY ” means Speed and Strength, when it is found on the name-
plate of a wheel. Here’s a curious corroboration : In Ottawa, last August, Major
Taylor could not get his wheel through the Customs in time for the race with
Kramer. He chose a Massey, and although the wheel was strange, he won easily.
At the next meeting Taylor discarded the Massey and lost the race.
What about the coming Racing Season ? Are you riding a Massey, or have you
no ambition to win ?
CANADA CYCLE & HOTOR CO., Ltd.,
OEORQE STREET (next David Jones').
Branches in Every State.

“ A canine earthquake ” was the heading
S.A. Register gave to a recent incident at
Woodside (S.A.) Presbyterian Church. During
service the congregation heard the rattling of a
door at the rear of the building. Excited cries
and gasps of “earthquake!” were heard on all
sides ; and a general stampede was about to begin,
when the preacher explained that it was only a
dog scratching himself against the door. Astonish-
ing how nervous of their lives are holy people
assembled to contemplate the bliss of Heaven.
A Victorian girl had to appeal to Judge
Hodges recently for a legal decision that she
might go and see her mother. The case, as laid
before his Honor, was to the effect that her
embittered male parent, now in Paradise, had
left her £lOO a year, contingent on her not residing
with her mother. The court was asked to decide
that visiting the mother was not residing with
her as the term was used by the parent who had
gone to a better land, and it decided accordingly.
Then the old man turned audibly in his grave.
Pigott, the new Messiah in London, is
probably a fairly powerful hypnotist. Anyhow,
a reporter who had nerve enough to get an inter-
view (refused to all others) on the plea of being a
truth-seeker come over from the R.C. Church,
admitted to turning limp when the “Son of
Man ” laid hands on his head ; and he actually
got away without the conversation he had come
for.

A lady writes :
Interesting to note that, while married women and
unmarried men are keen for leniency towards Selina
Sangal, the average married man is extremely virulent
against the proposed commutation, while the spinster
lady generally joins with him. The only visible explana-
tion is that the married man reckons that the sort of
thing which befell Sangal may happen any day to him-
self, considering how glad his wife would naturally be to
get rid of him ; while the spinster looks on the crime as
a sinful waste of Sangal, who might otherwise have
married her when Selina was dead. Hordes of wild-
eyed married men thronged the outskirts of the
Sangal meeting in Sydney Town Hall t’other night,
and breathed “Shame!” at intervals, while spinster
ladies, who were strangers to them, murmured gently
that they were quite right. “ Hundreds of women will
start doing it if she’s let off,” cried one male personage,
and a married lady, under an exalted bonnet, remarked
that it would merely prove that hundreds of husbands
required to be passed out. Whereat the married man
nearly foamed at the mouth. Then a speaker
on the side of “mercy” beseeched the audience
to remember their mothers, and the married
man, with his eyes nearly out of his head,
clambered up the steps to get on the stage to do some-
thing desperate. At the other end of the platform a man
who looked married was waving a stick above the head-
of the people who wanted Selina let off. Much o
the audience aired its views of married life in a public and
ostentatious manner. “ Husbands should be killed pain
lessly,” said one of the ladies present. So they ought.
Nothing tends more to depreciate property in a residen
tial district than for the inhabitants to be kept awake b.
the howls of a husband who isn’t being killed painlessly.

"A friend Told Me.”
A great deal has been written on the subject of
Advice—who are the people best qualified to give
it, and when and how it should be asked, offered,
accepted, rejected, etc. “ Pray, give a poor man a
few coppers,” whined a seedy-looking individual
into the ear of a fat dean, as both stood in St.
Paul’s Churchyard, London, watching the erection
of seats for viewing the ceremony of Queen
Victoria’s Diamond Jubilee. “ There is a notice,”
said the dean, rapping a placard with a scroll of
papers which he carried in his hand, “that
labourers are wanted. Work, my man, work;
don’t beg from people in the streets.” “I asked
you for money,” said the beggar contemptuously,
“not advice.” ’Twas'ever thus. Proffered advice
is seldom well received, those best qualified to
give it being generally the last to offer it. And
yet there are occasions when it is the plain duty
of anybody to volunteer the most positive advice —
as, s for instance, in the case of one who has a
suffering friend, and knows of some means to
effect his cure. The experience of Mr. C. A.
Bonnett, of 53, Currie-street, Adelaide, S.A., well
illustrates this. “For two years,” writes Mr.
Bonnett, “I suffered continuously from acute
indigestion and constipation. Before meal times
I would feel as hungry as a hunter, but no sooner
would I sit down to table and attempt to swallow
a few mouthfuls than my appetite vanished and I
loathed the very sight or smell of food. Nothing
could be more tantalising; I was always hungry,
yet unable to eat. Then there was the ever-present,
uncomfortable sensation of distension of the stom-
ach. As I became weaker from want of nourish-
ment, my symptoms increased in severity. I tried
many remedies, but got no good from any of them.
Several, indeed, seemed to aggravate my
complaint. I had almost decided to have nothing
more to do with them when, a little over a year
ago, a friend brought me half a bottle of Mother
Seigel’s Curative Syrup. He told me that he
himself had been cured by it, and strongly advised
me to let it do as much for me. In the end he

A curious inconsistency in the Yic.
Licensing Act is that, though it is legal for a
publican to serve a bona-fide traveller on Sunday,
it is illegal for him to open his bar on that day.
“ Then, how is the traveller to be served ? ” re-
cently asked a Hawthorn (Melb.) magistrate.
And with the prosecuting police-inspector it was
a bad case of “ Dunno ! ”
With the advent of Irvine and Co., the
Victorian J. P. once more starts his thriftless
system of gaoling the old poor for not possessing a
competence. T'other day, at South Melbourne, a
bench of unspeakables actually sent back to gaol
for 12 months an ancient (just released from gaol)
whose only crime was that he had no home, money
or friends, and then insulted him by telling him
“ it was the best place for him.'’ It ought to be
the best, seeing that it will cost far more to main-
tain him there than in the Benevolent Home, but
it doesn’t follow that it is.
“Of all the fads I ever heard of finding
favor with the ‘ idle rich ’ the most extraordinary
is that attributed in one of the London dailies to-
day to Mr. Charles Rothschild, of Tring Park,”
writes Sydney D.T’s. London correspondent.
“It is the collection of fleas ! He has a whole
section of a museum occupied by cabinets contain-
ing fleas of all forms and sizes, from nearly every
corner of the world. Altogether he has 10,000
specimens. He has a flea from the Kerguelen
Islands in Antarctica, and has just commissioned
the captain of an Arctic trawler to bring back a
collection of fleas from the blue-fox, the white
bear, the Esquimaux dog, and other animals of
the Polar regions. What useful purpose it serves
beyond that of a hobby—and a ridiculous one at
that—is not explained. ”
Dr. Bertillon, head of the Statistical
Service in Paris, told a recent interviewer that
“before 20 years are over France will simply have
ceased to exist,” if the excess of death-rate over
birth-rate continues to follow the present average.
And the chances seem to be that the excess will
increase rather than diminish.

N.S.W. newspaper proprietors have had
28 years of free postage for their wares. Postage
was imposed in 1864, but the prints of the day
worked up an agitation against it, and in three or
four years Parliament was appealed to. A motion
for the repeal of the postal charge was counted out
in 1869. Later a private member carried a mo-
tion urging Government to bring in a repealing
bill. Parkes did this, and passed it through both
Houses in’74. Should Reid get into power there
is probability that a similar agitation for repeal
will be again worked up.
The people of Morocco, who seem to be
bitten with Boxerism, complain that the Sultan
is getting altogether too European in his ideas of
civilisation. The Moorish potentate got his
private secretary (Verdon) from Melbourne, the
place where also his last lot of boots came from.
And it’s pretty certain that most of his other
foreign belongings are American. The trade in
civilisation is slipping away from Europe’s
wrinkled fingers.

A Hint.
Summer sickness may be prevented by Spring
renovation, but any haphazard attempt at the
latter may only the more surely prepare the
system for the former. If “ a stitch in time saves
nine ” when applied to our garments, it may apply
with equal truth and felicity to the body the
garments envelop. Bile Beans for Biliousness
prepare the system for the Summer, and they are
an ideal spring medicine. The Summer will* soon
be upon us, and at this time of year we should
take care to cleanse the whole human system and
fortify it to withstand the enervating effects of the
Australian Summer. The increasing hot weather
of this period has an extremely debilitating
effect on the system if it is not in perfect order all
through. It will thus be seen that a medicine
which will regulate and cleanse the interior
mechanism of man is especially valuable at this
time of the year. Such a medicine is Bile Beans,
and no better precaution can be found for toning
up the digestive organs, cleansing the blood, and
invigorating the liver. #

JUST WHAT YOU NEED.
Chronic Constipation results from a lack of
moisture in the residual matter of the food.
It becomes so dry and hard that the muscular
coating of the intestines is unable to expel
it. Two of Chamberlain’s Stomach and
Liver Tablets should be taken to begin
with and after that one each day. When
taken just after supper they will insure an
operation of the bowels on the following
morning. In most cases this lack of moisture
in the faecal matter is caused by an insuffi-
ciency of water being taken into the system
and cannot be cured unless more water is
taken with the food or drink. If taken
with the food, unless in shape of fruits
(apples and bananas), it is apt to interfere
with the processes of digestion; but if a regu-
lar habit is established Of taking a full glass
of water as soon as you get up in the morning,
about two hours after each meal, and just be-
fore going to bed, it will in most cases correct
the disorder—not at once, but in time.
Chamberlain’s Stomach and Liver Tab=
lets are not intended for habitual use but
to give temporary relief until a cure can be
effected by tailing more water into the system
and establishing a regular habit by having
the bowels move every morning immediately
after breakfast whether there is an inclination
or not.

At Poverty Point.
Melb. theatrical managers, who held on in the hope of
making up at Oup time for the sparseness of recent
audiences, were doomed to bitter disappointment. Even
night itself, not one of the theatres was more
than decently full, and the whole racing season was the
leanest ever experienced in Melb. One reason was the
noticeable absence of the usual contingent of visitors from
the Riverina, western N.S.W., and Queensland squattages,
where dying sheep and cattle and the expectant holder
of the mortgage claim far more interest now-a-days than
Cup winners and the bejewelled ring-men.
Ik Writing to a relative in Australia some six months ago,
Melba stated positively that she would not sing during Cup
week, but would gratify the dearest wish of her heart by
going to the Oup along with “ the boys and girls ” All
the same, she did give a concert last week, and she didn't
go to the Cup.
Charles Bright writes :—Re par in Bulletin (30/10/’O2)
about Kyrle Bellew and the trial of his dramatic pieces
before a committee of pressmen. The event occurred
about 30 years ago and the scene was a room in Melbourne
Pubhc Library to which Marcus Clarke, then assistant
librarian, had access. There were present F. W. Haddon,
editor of Argus, James Smith, Drs. Neild, Clarke, George
Walstab and several others besides myself. I think
Henry Gullett, present editor of Sydney I).T. was there,
but won’t be certain. Any old journalist will understand
the ordeal a young fellow had to go through in standing
up to recite things before such a jury of blasb theatre-
goers. Of course Bellew was advised to shun the stage,
as probably Edmund Kean would have been under like
circumstances. Not long after Bellew came to my office
to bid me good-bye, dressed up in the becoming uniform
of third officer of one of the crack line of clippers. Very
handsome he looked too. That was the last I saw of him
till I met him as “ David Garrick ” and recognised that
he could act, spite of the opinion to the contrary of his
earliest critics.
Au actor man made a row In the office of a metropolitan
weekly t’other day. In noticing a show, the paper mixed
up Brown, the brilliant comedian, with Jones, the other
brilliant comedian, and said that Brown was middling
bad on the whole. Whereupon Brown wrote in a state of
restrained fury to say that the performance mentioned
was Jones’s and not his (Brown’s), and demanding profuse
apologies. The paper blandly acknowledged receipt of
the letter, admitted that it had mistaken Jones for
Brown, and that an apology was called for, and it
straightway humbly apologised—to Jones 1 It was at
this point that Brown called to eat the editor.
A certain actress, concerning whose artistic merits
there was hot disputation in Australia not long ago, was
an enormous eater, and took but little exercise off the
stage, where she was wont to let herself go all the time.
Revelations as to her dining achievements—she and her
manager ordinarily partook of 12 courses at a sitting—
explain her opulent proportions and account for her
histrionic effect on her admirers. A commercial said of
her, in Melbourne : “ I have often seen her when she
was travelling round, years ago, and I got very tired of
her. But she used to be quite thin—a Maypole' of a girl.
The flesh she has put on has improved her a lot.” The
storming of the 15st. lady was fully sst. more impressive
than it had been before she filled out.
Melba still takes periodical trips to Marchesi and gets
that experienced lady to overhaul her voice. Yet there
are hundreds of singers in Australia who settle down to
professional singing and teaching after a year’s tuition,
convinced that they have no more to learn.
Some facts from London about theatrical people known
in Australia :—The new Henry Arthur Jones play,
“ Chance, the Idol,” is not a great success. It is a thin,
weak play, with a wildly improbable plot, saved by the
charm of the writer and the acting of Lena Ashwell as
Ellen Farndon. Titheradge plays' a thankless “heavy
father” pirt, and seems out of his element. Mrs.
Maesmore Morris gets no chance save to look sweet and
wear lovely frocks—l saw Mabel Love in “ Sweet and
Twenty ; ” pretty and charming she certainly is, but not
at home in comedy. She ought never to have left bur-
lesque—Joseph Carne is playing the old clergyman in
“ Sweet and Twenty ” in the suburban theatres—Julius
Knight does nothing much ; the critics don’t like him—
Dot Boucicault is one of the few we know in Australia
who has'any position ; he has almost entirely given up
acting, and is in demand for producing all the new
Pinero plays ; he is at present in America seeing “ Iris ”
through its first night—Florence Schmidt, although not
a great star, is generally at work, and doing well ; she
sings at popular promenade-concerts in the big Queen’s
Hall, where you can pay a shilling and hear the most
wonderful orchestra play Wagner. The men in the
audience smoke and walk about, and it is all very free
and Bohemian—Alice Hollander made a good impression
at her concert-recital. Critics spoke well of her, and she
wisely whisked off to Paris to study some more and
acquire a Parisian accent.
London papers comment upon one of Nance O’Neil’s
mannerisms which passed unnoticed during her Austra-
lian tour. No matter what the play was, the bulky
tragedienne was wont to extend her semaphore arm and
prefix her lines with the word “So 1 ” Bucklaw, the
melodramatic actor who was in Australia a few years
back, was similarly in the habit of crying “ My God ! ” at
frequent intervals in every one of his parts ; and there is
a stage tradition that he, as Hamlet, began the great
soliloquy :“Tobe or not to be—My God !—that is the
question.”
Juliet Wray, the almost forgotten lady who had a
meteoric career in Williamson’s comic opera co. a few
years back, lately revisited her native Ballarat. The thin
and angular J uliet, now wife of a medico in Maoriland,
has filled out to such a degree that she found it hard to
convince her old acquaintances that she was not some-
body else. In the old comic opera days, with memories
of Nellie Stewart and others still at their freshest, the
painfully slim and thin voiced girl from Ballarat was
regarded in many quarters as somewhat of a grim
managerial joke. In these days of musical comedy minus
either music or comedy, she would probably have out-
distanced Carrie Moore and all other competitors.
John Fuller’s “ Polite Entertainers ” have recorded six
months constant going at Hobart Temperance Hall and
Launceston Empire. This constitutes the record for Tas-
mania in show business. Denis Carney, Maud Faning,
Fred. Bluett, the coon brothers Jones) Bob Bell, Harry
Hart, Juggler Arnoldi, and the Mowbray trio are the
latest successes. The Fuller family spreads itself over the
performance in various attitudes and disguises, and gets
up a wedding occasionally by way of variety. Walter
Fuller took the clever Dolly Lamont for better or worse
last month, brother John having set example a while
earlier by marrying another of pa Fuller’s artistes.
Barton on the Naval Tribute.
Premier Barton, who seems to have been hope-
lessly hoodwinked and nobbled by Joseph of
Birmingham, has explained at Melbourne that
the idea of an Australian fleet for defence pur-
poses is quite absurd, and is based on a palpable
fallacy. To quote from the telegraphed report of.
the Premier’s speech:
The theory that a fleet could be kept selfishly at home
was also wrong, so that the only policy we could pursue
was the very policy with which this Empire had been
made one, and to the maintenance of which the Empire
was indebted for the holding of Australia. If, then, we
had an Australian navy it must be maintained in common
with the Empire. If such a squadron as was proposed
had to be maintained by Australia alone and kept sel-
fishly in one place, the cost to us would be £520,000 a
year.
A curious feature of the case is that Britain itself
pursues the allegedly wrong and selfish policy
which Mr. Barton thus denounces. No matter how
great the necessity for attacking the enemy in his
own waters, and no matter how large the fleets
that have been sent away, a squadron has always
been selfishly kept in reserve for the defence
of Britain itself, and in addition to the fact that some
of Britain’s first-class vessels are always retained for
local defence there is a “Coast Defence Squadron”
in reserve. It is simply proposed that Australia,
by establishing a fleet which will always stay in
Australian waters to form a last line of defence
for our coastal cities, shall follow Britain’s bad
and selfish example, and if Britain’s bad example
isn’t good enough for us to follow it is
time to ask what the empire is coming
to. If the British Government is prepared to
swear that, should a big enough enemy turn up, it
will send its last ship to attack him in his own
waters, and leave Liverpool and London liable
to bombardment should "any casual cruiser of the
enemy slip through, then it will be a fair gamble
for Australia to do the same. But the British
proposal is that Australia should pay for a
special defensive squadron, and when Britain
makes a war, in which we have no voice whatever,
that squadron shall sail away to hunt up the
enemy and leave Sydney and Melbourne liable to
destruction, partly, at least, in order that a
squadron may be reserved to save London
and Liverpool from any such risks. The offer
isn’t good enough. Sydney and Melbourne are
just as much to us as London and Liverpool are
to England. Let us have our own defensive
squadron—and pay for it.
Black tracker Sam Johnson, in cross-
examination at the Kenniff trial, stated that when
he saw James Kenniff racing towards him at
Lethbridge’s Pocket, he abandoned his pack-horse
and galloped away up the mountain. “ How far
did they follow you ? ” asked barrister M'Grath.
“ I didn’t see them,” said Sam ; “I never looked
back.” Further on M'Grath asked, “ What time
of day was it when bullets fly about—sun go up,
go down, or him on other side ? ” Sam (quietly):
“ About eight o’clock.”
The conviction and death-sentence of the
Kenniffs ends the latest attempt at Australian
bushranging, if their feeble proceedings could be
called bushranging. The Kenniffs were obscure
cow-stealers, and shot a policeman and a station-
owner who came after them to inquire about the
cows. Then they burned the bodies, put
the bones in a bag, and put the bag
on a horse—presumably to remove it for
burial. The horse somehow got loose, and
was found wandering around with the evidence
on its back —which was the worst kind of mis-
management. Then the Kenniffs bushranged in a
passive sort of way, dodging the police and steal-
ing mutton or cow flesh when they could get it,
but they never ranged in the aggressive Kelly
fashion. They were rather fugitives hiding
in the bush than active enemies of society, a id
the robbery of banks and sticking-up of towns was
a branch of the art that was quite beyond them.
The youthful Premier of Westralia pro-
poses to fix an age of consent for males, too. Any
woman or girl who “by signs, gestures, or other-
wise ” invites any male that looks less than 16 to
stray is to be liable to six months. Any male
who looks older than that may be invited for £lO.
An unpromising Victorian youth of 11
lately stole a horse and sold it to a cabman’s wife
for a pound—Bs. cash and 12s. credit. There is no
future ahead of that boy. A youth who doesn’t
know the folly of giving 12s. credit on a twenty-
shilling horse lacks the commercial instinct which
is the basis of all true greatness.
Be the waste of public money in Queens-
land. A wretched little row took place recently
in the Bacteriological Institute, and two enquiries
were held into the matter, costing altogether
£350. The enquiring tribunal recommended
that the expenses of defendant be paid, and as
it is generally understood that over £3OO was that
unfortunate man’s little bill, the total cost of this
lovely busines will be something like £7OO
Adelaide is dreadfully disappointed be-
cause a prominent gentleman who was to have
come forward as a co-re. has managed to get his
name struck out of the suit. Leave has been
given to the husband to proceed without specify-
ing the partner of his wife’s alleged infidelity.

Four Letters.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WHAT HE WROTE.
To-day, when looking through some papers, I
found the little volume of Philip Bourke Marston
which you lent me, and which I thought I had
lost.
Do you remember the day you lent it to me ?
At the sight of the dull green cover, my brain
went beating back to that morning on the beach,
the morning I first guessed you cared. You wore
a white frock, and a hat with a big green bow that
matched your clear grey eyes. I remember as I
walked along to meet you, my eyes were caught
by the green on your hat and the green book in
your hand. So shyly you told me you wanted to
lend it to me, because I knew nothing of the blind
poet, and because you were proud of knowing well
a book of which I knew nothing.
“Read me something,” I begged, as we sat on
the sand ; but you shook your head with the little
shake I know so well, and said the gulls were
making too much noise, and I could not hear.
You would not talk at all that day, but sat watch-
ing the waves as they broke from blue to green ;
and I sat watching you in your dainty frock and
pretty hat, thinking how true a match the bow
was to your eyes, and the old rhyme came ringing
to my mind, and I found myself chanting
“Yellow’s forsaken, green’s foresworn.”
But why do I write this ? Are you thinking
me stupid, I wonder? Little girl, in all God’s
world there is nowhere such a fool as I. Did I
not have your love ? Were you not mine, mine,
mine? And yet, though knowing that God, in
His goodness, had given me the love of an angel
woman, I doubted you, and worse—forsook you !
How does the old rhyme go now? “Green’s
forsaken, yellow’s foresworn.” Oh, dear heart,
in the days gone by, you used to say you had a
genius for forgiving. Have you no forgiveness
for your lover ? You know I did not believe the
vile things I said, even while saying them. I
know you to be faithful and pure, and yet, in my
wicked jealousy—what things I said ! I can see
you now as you stood with your white face and
blazing grey eyes, listening silently while I, foul
brute, struck you with cruel words. Little girl
—my little girl, can you forgive me?
I opened your book at these words : “ Love has
turned his face away.” Was that an omen ? No ;
I do not believe that you are gone from my life
for ever. You small, presumptuous person, you
dare not try to live without me, for you are mine,
heart and soul. God made you for me from the
beginning, and I cannot lose you now. Write
and tel} me I may come. Three months and not
a sign of you —or is it three years ? I cannot wait
for your letter, dearest; I shall be there before
you have finished reading this, and I shall
hold you in my arms, and read my answer in your
dear grey eyes.
Your lover, Phil.
WHAT HE SENT.
Dear Miss Norton, —Looking through some
papers to-day I discovered the enclosed book,
which you lent me some time ago. I must apolo-
gise for the delay in returning it, but, being small,
it was hidden beneath some manuscript. I trust
you have not needed it.
Yours sincerely, Philip S. Birch.
WHAT SHE WROTE.
Why did you send me back that book after all
this time? As the days went by and did not
bring it, I began to hope that you would keep it,
and perhaps read it, and I knew that, reading,
you must remember me. Fool that I was !
I did not expect you to keep the others, but
that little volume was different. I always meant
to give it to you, although you only took it for a
loan. I wonder if you remember the day I lent it
to you ? It was one of those perfect days of which
we counted so many, but the most perfect of all to
me, for it was that day I first knew what you were
to me. I remember it all so well. 1 woke-up in
the morning with a pleasurable feeling of “ some-
thing going to happen.” I put on my prettiest
white frock, and the hat I had trimmed with green
the night before. Green, you had said, was your
favorite color, and yet you didn’t notice it that
day. But I was too happy to be disappointed.
I wonder if you remember asking me to read
you something from the little book. _ Did you
think me sulky because I made a stupid excuse,
and would not ? I never told you before why I
refused. Shall I tell you now ? There was only
one line in that book that I could say that morn-
ing. It flashed into my heart as I saw you walking
along the beach to meet me. So strong ac. 3 proud
you looked with your broad shoulders and head
held high ; and, as you came near, suddenly my
heart cried out, “ Philip, my king ! ” and I knew
why the day was perfect.
Now, do you understand why, of all books in
the world, I would rather you had kept that little
volume of the poor blind poet ? But you have
sent it back. Why did you send it now ? Why
not before? I was beginning to believe I could
forget, but I know now there is no forgetting in
this life. I shut my eyes and say, “ I will forget,”
and then I find myself dreaming of that evening
we climbed up the cliff to see the best of the
sunset. Do you remember how we stood leaning
on the old fence, side by side, with no words
spoken, till I began to fear the silence, and for
something to say, asked you which of Marston’s
poems you liked best ? You turned then, and took
my hands in yours. I could feel you trembling,
though I was calm with the wonder of a great
approaching happiness, and you leaned to look
into my eyes, and said, so low I could, hardly hear
you—
Sometimes I say in an hour supreme,
Bride, my bride !
I shall hold you fast, and not in a dream,
O my Bride!
I closed my eyes as you began to speak, fearing
to wake and find I had been dreaming. But again
I heard your voice, “Bride, my Bride,” and I
opened my eyes, and saw you standing there. Out
at sea the eastern sky was rosy with reflected
light, and the waves were a wonderful blue; in
the west the golden sunshine blazed over the hills,
turning the lagoon to a sheet of burning copper ;
but all the glory of the sunset, east or west,
gleamed in your eyes as you stood and looked at
me. Oh, Philip, my king, whatever else the years
may dim, that picture will be with me always.
But you have sent back my little book !
Perhaps I should not write like this. I should
be dignified, and bear my sorrow in silence ; or
pretend the indifference that is demanded by the
un-caring world from all poor wounded women.
But I must speak this once. I never answered
you that day you were so cruel. I could not. My
heart was crying out, “ I love you—how can you
hurt me so? Let me prove my love for you.” But
my pride kept me silent, and you went away,
believing me untrue, unfaithful, because of my
silence. Oh, my pride, what devil sent you to my
heart, the heart which God had filled with love ?
Philip, I must tell you now. Listen to me, dear.
I love you, and have always loved you. I have
always been true to you in every, thought and
deed. You must believe me, for this is truth. Is
your love for me dead ? I fear it, and yet I take
pride in showing you my heart. Oh, my dear, my
dear, come back to me. You used to laugh at me
because I could forgive so easily. Dear love, God
gave me that power in the beginning that I might
write this letter, and thwart the devilish pride
that tried to ruin our lives.
Come to-morrow, and we will walk along the
beach to see the sunset. “ Along the beach,” did
Isay? Through Paradise it will be, for—Your
Meg.
WHAT SHE SENT.
Sir, —I duly received the volume of Marston
this morning. I had quite forgotten to whom I
had lent it.— Margaret M. Norton.
FAYRE.
The Deaf-’un,
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bill and I were lurching along the road from
Germanton to Culcairn, bluey up. The figure of
a man loomed ahead. “Swaggie coinin’,” said
Bill. “Whaler, too; I’ll bet. I know by the
swing of him.”
“Good-day,” said I, when the man came up.
He cocked his head one side and put one hand to
his ear; then, as if he had made up his mind
about something, dropped his swag. “Deaf,” he
said, in a loud voice. “Awkward, ain’t it?” he
continued. “ You’ll ’ave to sing-out a bit louder.”
“ Good-day,” I shouted.
“Which way? Wagga Wagga? Strike me!
ain’t it a perisher, too ? You going there ? ”
“Yes,” I said, seeing that he misunderstood
me.
“ What for ? Starve ? Bli-me ! if a deaf man
can’t live, how do you think you can ? Look-a-
’ere, mate, see that ’ere dog? Well, he can do
anything but talk ; an’ e’ couldn’t get me a bit o’
meat ’tween Wagga an’ ’ere. That’ll tell yer.”
“Did you call at ?” I asked.
“ Call into Mingy Station ! What for? Listen,
mate. I asked the woman about a billy of water,
an’ she says : ‘ Would you like some milk ? ’
‘Course I would, mum,’ says I. Then I arsks
’er for some chuck, an’ she gives me some flour, a
piece of meat and duff. Strike me!—l don’t
believe in this ’ere baking of johnny-cakes. Why
don’t they give a bloke bread ’stead of flour ?
There’s ther pub. now. Don’t you go to it,” he
said to me. “Too young and ’ealthy-looking,
you are. Send yer cobber —he looks just ther
t k“'oh,” I said aside to Bill, “ this fellow is a
pure bummer, and ”
“ What yer say ? ” chipped in Deafy ; “ Strike
me, I ain’t so deaf as yer thinks. Look-a-’ere—
p’raps I am a bummer. But I tell yer times is
gettin’ so hard that I has ter crack deaf and write
it on paper when I arsks for chuck; an’ sometimes
I forgets myself. That’s ’ow I ’eard what you
said. Don’t yous go along this track. What?
You’s will, will you? Well, yous won’t be the
fust what’s died on the road. Anyhow, so long ! ”
We soon forgot about the deaf chap, but when
Bill went to get supper ready at night he gave a
yell of dismay that startled the mopokes out of
the trees. The deaf man’s dog, that “could do
anything but talk,” had, while we were talking,
commandeered 21b. of steak from my billy-can
that was standing on the ground.

S.M. Herald advt. :
r\ ENTLEMAN requires OOAOH to prepare him for

per week. State terms, &c., to CREDO, Herald
