Interesting to know how many replies this inno-
cent received, and how genuine the advt. was in
the first place.
That was a quaint note found in the
handwriting of an Adelaide doctor: —
Not being addicted to the habit of taking morphia, it
seems to me a unique experience that I took with very
little discomfort 43 grains of sulphate of morphia in less
than 12 hours on Sept. 9. Only symptoms : Slight dizzi-
ness, drowsiness, and slightly bitter taste.
That was a month before he tried again, and suc-
ceeded. Probably he was immune to morphia-
poisoning (the ordinary dose is about a quarter of
a grain); or perhaps he was already unbalanced.
But how characteristic it is of the fine habit of
noting experiences and phenomena on which
almost the whole progress of medicine is based.
Brisbane Worker recently criticised John
Lane and his Cosme propaganda somewhat
severely. An indignant reply to the Worker
followed, containing the following assertion
“The movement is as great a power for good in
Paraguay as in Queensland; and not until
the proletariat are educated to the Oneness of
Labor all the world over will any material
progress be made.” The amusing part of the
matter is that the Cosme communist is himself
anything but cosmopolitan. He won’t allow the
Paraguayan (who gave him the land he lives on)
to enter his sacred colony at all. The Cosme
apostle proves the “Oneness of Labor” by re-
fusing to adopt into the sacred brotherhood the
very man who has been his best friend.
A cable :
The French Government has stopped the salary of
Cardinal Perraud, Bishop of Autun, for having in a
speech referred to the “Government’s depraving in-
fluence.”
Napoleon 1., greater as a statesmen, perhaps, than
as a soldier, adopted the expedient, to
power of French clerics, of paying them. The
clergy receiving State subventions, the congrega-
tions got out of the way, to a large extent, of
making voluntary offerings; and now, when the
church tries to create trouble, it is threatened
with a stoppage of its screw, and soon comes to
heel. State aid to religion is, in theory, wrong ;
in practice, when there is but one powerful church
in a State, it may be a good means of discipline.
“S.S.” : Struck an energetic collector for
the hospital funds on Hospital Saturday in Mel-
bourne. The collector was a lassie, one of two
(they usually hunt in couples). She was chasing
small change on the sands, and when a man who
looked something of a sport chanced along her
companion would say seductively : “ Oh, do give
something,” adding “ Mary will turn a handspring
for sixpence.” If the sixpence was dropped m
the box, Mary turned her handspring, and turned
it well. It was not at all an unseemly display,
which probably accounts for the fact that it only
earned three sixpences in the course of half-an-
hour.


RHEUMATISM
Is a form of Blood Poisoning. You will never have Rheu-
matism if your Kidneys are well. Por Healthy Kidneys
filter from the Blood all the Rheumatic and other
Poisons that cause Disease. Keep the Kidneys well,
and they will keep you well.
Rheumatism is not a
disease in itself; it is but
the symptom of a disease.
It is caused by an acid in
the blood, called uric acid,
of which there is enough
formed every twenty-four
hours to cause certain death
if not removed.
Nature has provided the
kidneys to take this acid
out of the system, and they
are the only means by which
it can be removed. As the
blood passes through the
kidneys it is filtered, and
the acids and poisons are
passed out of the system
along with the surplus
water.
But when the kidneys
get sick they do not do
this work properly, and
some of this acid stops in
the system, causing endless
pain and suffering. It will
crystallise somewhere, and
when it crystallises in the
muscles, the little, sharp,
jagged crystals cut and tear
the flesh, causing great pain.
*
**•
an
1
s>>
\
&
y
V
That is muscular rheumatism.
They may crystallise in the joints, and that is the cause of gout; or in
the kidneys or bladder, causing stone in bladder or kidney, gravel, sedi-
ment, &c.
The acid will penetrate every nerve and fibre of the body, causing
nervousness, headache, sleeplessness, and interfering with the valves of the
heart; it will affect the eyes, and will cause a general weakness and fagged
feeling, bloating of limbs, &c.
The whole health of your body therefore depends on keeping your kidneys
well. There can be no rheumatism with healthy kidneys, no nervousness, no
headaches, no bad blood. Keep the kidneys well, so they may filter all
impurities from the system, and they will keep you well.
Doan’s Backache Kidney pills
are the great medicine for this purpose, because they give just the aid the
kidneys require to properly filter the blood. They heal the kidneys’ delicate
membranes, and, best of all, they are for the kidneys alone.
They do not act on the bowels, as so many medicines do.
SYDNEY PROOF.
Mr. Jas. I otheringham, Watson’s Kay, late 6th Imperial Bushmen, writes :
“Whilst in South Africa with the 6th Imperial Bushmen, through constant ex-
posure to all sorts of weather, sleeping in wet clothes, etc., I got a very severe
attack of lumbago. I was so bad that I could not get up off the ground-let alone
mount my horse. Two military doctors had ago at me. One blistered my back
until all the skin was off; and the other gave me medicine, but neither of them
did me any good. Fortunately, a comrade came along with a box of Doan’s Back-
ciche Kidney Pills, <ind before X hud finished the box I wus &s right as ever X wrs
in my life. Since then I always keep a box of the pills handy in case of the
lumbago returning, but, so far, I am glad to say, I have had no necessity to take
them. You may make whatever use you like of this, as I consider Doan’s Back-
ache Kidney Pills a boon to humanity.”

Genius.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"The late Prince Consort has been awarded statues
in hundreds. Cromwell has, at last, atter hundreds
of years, been granted one.”
Why do ye patter of Genius with infinite ardor
and zest,
And say ye would raise her a statue and place it
on high with the best ?
Genius is calm and immortal, and ye who but live
for a day
Would render her duty and homage by raising a
carving of clay!
Lift up your statues, O people, to those who of
statues have need,
Lift them to men who were never of note in the
doing of deed ;
Did ye not raise them their marble and carve it
and place it on high,
Would not their names in a moment for lack of a
monument die ?
Genius hath scorn of your carvings, your tombs
and your tablets of brass ;
Is not her labor immortal, and do not your monu-
ments pass ?
Built by herself is her temple—right in the dawn-
ing of day :
What does she want with your marble ? Go—
burden your clods with your clay !
Have ye not nidd’rings who love such ? Have ye
not princes and clowns ?
Have ye not grovellers gilded and creatures who
creep under crowns ?
Have ye not louts who are lavish—have ye not
lords who are proud 1
Give them your bronzes and mouldings—O ye
of the mutable crowd.
Genius is native of Heaven —she sprang from the
brain of a god ;
Shall she get ultimate glory because ye have
shapen a clod ?
Shall she be proud and uplifted—she who is
radiant of soul —
Because ye have sorted your baubles and found
her a pitiful dole.
Yet there is use for your marble ; yes—load it as
lead unto lead,
Over the heads that ye cherished —over your com-
monplace dead;
See that ye place it there only—so shall the after-
world know
Never a Sage or a Hero was laid in a sepulchre so !
Q.
P.L.
Explorations in Civilisation-
BEING THE LETTERS OF AN AUSTRALIAN IN
EXILE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
November 14th. —To-day, walking to the Strand
>by way of Charing Cross, I found the fog not so
'dense as usual, but there was a freezing east wind,
and the chilblained faces of the working people
showed the winter the more. And, standing at a
hoarding, I saw the Genius of Famine—the seven
stars of Misery.
The man stood in his rags exhaling the odor of
poverty, even in that biting wind ; but my interest
was greater than my objections to bad smells, and
I went close to him. He was intently regarding
a poster, a fine pictorial advertisement of a
steamer line to the West Indies, with very blue
seas and very golden palm-trees and translucent
white beaches. And the poster exhorted the
starving man thus :
Avoid the
Inclemency of
the North by Wintering
in THE
WEST INDIES.
West Indian Navigation
Co.’s Steamers
Leave Fortnightly.
Return
Passage, 35 guineas.
He read it again, and looked at me, and into his
hunger-bright eyes, and chalk-white face, there
came a brave gleam. I grinned also, and we
stood there looking at each other for a minute,
grinning foolishly in one another’s faces.
Then I: “Two bob any good to you ?”
He : “ Gord bless yer; it’s a fortchin’.”
I: “ Here you are then.”
He : “ May you never want it yerself, sir ! ”
I: “ And good luck to you.”
He : “ Same to you—an’ many of ’em.”
I: “If you have two rums, you can fancy you’re
in the West Indies.”
He : “I’ll ’ave a shillin’s worth, mister.”
And so we parted with a big respect for each
other. It seemed to me a good man who could find
a grin in that wind and those circumstances. And
I suppose thousands of good men are trampled out
of existence every year in this great muddy
shambles.
November 15th.— London is big, but it is the
bigness of squalor mostly. A great collection of
endless Bourke streets, with Saturday night
crowds in them always —that is London. Have
been exploring more in the fog. Newgate and
the publishers’ quarter. Paternoster Row, Amen
Corner, Ave Maria Lane, and through Creed
Lane to a little stable yard grandiloquently named
Printing House Square, where the solemn and
stodgy Times produces daily its barren wastes of
ink. The Times office is built on a portion of the
Black Friars monastery, where Catherine of
Arragon was tried, and where sat also the Parlia-
ment that condemned Wolsey. Almost opposite,
and on the Surrey side of the river, is Bankside,
where stood the Globe Theatre of Shakespeare.
Beaumont and Fletcher and Massinger belong to
that quarter, too, and there also were situated the
Rose Theatre, the Paris Garden, and the Bear
Garden. I would the Bear Garden were open
now, that I might see what manner of men they
really were who were fit to carry offal to the bears.
I have always been curious in this matter.
In the Borough on the Surrey side, and near
London Bridge, is the Tabard Inn of Chaucer’s
Pilgrims. These frauds were going to Canter-
bury, which any man on a good horse would do in
a day and a half at most, but they lied better’ than
they travelled in those days.
, -thlbngsffate is another delusion. After seeing
all these things I am moved to wonder that a few
men writing of these trivialities of London were
able to so cover them with romance that they
appeared of real magnitude. Or did we who read
of . them provide the romance from our own
spinning ?
The Monument on Fish-street Hill is 202 ft.
mi?"’ that is the best that can be said of it.
Ihe reliefs on the base are ridiculous in design,
and bad in execution. Fish-street has no hill, by
the way—-only a gentle rise. We would call it,
in Australia, “ rolling country.”
Ratcliff Highway is gone, and has become St.
George s-street, but the East End Jews still have
the Petticoat Lane fairs on Sundays. The Jew is
everywhere, by the way—selling red and yellow
handkerchiefs in the East End, running his own
theatres to exclusively Jewish audiences, who
witness Jewish dramas played by Jew companies;
owning the Stock Exchange in Throgmorton-
street, and bossing the South African market, and
singing
Rule Brittanyas—Brittanyas roolse der waifs,
Bntonse nefer, nefer, nefer shall be slaifs.
while the “true born Englishman” marches off
to fight Stock Exchange battles at a shilling a
day ; entertaining society at the West End ; and
cheek by jowl—or rather nose by nose—with the
King, in the palace and at the racecourse.
Going back through the Minories to Aldgate, I
wandered on to Great Tower-street, and into
Seething Lane. Good old Sam Pepys was living
there on the night the Great Fire broke out. His
diary, states that Sam “got up at three in the
morning and observed the fire from his maid’s
window.” Ah Samuel! Samuel! why from the
maid’s window ?
The church near this place is named “All Hal-
lows Barking.” This doesn’t mean any more than
a Jew lizard, but it looks fearsome and mysterious,
and at that time the church that failed to make
the hair of its parishioners stand on end was a
failure.
November 17th. —I heard an affecting story
to-day of an author who had done excellent work
in a squalid room, and on a table made of the
boards of gin-cases. Success came to him, and he
burned his faithful gin-case table, and bought a
roll-top desk with about 100 drawers in it. The
magnificence of the desk sterilised his imagina-
tion, and he never wrote another line that was
not incoherent bosh. And people said that he
really had never written the good work; that it
had been done by his brother-in-law ; and the
story was believed; although everybody knows
that a mere brother-in-law is incapable of any-
thing. This sort of fate will never happen to me,
because I will never be able to buy a gorgeous desk,
from my literary earnings, anyway. My novel
has been read by two publishers, and one of them
has strangulated hernia, and the other an
aneurism, as the result. However, a very strong
publisher (physically) has got it now, and I
believe that if he survives the first 30 chapters
without permanent injury to his health the book
may win a binding this year.
They wouldn’t mind my being unconventional
if I were Imperialistic, but, as a publisher’s reader
said to me the other day: “To be Unconven-
tional and Conservative, all right; to be Conven-
tional and Republican, good ; but to be Uncon-
ventional and Republican—it’s too much.”
November 18th. —Went along to Regent-st. this
morning to discover the ways of the women by
their shops. Saw a disconsolate widow looking at
some perfectly lovely mourning, and breaking her
heart—with her stay busks. She was otfer-laced
quite three inches.
The street, dotted with frock-coated, tall-hatted
Englishmen, pink and white, and all evidently
of the same mother, with the unmeaning re-
semblance of waxworks.
Also saw a straight-up-and-down fat lady
breathing stertorously in front of an advertise-
ment of the “ Rejane Corset.” The poster said
“ Stout figures can easily obtain a straight figure
below the waist line.” Haste thee ! Good straight
up-and-down fat lady 1 Flee this shop of Ananias
and Sapphira, Ltd.
This afternoon, I go out to see an “ Australian ”
Colony at St. John’s Wood. The people I am to
see are real Australians, although they live at St.
John’s Wood. It is in that quarter that people
live who have been a month in Australia, having
struck Melbourne or Sydney as the Great Ameri-
can Actress or the Great Canadian Lecturer, and
on the strength of the 30 days asylum describe
themselves for ever after as “the Great Australian
Tragedienne,”or the “Australian Bret Harte.”
I pray you request the Federal Government to
appoint an “Approver of Australians in London ”
so that the self-advertiser may no longer drag our
nation in the dirt.
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
Dr. J. M. Creed, who holds the high-
sounding, though honorary, office of “ Advisory-
Chairman-of-the-Board-under-the-Inebriates-Act-
of-1900,” has persuaded N.S.W. Government to
allow him to convert Shaftesbury Reformatory,
at South Head, now used as a house of correction
for girls committed from the police courts, to a
home for the treatment of inebriates. Dr. Creed
has had a long battle on behalf of the sane medical
treatment of “alcoholics,” and is at last begin-
ning to see some benefit from his efforts.
In the dear, dead war-days the prepos-
terous Seddon managed to bluster Maoriland
Labor party into more or less surly acquiescence
in Imperialism and cohentingenting. Workers’
hearts were never in the sorry business, but ap-
prehension of “unpatriotic” odium exercised a
malign effect. Little lawyer Barclay, Labor
M.H.R. for Dunedin, stood almost alone in boldly
denouncing the war —accusing Richard the Ri-
diculous of wantonly hitching Maoriland to
Chamberlain’s chariot—what time his two Labor
colleagues were spouting Maffickism in company
with “His Lordship the Bishop” and “His Honor
the Judge. ” Local ‘ ‘ patriotic ” press foamed at
Barclay, and counselled the Labor party to drop
him. Barclay is still politically alive, however.
The elections are coming on; the once familiar
word “patriotism” is never spoken; the gilt is
finally off that particularly fraudulent ginger-
bread ; and the Labor party, free from the spell of
gory Seddonism, has decided to stick to Barclay.
His opponent is one Chisholm, who was mayor of
Dunedin during the first war year. His pet
formula in those days was “ Three hearty cheers! ”
and on the strength of a choice commandeering of
patriotic adjectives, foolish friends persuaded him
that he was a born orator. When the news of the
relief of Ladysmith was expected, Chisholm was
afraid to go to bed o’ nights, lest the wire should
come at such an hour as he knew not; and he is
said to have rushed down to the Town Hall in
breezy pyjamas, on one occasion, to set the bells
ringing for a false alarm of “Pretoria entered.
